The Objection of Created Writing and Its Answer
Ashari responds to the objection that writing can be created without the substrate becoming a writer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite objection concerning writing and Ashari's initial response distinguishing between necessary and acquired writing.

Core Idea
Writing on a tablet doesn't make it a writer—so why does speech make a speaker?

The Mu'tazilites press an analogy: God creates writing on a tablet, but the tablet is not a writer. So why must the substrate of speech become a speaker? Ashari begins his answer by splitting writing into two types—necessary and acquired—to show the analogy fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the objection from the Mu'tazilites.
The objectors use the example of a tablet that receives writing but is not called a writer.
2
Understand the analogy
The analogy is: God creates writing on a tablet, but the tablet is not a writer. Similarly, why should a body that receives God's speech be called a speaker?
This challenges the Ash'ari position that the Qur'an is God's speech subsisting in a non-speaking substrate.
3
Note Ashari's distinction
Ashari distinguishes two types of writing: necessary (directly created by God) and acquired (kasb). He claims that in necessary creation, the substrate becomes a writer by necessity.
The text cuts off before completing the argument for acquired writing.
4
Reflect on the incomplete answer
Consider how the distinction might undermine the objection. If necessary writing makes the substrate a writer, then the analogy fails because the tablet does not have writing created by necessity.
The full argument is not given, but the direction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : أفليس قد يحدث الله -تعالى- كتابةً في غيره ولا يكون الشيء الذي قامت به الكتابه كاتبا ؟ قيل لهم : إن أحدثَ اللَّهُ -تعالى- في غيرِهِ كتابةٌ ضَرُورَةٌ، كَانَ ذلكَ الغيرُ كاتبا باضطرار، وكذلك إن كانتِ الكتابة كسبا كانَ
If they say: 'Does not God - exalted - create writing in another, and yet the thing in which the writing subsists is not a writer?' It is said to them: 'If God - exalted - creates writing in another by necessity, then that other is a writer by necessity, and likewise if the writing is acquired, it would be...'
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents another objection from the Mu'tazilites and the beginning of Ashari's reply. The objectors point out that God creates writing on a tablet or parchment, but we do not call the tablet a 'writer' (it just has writing on it). So why can't God create speech in a body without that body becoming a speaker? Ashari begins his answer by distinguishing two types of writing: (1) writing created necessarily (directly by God), in which case the substrate does become a 'writer' by necessity (i.e., it is properly called a writer in that context); and (2) writing that is acquired (kasb) by a human agent. The text cuts off here, but the argument is likely that the analogy fails because speech is an essential attribute, whereas writing is often accidental, or that in the case of divine action, the substrate does take the name.

Key Terms
كتابة
ضرورة
كسب
كاتب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The writing analogy proves that accidents can subsist without the substrate being the agent.
Correction: Ashari argues that in necessary creation, the substrate does become the agent (writer) by necessity, so the analogy does not hold.

Checkpoint
What distinction does Ashari begin to make in response to the objection about writing?
A) Between writing and speech.
B) Between writing by necessity and writing by acquisition (kasb).
C) Between God's writing and human writing.
D) Between the writer and the written.

Key Takeaway
Ashari counters the writing analogy by distinguishing necessary writing (where the substrate becomes a writer) from acquired writing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a difficult truth about God by creating a clever analogy that made it seem absurd? Will you repent from using your intellect to avoid submission, starting with this lesson's teaching that God's actions are unique and incomparable?
?
When you hear an argument that challenges your faith, do you first seek to understand it honestly or do you immediately look for a counter? What would it cost you to truly listen to the Mu'tazilite objection before seeking its answer?
?
The tablet does not write, yet if God wills, it becomes a writer. What in your life feels inert or incapable of bearing divine truth? Will you ask God tonight to make you a vessel for His speech, even if it means becoming something you never expected?

The Analogy of Attributes: God's Speech Cannot Inhere in Creation
Ash'ari presents an argument by analogy to refute the creation of God's speech in a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari argues that if God's speech were created in a substrate, that substrate would have to be described as speaking, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If speech inheres in creation, the creation becomes a speaker—and that is impossible.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari presents a powerful analogy to refute the claim that God's speech is created in a substrate. He shows that just as the presence of strength makes a body strong, and knowledge makes it knowing, the presence of speech would make the substrate a speaker. Since a created thing cannot be described as speaking with the eternal speech of Allah, the claim fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly. Notice how the argument proceeds from a general principle to a specific conclusion.
The Arabic has some OCR issues; the meaning is clear from the translation.
2
Identify the analogies
Find where al-Ash'ari lists attributes like strength, knowledge, life, etc. that God creates in a substrate, and how the substrate is then described.
These analogies set up the expectation for how speech would work.
3
Understand the conclusion
Trace why al-Ash'ari says the analogy fails for speech: because the substrate would be called 'speaking', which is impossible for a created thing.
This conclusion refutes the opponent's claim.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Reflect on how this argument supports the eternity of the Quran, as God's speech cannot be created in a temporal substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا : فإنَّ اللَّهَ -تعالى- إذا أحدث في غيره تفضّلا ونعمةً وفعلا وإحسانا ورِزْقًا، كانَ ذلك الجسم موصوفًا بأخص أوصاف الفعل والتفضلِ والرِّزْقِ والنّعمَةِ والإحسانِ؛ لأنَّهُ إنْ كانَ التفضُلُ والنعمة والإحسان والفعلُ قُوةٌ لا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ ذلك الجسم قويا . وكذلك إن كانَ عِلمًا أو حياة أو إرادة أو سَمْعًا أو بَصَرًا ، فيجـ إذا أحدَثَ كلامًا في غيرِهِ أن يُشْتَقَ [لِذلِكَ] الغيرِ من أوصاف الكلامِ [اسْمٌ] . أخَصٌ فلما لم يُجز ذلك ، بطَلَ أنْ يكون الكلامُ مَقِيسًا عَلَى مَا قلتُم مِنْ أن الله - تعالى - يُحدِثُ كلامَهُ في غيره، كما يُحدِثُ فعله وتفضّلَهُ ونِعَمَهُ وإحسانه في غيره . فإن قالوا : أفليس قد يحدث الله -تعالى- كتابةً في غيره ولا يكون الشيء الذي قامت به الكتابه كاتبا ؟ قيل لهم : إن أحدثَ اللَّهُ -تعالى- في غيرِهِ كتابةٌ ضَرُورَةٌ، كَانَ ذلكَ الغيرُ كاتبا باضطرار، وكذلك إن كانتِ الكتابة كسبا كانَ ذلك الغير كاتبا باكتساب، فيجب إذا أحدثَ اللَّهُ - تعالى - كلامَهُ في غيره، أنْ يكونَ ذلكَ الغيرُ مُتكلّما بكلام الله
And also: Verily Allah—exalted is He—when He creates in another favor, grace, act, beneficence, and provision, that body is described with the most specific descriptions of the act, favor, provision, grace, and beneficence; because if the favor, grace, beneficence, and act are strength, then necessarily that body is strong. Similarly, if it is knowledge, life, will, hearing, or sight, then when He creates speech in another, that other should derive a name from the descriptions of speech. [Most specific.] So when that is not permissible, it becomes invalid that speech be analogized to what you said—that Allah—exalted is He—creates His speech in another, just as He creates His act, favor, graces, and beneficence in another. If they say: 'Does not Allah—exalted is He—create writing in another, and the thing in which writing inheres is not a writer?' It is said to them: If Allah—exalted is He—creates writing in another by necessity, then that other is a writer by necessity; and similarly, if the writing is acquired, then that other is a writer by acquisition. So it is necessary, if Allah—exalted is He—creates His speech in another, that that other be one who speaks with the speech of Allah.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that Allah's speech is created in a substrate. The argument proceeds by analogy: When Allah creates an attribute like favor, strength, knowledge, etc. in a body, that body is described with its most specific descriptor (e.g., strong, knowing). Therefore, if Allah created speech in another, that other would have to be described as 'speaking' with that speech. But this is impossible because it would mean a created thing speaks with the eternal speech of Allah. The opponents object by citing the case of writing: Allah creates writing in a thing without that thing becoming a writer. Al-Ash'ari answers that if the writing is necessitated, the thing is a writer by necessity; if acquired, by acquisition. Similarly, if speech were created, the substrate would be a speaker. Since it is not permissible for a created thing to be described as speaking with Allah's speech, the analogy fails, and Allah's speech must be eternal, not created in a substrate.

Key Terms
كلام
أحدث
تفضل
نعمة
إحسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's speech being created in a substrate is analogous to His creating actions in a substrate, but the author shows the analogy fails because actions do not imply the substrate is an agent, whereas speech would imply the substrate is a speaker.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that if speech inhered in a created thing, that thing would be described as speaking, which is impossible for a created thing with respect to Allah's eternal speech.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ash'ari argue would happen if God created speech in a substrate?
A) The substrate would be described as speaking.
B) The substrate would become strong.
C) The substrate would become knowledgeable.
D) The substrate would remain unchanged.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari demonstrates that if speech inhered in a created thing, that thing would be a speaker, which is impossible, thus speech must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's speech is eternal and uncreated, how does that change the way you approach the Quran each day? Does it make you revere it more? What would it mean for you if you treated the Quran as a created thing? Are you guilty of treating the Quran as just another book? Commit to one change in how you read the Quran starting today.
?
The argument shows that when Allah creates an attribute in a creature, the creature is described by that attribute. But you are a creature—what attributes has Allah created in you? Are you allowing those attributes to define you in a way that honors Allah? Or are you using them to claim independence? Examine yourself: in the past week, have you acted as if your strength, knowledge, or provision came from yourself? Write down one moment of ingratitude and repent.
?
Ash'ari refutes the idea that speech can inhere in creation because it would make the creation a speaker. But you speak every day—when you speak truth, are you aware that your speech is created by Allah? How should that awareness change the words you choose? Today, before you speak, pause and remember that your ability to speak is a gift from the Creator. What one thing will you say differently because of this lesson?

The Writing Analogy: Answering the Objection
Ash'ari answers an objection using the case of writing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari refutes the objection that God can create writing in a substrate without the substrate becoming a writer, thus undermining the analogy.

Core Idea
Writing does not create a writer without acquisition, but speech would necessitate a speaker.

In this continuation, al-Ash'ari addresses an objection: opponents argue that God creates writing in a thing without that thing becoming a writer, so why can't He create speech in a thing without it becoming a speaker? Al-Ash'ari responds by distinguishing between necessitated and acquired writing, and shows that the analogy actually supports his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ash'ari's response
Read the passage from 'فإن قالوا' to the end. Notice the structure: an objection is raised, then answered.
2
Understand the two cases of writing
Al-Ash'ari divides writing into two types: by necessity (directly from Allah) and by acquisition (kasb). In both cases, the substrate is called a writer.
This shows that writing does not escape the rule.
3
Apply the same logic to speech
If writing makes the substrate a writer, then speech would make the substrate a speaker. Thus the objection backfires.
4
Conclude the refutation
Understand that Ash'ari's response strengthens his original argument: the analogy from writing actually supports his position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا : فإنَّ اللَّهَ -تعالى- إذا أحدث في غيره تفضّلا ونعمةً وفعلا وإحسانا ورِزْقًا، كانَ ذلك الجسم موصوفًا بأخص أوصاف الفعل والتفضلِ والرِّزْقِ والنّعمَةِ والإحسانِ؛ لأنَّهُ إنْ كانَ التفضُلُ والنعمة والإحسان والفعلُ قُوةٌ لا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ ذلك الجسم قويا . وكذلك إن كانَ عِلمًا أو حياة أو إرادة أو سَمْعًا أو بَصَرًا ، فيجـ إذا أحدَثَ كلامًا في غيرِهِ أن يُشْتَقَ [لِذلِكَ] الغيرِ من أوصاف الكلامِ [اسْمٌ] . أخَصٌ فلما لم يُجز ذلك ، بطَلَ أنْ يكون الكلامُ مَقِيسًا عَلَى مَا قلتُم مِنْ أن الله - تعالى - يُحدِثُ كلامَهُ في غيره، كما يُحدِثُ فعله وتفضّلَهُ ونِعَمَهُ وإحسانه في غيره . فإن قالوا : أفليس قد يحدث الله -تعالى- كتابةً في غيره ولا يكون الشيء الذي قامت به الكتابه كاتبا ؟ قيل لهم : إن أحدثَ اللَّهُ -تعالى- في غيرِهِ كتابةٌ ضَرُورَةٌ، كَانَ ذلكَ الغيرُ كاتبا باضطرار، وكذلك إن كانتِ الكتابة كسبا كانَ ذلك الغير كاتبا باكتساب، فيجب إذا أحدثَ اللَّهُ - تعالى - كلامَهُ في غيره، أنْ يكونَ ذلكَ الغيرُ مُتكلّما بكلام الله
And also: Verily Allah—exalted is He—when He creates in another favor, grace, act, beneficence, and provision, that body is described with the most specific descriptions of the act, favor, provision, grace, and beneficence; because if the favor, grace, beneficence, and act are strength, then necessarily that body is strong. Similarly, if it is knowledge, life, will, hearing, or sight, then when He creates speech in another, that other should derive a name from the descriptions of speech. [Most specific.] So when that is not permissible, it becomes invalid that speech be analogized to what you said—that Allah—exalted is He—creates His speech in another, just as He creates His act, favor, graces, and beneficence in another. If they say: 'Does not Allah—exalted is He—create writing in another, and the thing in which writing inheres is not a writer?' It is said to them: If Allah—exalted is He—creates writing in another by necessity, then that other is a writer by necessity; and similarly, if the writing is acquired, then that other is a writer by acquisition. So it is necessary, if Allah—exalted is He—creates His speech in another, that that other be one who speaks with the speech of Allah.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that Allah's speech is created in a substrate. The argument proceeds by analogy: When Allah creates an attribute like favor, strength, knowledge, etc. in a body, that body is described with its most specific descriptor (e.g., strong, knowing). Therefore, if Allah created speech in another, that other would have to be described as 'speaking' with that speech. But this is impossible because it would mean a created thing speaks with the eternal speech of Allah. The opponents object by citing the case of writing: Allah creates writing in a thing without that thing becoming a writer. Al-Ash'ari answers that if the writing is necessitated, the thing is a writer by necessity; if acquired, by acquisition. Similarly, if speech were created, the substrate would be a speaker. Since it is not permissible for a created thing to be described as speaking with Allah's speech, the analogy fails, and Allah's speech must be eternal, not created in a substrate.

Misconception: Some might think that the case of writing proves speech can be created without the substrate becoming a speaker.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that writing actually implies a writer, so the analogy supports his position that speech implies a speaker.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what happens if Allah creates writing in a substrate by necessity?
A) The substrate is not a writer.
B) The substrate is a writer by necessity.
C) The substrate becomes a speaker.

Key Takeaway
Even with writing, the substrate is described as a writer, so the objection fails and the argument stands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you genuinely feel that you are speaking Allah's speech, or do you merely feel like a passive conduit? How would your reverence change if you truly internalized that you are a 'speaker' by acquisition, accountable for every word?
?
If a created object like a tablet can be called a 'writer' when Allah creates writing in it, what does that imply about your own actions? Do you ever forget that all your deeds are created by Allah yet attributed to you, and that you will be judged for them?
?
The objection in this lesson tries to separate an attribute from its subject. In your daily life, do you ever separate your actions from your own responsibility, blaming circumstances or even God? How does Al-Ash'ari's response call you to own your actions while acknowledging Allah's creation?

Proof for the Pre-Eternity of Divine Will
Ash'ari proves that God's will is eternal via impossibility of its creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari argued that God's will must be eternal by eliminating all possibilities of its temporal creation.

Core Idea
God's will cannot be created because no possible substrate for its creation is coherent.

This passage presents a parallel argument to the one for divine speech. Ash'ari shows that if will were temporal, it would have to exist in some way—as a self-subsisting attribute, created in God's essence, or created in another. Each possibility is impossible. By process of elimination, will must be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument sink in before analyzing.
Notice the parallel structure with the proof for speech.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage presents three options for how a created will could exist. Find each one in the text.
They are: in His essence, in another, or self-subsisting.
3
Understand why each is impossible
For each possibility, Ash'ari gives a reason: (1) God's essence is not a locus for events, (2) an attribute cannot subsist by itself, (3) creating in another would make that other willing by God's will. Grasp each reasoning.
The proof depends on the impossibility of these three.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since all possibilities are exhausted and impossible, the only remaining conclusion is that will is eternal. Trace the logic from impossibility to necessity.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the parallel with speech
The passage opens by stating that this proof is the same as for speech. Compare the reasoning for both attributes to see the consistent method.
This shows a systematic approach in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدم الإرادة وهذا الدليل على قدم الكلام، هو الدليل على قدم الإرادةِ للَّهِ - تعالى ؛ لأنَّها لو كانتْ مُحدَثة لكانت لا تخلُو مِنْ : أن يكونَ يُحدِثها في نفْسِهِ، أو في غيره، أو قائمة بنفسها . فيستحيل أن يُحدثها في نفسه؛ لأنه ليسَ بِمحل للحوادث. ويستحيلُ أن يُحدثَها قائمةً بنفسها ؛ لأنها صفةٌ ، والصِّفةُ لا تقومُ بنفسها، كما لا يجوز أن يُحدِثَ علمًا وقدرة قائمين بأنفسهما . ويستحيل أن يُحدِثَها في غير ؛ لأنَّ هذا يُوجِبُ أنْ يكونَ ذلك الغير مريدًا بإرادةِ اللَّهِ -تعالى-. فلما استحالت هذه الوجوه التي لا تخلو الإرادة منها ، لو كانت محدثة، صَحَّ أنَّها قديمةٌ، وأنَّ اللَّهَ لم يَزِلْ مُرِيدًا بِها .
The pre-eternity of will. And this proof for the pre-eternity of speech is the proof for the pre-eternity of will for Allah—exalted is He—because if it were created, it would not be free from: He creates it in Himself, or in another, or it subsists by itself. It is impossible for Him to create it in Himself, because He is not a locus for events. And it is impossible for Him to create it subsisting by itself, because it is an attribute, and an attribute does not subsist by itself, just as it is not permissible for Him to create knowledge and power subsisting by themselves. And it is impossible for Him to create it in another, because this would necessitate that other be willing by the will of Allah—exalted is He. So when these possibilities, from which will would not be free if it were created, are impossible, it is established that it is eternal, and that Allah has never ceased to be willing with it.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the pre-eternity (قدم) of the divine attribute of will (إرادة). The proof is parallel to the earlier proof for speech. The author presents a trilemma: if God's will were created (محدثة), it must be either (1) created in His essence, (2) created in another, or (3) created as self-subsisting. Each is impossible. First, God's essence cannot be a locus for temporal events (محل للحوادث). Second, an attribute cannot subsist by itself; it must inhere in a subject—knowledge and power cannot exist independently. Third, if it were created in another, that other would be described as willing by God's will, which is absurd (a creature cannot be said to will with God's eternal will). Since all options are impossible, the will must be eternal, meaning God has always been willing with it.

Key Terms
إرادة
قدم
محدثة
صفة
قائمة بنفسها
محل للحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's will could be created in time, like human will.
Correction: Temporal creation requires a substrate for the attribute to inhere in; but God's essence cannot be a locus of events, an attribute cannot be self-subsisting, and creating in another would make that creature a co-willer. All are impossible, so will must be eternal.

Checkpoint
What are the three impossible scenarios if God's will were created, according to al-Ash'ari?
A) In God's essence, in another, or self-subsisting.
B) In God's essence, in heaven, or on earth.
C) By God, by angels, or by prophets.
D) As speech, as power, or as knowledge.

Key Takeaway
God's will is eternal because all conceivable modes of its temporal creation are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that God's will cannot change because it is eternal. Consider a recent moment when you wished God's plan would change to suit your desires. How can you learn to entrust your specific, time-bound hopes to a will that does not change?
?
If God's will is eternal and never created, then every event that happens was never a 'new decision' for God. Does this change how you think about your daily prayers? Do you pray expecting God to 'decide' something new, or to actualize what He has eternally willed?
?
The proof eliminates the possibility that God's will could be created in another being. This means no creature can ever be a genuine agent of divine will. How does this affect your view of your own ability to do good? Are you truly acting with His will, or only acting within it?

Proof that the Divine Will is Eternal
Al-Ash'ari argues for the eternity of God's will using exhaustive division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses a reductio ad absurdum argument to prove that God's will is eternal, not created.

Core Idea
God's will is eternal because creation requires a locus, which God cannot be.

Al-Ash'ari now applies the same logical method he used for speech to prove that God's will is also eternal. He considers all possible ways a created will could exist and demonstrates each is impossible, thus establishing its eternity. This passage illustrates the kalam method of exhaustive division (taqsīm) and elimination.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parallel with the earlier proof for the eternity of speech.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage presents three possible ways a created will could exist. Identify each one and see how the author formulates them.
These are: in God's essence, as an independent substance, or in another being.
3
Understand the refutation of each
For each possibility, Al-Ash'ari gives a reason why it is impossible. Notice the principles behind each refutation: God is not a locus for temporal events; attributes do not subsist by themselves; a will in another would make that other a willer by God's will.
These principles are foundational in Kalam.
4
Conclude the argument
Since all three possibilities are impossible, the will cannot be created. Therefore it must be eternal, and God has always been willing by it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدم الإرادة وهذا الدليل على قدم الكلام، هو الدليل على قدم الإرادةِ للَّهِ - تعالى ؛ لأنَّها لو كانتْ مُحدَثة لكانت لا تخلُو مِنْ : أن يكونَ يُحدِثها في نفْسِهِ، أو في غيره، أو قائمة بنفسها . فيستحيل أن يُحدثها في نفسه؛ لأنه ليسَ بِمحل للحوادث. ويستحيلُ أن يُحدثَها قائمةً بنفسها ؛ لأنها صفةٌ ، والصِّفةُ لا تقومُ بنفسها، كما لا يجوز أن يُحدِثَ علمًا وقدرة قائمين بأنفسهما . ويستحيل أن يُحدِثَها في غير ؛ لأنَّ هذا يُوجِبُ أنْ يكونَ ذلك الغير مريدًا بإرادةِ اللَّهِ -تعالى-. فلما استحالت هذه الوجوه التي لا تخلو الإرادة منها ، لو كانت محدثة، صَحَّ أنَّها قديمةٌ، وأنَّ اللَّهَ لم يَزَلْ مُرِيدًا بِها .
The proof for the eternity of the will: This proof for the eternity of speech is also the proof for the eternity of the will of Allah, Exalted is He. Because if the will were created, it would not be free from being: either He creates it in His own essence, or in another, or it is subsistent by itself. It is impossible that He creates it in His own essence because He is not a locus for temporal events. And it is impossible that He creates it as subsistent by itself because it is an attribute, and the attribute does not subsist by itself, just as it is not permissible that He should create knowledge and power as subsistent by themselves. And it is impossible that He creates it in another because this requires that that other be a willer by the will of Allah, Exalted is He. So when these possibilities, from which the will would not be free if it were created, are impossible, it is established that it is eternal and that Allah has never ceased to be a willer by it.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dalil) for the eternity (qidam) of the divine will (al-irada). Imam al-Ash'ari uses the same method he used earlier for the divine speech (kalam). The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: If the will were created (muhdatha), then it would have to exist in one of three ways: either in God's own essence, or in another being, or as an independent substance. Each possibility is shown to be impossible. First, it cannot be in God's essence because God is not a locus (mahall) for temporal events (hawadith). Second, it cannot be an independent substance because it is an attribute (sifa) and attributes do not subsist by themselves. Third, it cannot be in another being because that being would then be described as willing by God's will, which is absurd. Since all three possibilities are eliminated, the will must be eternal (qadima) and God has always been willing (murid) by it.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
قدم (qidam)
محدث (muhdath)
صفة (sifa)
محل للحوادث (mahall lil-hawadith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's attributes like will are created in time.
Correction: This proof shows they are eternal because creation requires a subject, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What is the third impossibility that al-Ash'ari mentions regarding a created will?
A) It cannot be created in God's essence.
B) It cannot be created as an independent substance.
C) It cannot be created in another being.
D) It cannot be created at all.

Key Takeaway
God's will is eternal because none of the possible ways for it to be created are tenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's will is eternal and never changes, does your heart find rest in knowing that His decree for you was settled before time began, or do you sometimes wish His will could change to suit your desires? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in His wisdom?
?
If God's will cannot be created in another because that would make that being a 'willer by God's will', how does this affect your understanding of human free will? Do you ever act as if your choices are independent of God's eternal will, and how does this passage call you to align your will with His?
?
The proof shows that God's attributes are not like created things—they are eternal and inherent to His essence. Are there areas in your life where you treat God's commands or attributes as if they were temporary or negotiable? What would change if you fully internalized that His will is as eternal and unchanging as He is?

God's Will Encompasses All Created Things
Al-Ash'ari argues that God's will is universal, using analogy to knowledge and creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twofold argument by which al-Ash'ari establishes that God's will is universal, encompassing everything that can be willed, and how this follows from it being an attribute of the essence.

Core Idea
God's will, like His knowledge, must cover everything possible because it is essential to Him.

This lesson opens a new chapter on the divine will. Al-Ash'ari anticipates a common objection: why believe God wills everything? He responds with two tightly linked arguments—one from the nature of essential attributes, and another from the act of creation, capped with a Qur'anic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter opening and objection
Notice how al-Ash'ari sets up the discussion with a question: 'Why do you say that Allah wills everything that can be willed?' This frames the entire argument.
The objection is a standard dialectical device in kalam.
2
Understand the first proof from essential attributes
The will is an attribute of the essence (sifat al-dhat), just like knowledge. As knowledge must encompass all knowable things, so will must encompass all willable things.
This is an argument by analogy, using a previously established principle.
3
Examine the second proof from creation
God is the creator of every temporal thing. It is impossible that He creates something without willing it. Therefore, His will covers all created things.
This proof connects will to creative action.
4
Reflect on the Qur'anic support
The verse 'Doer of what He wills' (Hud 11:107) directly confirms that God does whatever He wills, implying His will is universal.
The verse serves as scriptural evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائر المُحدَثاتِ. فإن قال قائل : لم قلتُم : إِنَّ اللَّهَ - تعالى - مُرِيدٌ لكلِّ شيءٍ يَجوزُ أن يُراد؟ قيل له : قُلنا ذلك؛ لأن الإرادة إذا كانت من صفات الذات، بالدلالة التي ذكرناها، وجب أن تكون عامة، في كلِّ ما يجوز أن يُراد على حقيقته، كما إذا كان العلم من صفات الذات وجب عمومه بكلِّ ما يجوز أن يُعلم على حقيقته. وأيضًا فقد دلت الدلالة على أن الله - تعالى - خالق كل شيء حادث، ولا يجوز أن يخلق ما لا يريده، وقد قال الله -تعالى-: {فَعَّالٌ لِمَا يُرِيدُ} [هود: 107].
Chapter: Discussion on the Will and that it encompasses all created things. If a questioner says: Why do you say that Allah, Exalted is He, is a willer of everything that can be willed? It is said to him: We said that because when the will is from the attributes of the essence, by the evidence we have mentioned, it is necessary that it be general, concerning everything that can be willed in reality, just as when knowledge is from the attributes of the essence, its generality is necessary concerning everything that can be known in reality. And also the evidence has indicated that Allah, Exalted is He, is the creator of every created thing, and it is not permissible that He creates what He does not will, and Allah, Exalted is He, has said: 'Doer of what He wills' (Hud 11:107).
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter (bab) on the divine will (al-irada), specifically that it encompasses (ta'ummu) all created things (al-muhdathat). The chapter opens with an objection (fa'in qala qa'il) and its answer. The objector asks: Why do you claim that God is willing (murid) of everything that can possibly be willed? The answer is twofold. First, since the will is an attribute of the essence (sifat al-dhat), as proven earlier (presumably in the previous argument for its eternity), it must be universal in scope, just like divine knowledge ('ilm) which is an attribute of the essence and applies to everything knowable. Second, there is a proof from creation: God is the creator (khaliq) of every temporal thing (hadith), and it is impossible that He creates something He does not will. This is supported by the Qur'anic verse 'Doer of what He wills' (Fa''alun lima yurid). Thus, the will must encompass all things that occur.

Key Terms
إرادة
صفات الذات
عموم
علم
خالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God does not will evil or certain temporal events, or that His will is limited to good only.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari clarifies that God's will is universal: He wills everything that occurs, including what appears evil, but this is based on His wisdom. The passage asserts that God is the creator of everything temporal and must will it.

Checkpoint
What analogy does al-Ash'ari use to argue for the universality of God's will?
A) The universality of God's power
B) The universality of God's knowledge
C) The universality of God's mercy
D) The universality of God's life

Key Takeaway
God's will, as an essential attribute, necessarily encompasses all things that can be willed, just as His knowledge encompasses all knowable things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you point to one event in your life that you have been unwilling to accept as part of God's will? How does Al-Ash'ari's argument that God's will encompasses all created things—including that event—challenge your resistance? What would change if you truly believed that God willed everything that happens, even the painful parts?
?
The passage says that God is 'Doer of what He wills.' Think of a habit or sin you struggle with. If God wills everything that occurs, does that mean He wills your struggle? How does the fact that His will is based on infinite wisdom—not blind fate—allow you to trust Him while still striving to change? What step can you take today to surrender that struggle to His will?
?
Consider the analogy between God's knowledge and His will. You likely trust that God knows everything about you. But do you trust that He wills everything about you—your strengths, weaknesses, circumstances? What would it look like to live today with the conviction that God's will for you is as comprehensive as His knowledge? The Generality of God's Will Over All Created Things
Al-Ash'ari argues for the generality of God's will and answers an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's three arguments that God's will encompasses all created things and how he defends this against an objection.

Core Idea
God's essential will must be universal, otherwise imperfection would follow.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari addresses a foundational question: does God's will extend to everything that happens, or are some things outside His will? The questioner challenges the claim, and al-Ash'ari responds with a series of proofs drawn from the nature of essential attributes, scriptural testimony, and rational necessity. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the full translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the dialectical structure: question, three proofs, then a further objection and answer.
2
Identify the three proofs
Find the three distinct arguments al-Ash'ari gives: (1) parallel with knowledge as an essential attribute, (2) scriptural proof from the Quran, (3) dominion argument.
Each proof builds on the idea that an essential attribute cannot be limited.
3
Understand the objection and reply
Read the objection: why does the failure of a will to occur imply weakness? Then study the analogy with knowledge: if a known event does not occur, ignorance is implied, so similarly if a willed event does not occur, weakness is implied.
This strengthens the initial claim by showing the logical consequence of denying it.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this doctrine matters: it affirms God's absolute sovereignty and perfection, and refutes any notion that something could happen against His will.
This is a cornerstone of Ash'ari theology regarding divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ
فإن قال قائل : لم قلتُم : إِنَّ اللَّهَ - تعالى - مُرِيدٌ لكلِّ شيءٍ يَجوزُ أن يُراد؟ قيل له : قُلنا ذلكَ لأن الإرادة إذا كانت من صفات الذات، بالدلالة التي ذكرناها، وجبَ أنْ تكونَ عامةً، في كلِّ ما يَجوزُ أن يُرادَ على حقيقتِه، كما إذا كان العلم من صفات الذات وجبَ عُمومه بكلِّ ما يجوزُ أنْ يُعلَمَ علَى حَقيقَتِهِ .
وأيضًا فقد دَلَّتِ الدلالة على أنَّ الله - تعالى - خالقُ كلِّ شيءٍ حادث، ولا يجوز أنْ يَخْلُقَ مَا لَا يُرِيدُهُ، وقد قال الله -تعالى- : ﴿فَعَّالٌ لِمَا يُرِيدُ﴾ [هود : ١٠٧].
وأيضًا فإنه لا يجوز أن يكونَ في سُلطانِ اللَّهِ -تعالى- ما لا يُريدُه ؛ لأنَّه لو كانَ في سُلطانِ اللَّهِ - تعالى - ما لا يُريدُه ، لوجب أَحَدُ أَمرين : إمَّا إثباتُ سَهْوِ وغَفْلَةٍ، أو إثباتُ ضَعْفٍ، وعَجْزِ، ووَهَنِ، وتقصيرٍ عَنْ بُلوغ ما يُريدُهُ. فلمَّا لم يَجُز ذلك على اللَّهِ -تعالى- استحال أن يكون في سلطانه ما لا يريده .
فإِنْ قالَ : ولِمَ زعمتُمْ ذلكَ؟ قيل له : زَعَمْنَا ذلكَ ؛ لأنَّ المرادَ إِذا وَقَعَ ، لم يلحَقِّ مَنْ وقَعَ مُرَادُه ضعف، ولا تقصير عن بلوغ ما يُريدُه ، بوَقْعِ المرادِ، فَوَاجِبٌ أن يلحَقَهُ الضعفُ والتَّقصيرُ عن بلوغ ما يريده إذا لمْ يقَعْ مُراده . ألا ترى أنَّ مَنْ وقَعَ ما يعلَمُهُ لمْ يلْحَقْه جهل، ولا ضِدُّ مِن أضدادِ العلم بذلك الشيء، إذا وقع وهو يعلمه فدل ذلك على أنه إذا لَمْ يقع ، وهو لا يعلمه، وجبَ جهلُهُ أو وَصْفُه بضدّ مِنْ أَضدادِ العِلْمِ . وكذلك إذا كانَ ما يريده لم يجِبْ سَهْرُهُ ولا ضعفه ولا وَهَنُه ، ولا تقصيرُهُ عن بلوغ ما يريده. وإذا كانَ ما لا يُرِيدُهُ وجبَ سَهوهُ، أو ضعفُهُ وَوَهَنُه ، وتقصيرُهُ عن بلوغ ما يُرِيدُ .
Chapter on the discourse about the will and that it encompasses all created things. If a questioner says: Why do you say that God - exalted - is willing for everything that it is possible to be willed? It is said to him: We said that because the will, when it is an attribute of the self, by the proof we mentioned, it necessarily follows that it be general, in everything that it is possible to be willed in its true sense, just as when knowledge is an attribute of the self, its generality necessarily follows with respect to everything that it is possible to be known in its true sense. Also, the proof has indicated that God - exalted - is the creator of every originated thing, and it is not permissible that He create what He does not will, and God - exalted - said: 'Doer of whatever He wills' [Hud 11:107]. Also, it is not permissible that there be in the dominion of God - exalted - what He does not will, because if there were in the dominion of God - exalted - what He does not will, one of two things would be necessary: either the affirmation of forgetfulness and heedlessness, or the affirmation of weakness, incapacity, feebleness, and failure to attain what He wills. Since that is not permissible for God - exalted -, it is impossible that there be in His dominion what He does not will. If he says: And why do you claim that? It is said to him: We claimed that because when the object of will occurs, the one whose will occurs is not affected by weakness nor failure to attain what He wills, by the occurrence of the object of will. So it is necessary that weakness and failure to attain what He wills affect Him when His object of will does not occur. Do you not see that when what one knows occurs, he is not affected by ignorance nor by any opposite of knowledge concerning that thing, since it occurred while he knows it. That indicates that if it does not occur while he does not know it, his ignorance or his description with an opposite of knowledge becomes necessary. Likewise, when what He wills does not occur, His alertness, weakness, feebleness, and failure to attain what He wills are not necessary. And when what He does not will occurs, His forgetfulness, or weakness and feebleness, and failure to attain what He wills become necessary.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a theological proof establishing that God's will (al-irada) is general, encompassing all created things. The author, al-Ash'ari, begins with a hypothetical objection: 'Why do you say that God is willing for everything that can be willed?' The answer proceeds with three rational arguments.

First argument (a): The will is an attribute of God's essence (sifatu al-dhat). Just as divine knowledge must be universal, applying to everything knowable as it truly is, so too the divine will must be universal, applying to everything willable as it truly is. The parallel with knowledge is crucial: both are essential attributes, and essential attributes cannot be limited.

Second argument (b): Scriptural proof is combined with rational necessity. God is the creator of all originated things. It is impossible that He creates what He does not will. The Quranic verse 'Doer of whatever He wills' (Hud 11:107) confirms that His will is effective in all His acts.

Third argument (c): Suppose something existed in God's dominion that He does not will. This would force one of two unacceptable conclusions: either God is subject to forgetfulness and heedlessness (which is unworthy of Him), or He is weak, incapable, and unable to achieve His will. Both are impossible for God. Therefore, nothing occurs in His dominion that He does not will.

The opponent then raises an objection: 'Why do you claim that? That is, why does the existence of an unwilled thing imply weakness?' The answer draws an analogy with knowledge. If a person's intended object occurs, that person suffers no weakness or failure. Conversely, if the intended object does not occur, then weakness or failure must be present. Similarly, if someone knows something and it occurs, no ignorance is attributed; but if it does not occur while he is ignorant, ignorance is attributed. By extension, if God's will is not realized, that would imply imperfection, which is impossible. Therefore, whatever occurs must be willed by God; otherwise, imperfection would follow. The analogy with knowledge reinforces the logic: knowledge and will are both essential attributes; their non-realization would entail their opposites (ignorance for knowledge, weakness for will).

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irada)
صفات الذات (sifatu al-dhat)
المراد (al-murad)
السَّهْو (al-sahw)
الغَفْلَة (al-ghafla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will is like human will, subject to change or frustration.
Correction: Divine will is an essential attribute that must be universally effective; otherwise it would imply imperfection in God.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari's argument, what would be the consequence if something occurred in God's dominion that He did not will?
A) It would prove God's power is limited.
B) It would imply either God's forgetfulness or weakness.
C) It would be a mere possibility with no implication.
D) It would show God's will is not essential.

Key Takeaway
God's will is an essential attribute that encompasses all created things; nothing occurs in His dominion without His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship or a tragedy, do you truly believe that it is willed by God for a wise purpose, or does your heart waver and accuse God of oversight or weakness like the objection in this passage? Commit to trusting His will even when you don't understand.
?
In your daily decisions, do you acknowledge that everything you experience is under God's will, or do you live as if some events slip from His control? How does that affect your reliance on Him?
?
If God's will is essential and universal, can you ever claim that something 'just happened' without His will? Contemplate a recent event you attributed to chance and repent for making God less than what He is.

Nothing Unwilled Occurs in Allah's Dominion
Al-Ash'ari proves by contradiction that nothing unwilled can exist in Allah's dominion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's proof by contradiction that everything occurring in Allah's dominion is willed by Him.

Core Idea
If anything unwilled existed, Allah would be weak or forgetful — impossible.

Al-Ash'ari presents a crisp logical argument to show that nothing can happen in Allah's creation without His will. The argument works by showing what would follow if the opposite were true—and those consequences are impossible for Allah. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument as a whole
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the flow without pausing.
Notice how Al-Ash'ari uses 'if... then' logic.
2
Identify the two absurd consequences
The proof says if something unwilled existed, two impossible attributes would follow. Find both and understand why they are impossible.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since those attributes cannot apply to Allah, the original possibility (unwilled events) is impossible. Grasp how this shows Allah's will covers all events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج- وأيضًا فإنه لا يجوز أن يكونَ في سُلطانِ اللَّهِ تعالى ما لا يُريدُه؛ لأنَّه لو كانَ في سُلطانِ اللَّهِ تعالى ما لا يُريدُه، لوجب أَحَدُ أَمرين: إمَّا إثباتُ سَهْوِ وغَفْلَةٍ، أو إثباتُ ضَعْفٍ، وعَجْزِ، ووَهَنِ، وتقصيرٍ عَنْ بُلوغ ما يُريدُهُ. فلمَّا لم يَجُز ذلك على اللَّهِ تعالى استحال أن يكون في سلطانه ما لا يريده.
And also, it is not permissible that there be in the dominion of Allah, exalted, what He does not will, because if there were in the dominion of Allah, exalted, what He does not will, then one of two matters would be necessary: either the affirmation of forgetfulness and negligence, or the affirmation of weakness, incapacity, feebleness, and failure to attain what He wills. So since that is not permissible for Allah, exalted, it is impossible for there to be in His dominion what He does not will.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction regarding divine will. Al-Ash'ari argues that if something occurred in Allah's dominion that He did not will, it would force one to attribute to Him either (1) forgetfulness/negligence or (2) weakness/inability/failure. Both are absolutely impossible for Allah. Therefore, nothing unwilled can exist in His dominion. The argument is concise: the premise leads to an absurdity, so the premise is false.

Key Terms
الإرادة
السهو
الغفلة
الضعف
العجز
الوهن
التقصير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Allah's will is limited to only some events, while others happen by chance or independent causes.
Correction: This passage shows that any event occurring in Allah's dominion (the entire universe) must be willed by Him; otherwise, Allah would be imperfect. Therefore, His will encompasses everything that happens.

Checkpoint

A) Forgetfulness and negligence
B) Weakness and incapacity
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
Everything that happens in Allah's dominion happens by His will; otherwise, imperfection would be attributed to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How should this realization change your reliance on Allah and your acceptance of His decree?

When you face a hardship or a trial, do you ever question why Allah allowed it? Reflect on this lesson: If anything happened without His will, He would be imperfect. How does affirming His perfect will in all matters transform your response to difficulty? What would it mean for your trust in Him if you truly internalized that nothing occurs outside His will?
?
Consider a specific recent event in your life that you found difficult or undesirable. Are you able to say with conviction that it was from Allah's will? If not, what does that reveal about your understanding of His dominion? How can you train your heart to accept that everything—even pain—is under His control and therefore carries wisdom?

Objection and Answer on Will and Weakness
Al-Ash'ari responds to an objection by drawing an analogy from knowledge to will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses an analogy between knowledge and will to argue that non-occurrence of the willed entails weakness in the agent.

Core Idea
If a willed thing does not occur, weakness necessarily follows.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari addresses an objection by drawing a parallel between knowledge and will. He argues that just as ignorance follows when a known thing does not occur, weakness follows when a willed thing does not occur. This logical analogy reinforces the necessary connection between divine attributes and their effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Begin by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a response.
The objector asks for the rationale behind the earlier claim.
2
Identify the analogy from knowledge
Al-Ash'ari draws an analogy: just as occurrence of a known thing implies no ignorance, non-occurrence implies ignorance. Identify the parallel for will.
3
Understand the logical necessity
The argument shows that the relationship between will and its object is necessary, not arbitrary. Weakness follows from non-occurrence just as ignorance follows from non-knowledge.
4
Reflect on the implication for divine will
Consider how this analogy supports the claim that God's will is not weak or forgetful. This guards against misconceptions about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: ولِمَ زعمتُمْ ذلكَ؟
قيل له: زَعَمْنَا ذلكَ؛ لأنَّ المرادَ إِذا وَقَعَ، لم يلحَقِّ مَنْ وقَعَ مُرَادُه ضعف، ولا تقصير عن بلوغ ما يُريدُه، بوقوع المرادِ، فواجب أن يلحَقَهُ الضعفُ والتَّقصيرُ عن بلوغ ما يريده إذا لمْ يقَعْ مُراده. ألا ترى أنَّ مَنْ وقَعَ ما يعلَمُهُ لمْ يلْحَقْه جهل، ولا ضِدُّ مِن أضدادِ العلم بذلك الشيء، إذا وقع وهو يعلمه فدل ذلك على أنه إذا لَمْ يقع، وهو لا يعلمه، وجبَ جهلُهُ أو وَصْفُه بضدّ مِنْ أضدادِ العلمِ.
وكذلك إذا كانَ ما يريده لم يجِبْ سَهْوُهُ ولا ضعفه ولا وَهَنُه، ولا تقصيرُهُ عن بلوغ ما يريده. وإذا كانَ ما لا يُرِيدُهُ وجبَ سَهوُهُ، أو ضعفُهُ وَوَهَنُه، وتقصيرُهُ عن بلوغ ما يُرِيدُ .
If he says: And why do you claim that? It is said to him: We claim that because when the willed thing occurs, the one whose willed thing occurs is not affected by weakness nor failure to attain what he wills, due to the occurrence of the willed thing. So it is necessary that weakness and failure to attain what he wills affect him if his willed thing does not occur. Do you not see that the one for whom something he knows occurs is not affected by ignorance nor any opposite of knowledge concerning that thing, when it occurs while he knows it; so that indicates that if it does not occur while he does not know it, his ignorance or description by an opposite of knowledge becomes necessary. And likewise, if what he wills occurs, his forgetfulness, weakness, feebleness, and failure to attain what he wills do not become necessary. And if what he does not will occurs, his forgetfulness, or weakness and feebleness, and failure to attain what he wills become necessary.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector asks for the rationale behind the earlier claim. The answer provides a logical analogy: the occurrence of a willed thing does not cause weakness in the one who wills, just as the occurrence of a known thing does not cause ignorance in the knower. However, if the willed thing does not occur, weakness necessarily follows, just as if the known thing does not occur while the knower is ignorant, ignorance necessarily follows. The analogy shows that the relationship between will and its object mirrors that between knowledge and its object.

السهو
الضعف
الوهن

العلم
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine will is arbitrary, with no necessary connection to its object.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the will necessarily entails the occurrence of its object, otherwise weakness would follow. This is similar to the necessary connection between knowledge and its object.

Checkpoint
In the analogy from knowledge, what follows if a known thing does not occur while the knower does not know it?
A) The knower remains the same
B) Ignorance or its opposite becomes necessary
C) Weakness becomes necessary
D) Forgetfulness becomes necessary

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari demonstrates that the necessary connection between will and its object mirrors that between knowledge and its object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In your life, are there areas where you assume God's will is 'weak' or 'failing' because your prayers seem unanswered? Based on this lesson, what must you conclude about God's perfect will? What change in your perspective is required, and when will you begin to implement it?
?
This argument uses an analogy from knowledge to will. Consider your own knowledge: do you accept that your lack of knowledge leads to ignorance? Now, apply that to God's will: do you truly believe that nothing escapes His will without implying weakness? What does this demand from your trust in God's plan today?

Extending the Argument to Actions of Others
Al-Ash'ari argues that the same implications hold for unwilled actions of others as for Allah's own actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the same logical implications that arise from un-willed acts of Allah also arise from un-willed acts of others within His dominion, thus reinforcing the universality of divine will.

Core Idea
Allah's will encompasses all events, not just His own actions.

Al-Ash'ari extends his earlier argument about unwilled actions of Allah to unwilled actions of others. He shows that the same undesirable attributes (like forgetfulness or weakness) would have to be attributed to Allah if He did not will an action occurring in His dominion, regardless of whether the actor is Allah Himself or a created being. This passage solidifies the doctrine that Allah's will (irādah) covers all created events without exception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that al-Ash'ari asserts that the same necessity applies to unwilled actions of others as to Allah's own actions.
2
Examine the two analogies
The passage provides two analogies: one about knowledge (if ignorance follows from an unknown action of Allah, it also follows from an unknown action of another) and one about divine information (if discrediting Allah follows from a false report about His action, it also follows from a false report about another's action).
These analogies parallel the earlier argument but apply to others' actions.
3
Understand the conclusion
Al-Ash'ari concludes that there is no difference between Allah's own actions and those of others in terms of the necessity of will. This reinforces that Allah's will encompasses all events.
The conclusion is stated explicitly: 'There is no difference in that between what we have agreed is His action and what occurs from other than Him.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا إذا كانَ في كون ما لا يريدُهُ مِن أفعاله - التي اجتمعنا على أنَّها أفعاله - وجوبُ السهو، والغفلة، أو الضعفِ، والوهَنِ، والتقصيرِ عَنْ بُلوغ ما يريدُهُ، فكذلك يَلزَمُ في كونِ ما لا يريدُهُ مِنْ غَيرِهِ ما يلزَمُ في كون ما لا يريدُهُ ممَّا اتفقنا على أنَّه مِنْ أَفعالِهِ. ألا ترى أنَّه إِذا لَزِمَ، مَنْ زَعَمَ أَنَّهُ يَكونُ مِنْ أَفعالِ اللَّهِ ما لا يعلَمُه، أنْ يصفه بالجهل، أو بضد من أضدادِ العلمِ؛ لَزِمَ مثلُ ذلك مَنْ زعمَ أنه يكونُ مِنْ غَيْرِه ما لا يعلَمُه، وكذلك إِذا لَزِمَ مَنْ زعم أَنَّ اللَّهَ يُخْبِرُ أَنَّه يكون من فِعله ما لا يكُونُ - التكذيبُ لِلَّهِ، لَزِمَ مَنْ زعمَ أَنَّ اللَّهَ تعالى يُخبرُ أنه يكون من غيرِهِ ما لا يَكُونُ، التكذيب أيضًا، لا فرق في ذلك بين ما اتفقنا على أنه فعله، وبينَ ما يَكُونُ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ. وكذلك إذا لزم في كونِ ما لا يُريدُه الباري تعالى مِنْ فعِلهِ ضعف وتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده، أو سهو وغفلةٌ، لَزِمَ ذلك في كونِ ما لا يريده مِنْ غَيْرِهِ.
And also, if in the existence of what He does not will from His actions – which we have agreed are His actions – there is necessity of forgetfulness and negligence, or weakness, feebleness, and failure to attain what He wills, then likewise it becomes necessary in the existence of what He does not will from other than Him the same that becomes necessary in the existence of what He does not will from what we have agreed are His actions. Do you not see that if it becomes necessary for one who claims that there is in Allah's actions what He does not know to describe Him with ignorance or with an opposite of knowledge, the like of that becomes necessary for one who claims that there is from other than Him what He does not know. And likewise, if it becomes necessary for one who claims that Allah informs that there will be from His action what will not be – the discrediting of Allah – it becomes necessary for one who claims that Allah, exalted, informs that there will be from other than Him what will not be, discrediting as well. There is no difference in that between what we have agreed is His action and what occurs from other than Him. And likewise, if necessity of weakness and failure to attain what He wills, or forgetfulness and negligence, is established in the existence of what the Creator, exalted, does not will from His action, the same is established in the existence of what He does not will from other than Him.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage extends the previous argument to actions of others. Al-Ash'ari argues that the same consequences that follow from an unwilled action of Allah also follow from an unwilled action of another being (e.g., a human) that occurs in Allah's dominion. He uses two analogies: one from knowledge (if ignorance follows from an unknown action of Allah, it also follows from an unknown action of another), and another from divine information (if discrediting Allah follows from a false report about His own action, it also follows from a false report about another's action). The conclusion is that there is no difference in logical implication between Allah's own actions and those of others when it comes to divine will encompassing them. This reinforces the doctrine that Allah's will (irādah) encompasses all created events.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only Allah's own actions are subject to the necessity of will; actions of others are independent.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the same undesirable attributes would follow if Allah did not will an action of another, so all events in His dominion are subject to His will.

Checkpoint

A) They are not subject to the same implications
B) They are subject to the same implications as Allah's own actions
C) Only actions that are good are willed
D) Allah's will does not extend to others' actions

Key Takeaway
The same undesirable attributes would follow if Allah did not will any event in His dominion, whether it is His own act or another's, reinforcing the universality of divine will.

The Analogical Proof for Divine Will Encompassing All Acts
Ash'ari presents a proof by analogy that divine will encompasses all created acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari uses analogy to prove that God's will must encompass all created acts, and why denying this leads to attributing imperfection to God.

Core Idea
If God's own acts need His will, so do others' acts—or else imperfection follows.

Ash'ari now extends his argument about divine will from God's own acts to all created acts. He uses a powerful analogy: whatever implications follow from claiming that something occurs against God's will in His own acts must also follow for acts of others. This lesson unpacks that analogy and its two concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the chapter heading stating the topic.
The passage uses a dense analogical structure.
2
Identify the main claim and analogical structure
Ash'ari argues that what is true for God's own acts regarding will is also true for others' acts. This is a proof by analogy (tamthīl).
3
Examine the two analogies: knowledge and truthfulness
First analogy: If God were ignorant of His own act, He'd be ignorant; same for others' acts. Second: If God's information about His own act were false, He'd be a liar; same for others' acts.
Both analogies are reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the conclusion
There is no distinction between God's acts and others' acts in these implications. Therefore, God's will must encompass all acts.
5
Reflect on the significance
This establishes that denying God's will over any event leads to impossible attributes in God, preserving His perfection.
This argument safeguards divine omnipotence and will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ
وأيضًا إذا كانَ في كون ما لا يريدُهُ مِن أفعاله - التي اجتمعنا على أنَّها أفعاله - وجوبُ السهو، والغفلة، أو الضعفِ، والوهَنِ، والتقصيرِ عَنْ بُلوغ ما يريدُهُ ، فكذلك يَلزَمُ في كونِ ما لا يريدُهُ مِنْ غَيرِهِ ما يلزَمُ في كون ما لا يريدُهُ ممَّا اتفقنا على أنَّه مِنْ أَفْعالِهِ. ألا ترى أنَّه إِذا لَزِمَ مَنْ زَعَمَ أَنَّهُ يَكونُ مِنْ أَفعالِ اللَّهِ ما لا يعلَمُه، أنْ يصفه بالجهل ، أو بضد من أضدادِ العِلمِ؛ لَزِمَ مثلُ ذلك مَنْ زعمَ أنه يكونُ مِنْ غَيْرِه ما لا يعلَمُه ، وكذلك إِذا لَزِمَ مَنْ زعم أَنَّ اللَّهَ يُخْبِرُ أَنَّه يكون من فِعله ما لا يكُونُ - التكذيبُ لِلَّهِ ، لَزِمَ مَنْ زعمَ أَنَّ اللَّهَ - تعالى - يُخبرُ أنه يكون من غيرِهِ ما لا يَكُونُ، التكذيب [أيضًا ] ، لا فرق في ذلك بين ما اتفقنا على أنه فعله، وبينَ ما يَكُونُ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ. وكذلك إذا لزم في كونِ ما لا يُريدُه الباري -تعالى- مِنْ فعِلهِ ضعف وتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده، أو سهو وغفلةٌ، لَزِمَ ذلك في كونِ ما لا يريده مِنْ غَيْرِهِ.
Chapter: Discourse on Will and That It Encompasses All Created Things. And also, if there is, in the occurrence of what He does not will from His acts - which we agree are His acts - the necessity of forgetfulness, heedlessness, or weakness, frailty, and deficiency from attaining what He wills, then likewise it follows in the occurrence of what He does not will from others what follows in the occurrence of what He does not will from what we agreed are His acts. Do you not see that if it follows, for one who claims that there occurs from God's acts what He does not know, that he describes Him with ignorance or with a contrary of knowledge, then the like of that follows for one who claims that there occurs from other than Him what He does not know. And likewise, if it follows for one who claims that God informs that there will occur from His act what does not occur - the accusation of lying against God - then it follows for one who claims that God - exalted - informs that there will occur from other than Him what does not occur, the accusation of lying as well. There is no difference in that between what we agree is His act and what occurs from other than Him. And likewise, if it follows in the occurrence of what the Creator - exalted - does not will from His act, weakness and deficiency from attaining what He wills, or forgetfulness and heedlessness, then that follows in the occurrence of what He does not will from other than Him.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy (*tamthīl*) establishing that God's will encompasses all created acts. Ash'ari begins with the chapter heading stating the topic: will encompasses all created things. He then argues: if we agreed that God's own acts require His will—otherwise lacking will would imply in Him forgetfulness, weakness, etc.—then the same must hold for acts of others. He draws two parallels: (1) just as claiming God acts without knowledge leads to attributing ignorance to Him, so claiming others act without His knowledge does the same; (2) just as claiming God's information about His own act being false leads to charging God with lying, so claiming the same about others' acts does. He concludes there is no distinction between God's acts and others' acts in these implications. The argument is a *reductio* against anyone who separates God's will from His acts and others' acts.

Key Terms
إرادة
سهو
جهل
تكذيب
ضعف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ash'ari is equating human acts with God's acts in all respects.
Correction: The passage only argues that the implications of denying God's will are the same; it does not claim identity of essence or nature between divine and human acts.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari's analogy, what follows if one claims that God's own act occurs without His will?
A) It implies God is wise.
B) It implies forgetfulness or weakness in God.
C) It implies God is all-powerful.
D) It implies human free will.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari's analogy shows that denying God's will over any act entails attributing imperfection to Him, whether the act is His or another's.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every event in your life—including your own choices—occurs only because God willed it? When you face something you dislike, do you ever secretly resent that it could have been otherwise? If so, are you attributing to God an inability to will what He does not want?
?
Ash'ari argues that denying God's will over others' acts leads to accusing God of lying about His foreknowledge. How does your daily trust in God's promises and news reflect (or fail to reflect) your conviction that His will is accomplished in all things?
?
If you were to fully internalize that nothing happens without God's will, what area of your life would need the most radical change in attitude—your worries, your ambitions, or your relationships? Why is that area hardest to surrender to His will?

All Created Things Are God's Acts
Ash'ari argues from creation to necessary will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Ash'ari argues that nothing can occur in the universe against God's will.

Core Idea
Everything created is God's act, so nothing occurs without His will.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari presents a foundational proof for the Ash'ari doctrine of qadar. He begins with a simple premise: all created things are created by God. From this, he derives a powerful conclusion about the scope of God's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the compact style.
2
Identify the premise
The argument starts with the premise that all created things are creations of God. Note the phrase 'دلَّتِ الدّلالةُ' indicating proof.
This premise is taken as established.
3
Follow the logical conclusion
Since God cannot do what He does not will, and all created things are His acts, it follows that nothing from others can happen against His will. Understand why this is a necessary conclusion.
Think about what would happen if the premise were false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا فقد دلَّتِ الدّلالةُ على أن كلَّ المُحدَثاتِ مخلوقات للَّهِ تعالى. فإذَا استحالَ أَنْ يفعل الباري -تعالى- ما لا يريدُهُ استحالَ أَنْ يقعَ مِن غيرِهِ ما لا يُرِيدُهُ إِذْ كانَ ذلك أجمعُ أفعالا لله -تعالى.
وأيضًا فلو كان في العَالَمِ ما لا يريده الله -تعالى-، لكانَ ما يَكْرَهُ كَوْنَهُ، ولو كَانَ ما يَكْرَهُ كَوْنَه، لَكَانَ مَا يَأْبَى كَونَه . وهذا يُوجِبُ أَنَّ المعاصِيَ كانَتْ ، شاءَ اللَّهُ أَم أَبَى ، وهذه صِفةُ الضعيف المقهور، وتعالَى ربُّنا عن ذلكَ علوا كبيرًا .
And also, the proof has indicated that all created things are creations of God - exalted. So if it is impossible for the Creator - exalted - to do what He does not will, it is impossible for anything to occur from other than Him that He does not will, since all of that is acts of God - exalted. And also, if there were in the world something that God - exalted - does not will, then there would be something He dislikes to be, and if there were something He dislikes to be, then there would be something He abhors to be. This necessitates that sins occurred whether God wills or abhors, and this is the attribute of the weak and overpowered, and our Lord is exalted above that, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a two-part proof establishing that nothing occurs contrary to God's will. First, Ash'ari states a premise: evidence proves all created things (*al-muḥdathāt*) are God's creations. Since it is impossible for God to do what He does not will, it follows that nothing from others can occur against His will, because all such occurrences are ultimately God's acts. Second, he considers the contrary: if something existed that God does not will, it would be something He dislikes to exist, and that would mean He abhors its existence. This would imply that sins happen regardless of God's wish, which is a sign of weakness and being overpowered—impossible for God. The argument reinforces that everything occurs by God's will, including acts of obedience and disobedience, but He may dislike the latter yet still will them for wisdom.

Key Terms
محدثات (created things)
مخلوقات (creations)
استحال (is impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that evil deeds are not willed by God.
Correction: This passage shows that all created things are God's acts, so even sinful acts are willed by God in the sense of existing, though He may dislike them.

Checkpoint
Why does Ash'ari conclude that nothing occurs from others against God's will?
A) Because others are independent agents.
B) Because all created things are God's acts.
C) Because God cannot will evil.
D) Because God does not know the future.

Key Takeaway
All created things are God's acts, so nothing in creation defies His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every event in your life—even the painful ones—is an act of God that He has willed, how does that reshape your reaction to a recent disappointment or loss? Will you now turn to Him in gratitude or complaint?
?
Do you truly believe that your own choices are under God's will, or do you secretly reserve some area of your life as 'yours'? What would it mean to surrender that area to Him today?
?
When you sin, do you think God merely permits it, or do you accept that He willed it for a wisdom beyond your understanding? How might this change your repentance and reliance on His mercy?

The Absurdity of Anything Against God's Will
Ash'ari uses reductio to argue against anything occurring against God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for anything to occur in the world without God's will, using a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Core Idea
If anything occurred against God's will, God would be weak and overpowered.

Imam al-Ash'ari strengthens his case with a second argument. He imagines the hypothetical scenario where something exists that God does not will. He shows that this leads to an absurd consequence: that God would be weak and overpowered, which is impossible for the Almighty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the second argument carefully. Notice the hypothetical 'if' statement at the beginning.
This argument is a conditional chain.
2
Trace the conditional chain
Identify the sequence: if something exists that God does not will → it is something He dislikes to be → it is something He abhors to be → this implies sins occur whether God wills or not.
Each step follows from the previous.
3
Understand the absurd consequence
The final consequence is that God would be described as 'weak and overpowered', which is impossible for the Lord. This proves the initial hypothesis is false.
Reductio ad absurdum: if a premise leads to impossibility, the premise is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا فقد دلَّتِ الدّلالةُ على أن كلَّ المُحدَثاتِ مخلوقات للَّهِ تعالى. فإذَا استحالَ أَنْ يفعل الباري -تعالى- ما لا يريدُهُ استحالَ أَنْ يقعَ مِن غيرِهِ ما لا يُرِيدُهُ إِذْ كانَ ذلك أجمعُ أفعالا لله -تعالى.
وأيضًا فلو كان في العَالَمِ ما لا يريده الله -تعالى-، لكانَ ما يَكْرَهُ كَوْنَهُ، ولو كَانَ ما يَكْرَهُ كَوْنَه، لَكَانَ مَا يَأْبَى كَونَه . وهذا يُوجِبُ أَنَّ المعاصِيَ كانَتْ ، شاءَ اللَّهُ أَم أَبَى ، وهذه صِفةُ الضعيف المقهور، وتعالَى ربُّنا عن ذلكَ علوا كبيرًا .
And also, the proof has indicated that all created things are creations of God - exalted. So if it is impossible for the Creator - exalted - to do what He does not will, it is impossible for anything to occur from other than Him that He does not will, since all of that is acts of God - exalted. And also, if there were in the world something that God - exalted - does not will, then there would be something He dislikes to be, and if there were something He dislikes to be, then there would be something He abhors to be. This necessitates that sins occurred whether God wills or abhors, and this is the attribute of the weak and overpowered, and our Lord is exalted above that, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a two-part proof establishing that nothing occurs contrary to God's will. First, Ash'ari states a premise: evidence proves all created things (*al-muḥdathāt*) are God's creations. Since it is impossible for God to do what He does not will, it follows that nothing from others can occur against His will, because all such occurrences are ultimately God's acts. Second, he considers the contrary: if something existed that God does not will, it would be something He dislikes to exist, and that would mean He abhors its existence. This would imply that sins happen regardless of God's wish, which is a sign of weakness and being overpowered—impossible for God. The argument reinforces that everything occurs by God's will, including acts of obedience and disobedience, but He may dislike the latter yet still will them for wisdom.

Key Terms
يَكْرَهُ (dislikes)
يَأْبَى (abhors)
ضعيف مقهور (weak and overpowered)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God wills sin, He approves of it.
Correction: This argument merely shows that He wills the existence of sinful acts, not that He approves of them. He dislikes and abhors them in terms of moral approval.

Checkpoint
What does Ash'ari conclude would be the attribute of God if something existed against His will?
A) Merciful and just.
B) Weak and overpowered.
C) Knowing and wise.
D) Patient and forgiving.

Key Takeaway
Anything occurring against God's will would imply divine weakness, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you found yourself believing that something happened outside of God's will, perhaps due to its apparent evil? How does this passage correct that belief, and what does it ask you to trust about God's sovereignty—even over what you dislike?
?
What sin in your life have you considered 'too great' to be included in God's will? How does the reductio argument force you to either accept that God's will encompasses all things—including your sin—or face the absurd conclusion that God is weak?
?
If you truly believed that nothing occurs against God's will, how would that change the way you respond to a recent disappointment or tragedy? Would you still feel entitled to question God's wisdom, or would you humble yourself before His absolute power?
