The Logical Proof That All Acts Are by Allah's Will
The first part of Ash'ari's proof: logical necessity of universal divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical connection between Allah's will and the occurrence of all events, and why the occurrence of sins does not imply weakness in Allah.

Core Idea
If Allah cannot act against His will, nothing can happen against His will.

Ash'ari opens this passage with a powerful logical proof: since it is impossible for Allah to do what He does not will, it follows that no creature can do what Allah does not will, because all acts are ultimately His. This sets the foundation for understanding divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first part of the Arabic text carefully. Notice the conditional structure 'إذا ... استحال ... استحال'.
This is a tightly reasoned argument; each clause matters.
2
Understand the implication
The argument is that if Allah cannot do something against His will, then creatures cannot do something against His will either. Why? Because all acts are attributed to Allah as Creator.
3
Consider the consequence
If something occurred against Allah's will, it would mean He dislikes its existence, which would imply weakness. But Allah is exalted above that.
This shows the absurdity of the contrary assumption.
4
Reflect on the significance
This proof shows that even sins are willed by Allah in the sense of His creative will, not necessarily His legislative approval.
Keep this distinction in mind as we proceed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذَا استحالَ أَنْ يفعل الباري -تعالى- ما لا يريدُهُ استحالَ أَنْ يقعَ مِن غيرِهِ ما لا يُرِيدُهُ إِذْ كانَ ذلك أجمعُ أفعالا لله -تعالى-.
وأيضًا فلو كان في العَالَمِ ما لا يريده الله -تعالى-، لكانَ ما يَكْرَهُ كَوْنَهُ، ولو كَانَ ما يَكْرَهُ كَوْنَه، لَكَانَ مَا يَأْبَى كَونَه . وهذا يُوجِبُ أَنَّ المعاصِيَ كانَتْ، شاءَ اللَّهُ أَم أَبَى ، وهذه صِفةُ الضعيف المقهور، وتعالَى ربُّنا عن ذلكَ علوا كبيرًا .
مِثالُ الزَّمِنِ فإن قال قائل : خَبِّرُونَا عن ملك من ملوكِ الدُّنْيَا، لَوْ مَرَّ برجلٍ مُقْعَدِ زَمِن أعمى فشتَمَهُ، والملك لا يريد شَتْمه، أتقولون إِنَّ الملك يلْحَقُهُ في ذلك ضعفٌ ووَهَنْ وتقصير عَنْ بُلُوغ ما يُرِيدُهُ؛ إذ أرادَ أنْ لَا يَشْتُمَهُ فَشَتَمَهُ؟
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثاتِ
قيل له : أجل؛ ولَوْ لم يكُنْ ما أرادَ الزَّمِنُ مِن شَتْمِهِ وكانَ ما أرادَهُ الملِكُ مِنْ مَدْحِهِ إيَّاه، كانَ ذلك أولَى بِزَوالِ الضعفِ والوَهَنِ عَنْهُ.
على أن الملك إذا لم يُرِدْ شتمَ الأعمى المقْعَد لَهُ، فقد كَرِهَ شَتمُهُ إيَّاهُ، وأَبَى ذلكَ، وقَدْ كانَ شَتْمُهُ، شَاءَ ذلِكَ الملِكُ أو أَبَاه، وهذِهِ صِفَةُ الضَّعْفِ والوَهَنِ .
وأيضًا فإن مَنْ إذا أرادَ مِنَّا أمرًا كانَ، وإذا لم يُرِدْ كونَهُ لَمْ يَكُنْ، أَوْلَى بصفة الاقتدارِ، ممنْ يُريدُ كونَ ما لَا يَكُونُ، وأنْ لا يكون ما يكونُ. وربُّ العالمين لا يوصف إلا بالوصف الذي هو أولى بصفة الاقتدار .
If it is impossible for the Creator - exalted - to do what He does not will, then it is impossible for anything else to occur that He does not will, since all of that are acts of Allah - exalted. And also, if there were in the world something that Allah - exalted - does not will, then He would dislike its existence, and if He dislikes its existence, then He would reject its existence. This entails that sins occur whether Allah wills or refuses, and this is the attribute of the weak and overcome, and our Lord is exalted above that greatly. The example of the lame: if a speaker says: Tell us about a king of the kings of the world, if he passed by a man who is quadriplegic, lame, blind, and he insulted him, while the king does not will his insult, do you say that the king is thereby affected by weakness, feebleness, and deficiency from attaining what he wills, since he willed that he not insult him but he insulted him? Chapter on speech about the will and that it encompasses all originated things. It was said to him: Yes; and if what the lame man willed of insulting him did not occur, and what the king willed of his praising him occurred, that would be more fitting for the removal of weakness and feebleness from him. Moreover, since the king did not will the insult of the blind quadriplegic to him, he disliked his insulting him, and he rejected that, and yet his insult occurred, whether that king willed or rejected it, and this is the attribute of weakness and feebleness. And also, whoever among us, when he wills a matter it occurs, and when he does not will its existence it does not occur, is more deserving of the attribute of power than one who wills the existence of what does not occur and that what occurs not occur. And the Lord of the worlds is not described except by the attribute which is more fitting for the attribute of power.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that all events occur by Allah's will, followed by an objection-and-answer using an analogy. The structure is: (1) a logical proof, (2) an objection, (3) an answer. 

The author first argues that if it is impossible for Allah to do what He does not will, it is likewise impossible for any creature to do what Allah does not will, because all acts are ultimately acts of Allah. If something occurred against His will, He would dislike and reject it, but since sins do occur, they would then occur against His will, implying weakness—which is impossible for Allah. 

Then an objector raises an analogy: a king passes a lame blind man who insults him, but the king does not will the insult. Does this mean the king is weak for failing to prevent the insult? The objector implies that if the king were truly powerful, he would prevent the insult. 

The answer begins by agreeing ('ajal') but turns the analogy around: if the king had willed praise and that praise had occurred, that would show his power more than the undesired insult occurring. The point is that the occurrence of what one does not will does not necessarily indicate weakness; rather, the inability to prevent it would be weakness. Since the king did not will the insult, and it occurred, one might think him weak, but the true attribute of power is that one's will is effective—what one wills occurs, and what one does not will does not occur. This applies to the king only partially, but Allah is absolutely powerful: whatever He wills occurs, and whatever He does not will does not occur. Thus, the occurrence of sins cannot be against His will, because then His will would be ineffective.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الضعف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sins occur against Allah's will because Allah hates them.
Correction: Ash'ari argues that if Allah hated sins and they still occurred, that would imply He is weak. Instead, Allah wills all events in the sense of creative will, though He hates them in terms of legislative will.

Checkpoint
According to the logical proof, why is it impossible for anything to occur against Allah's will?
A) Because Allah controls everything directly.
B) Because Allah cannot do what He does not will, and all acts are His.
C) Because Allah punishes anyone who does wrong.
D) Because angels prevent it.

Key Takeaway
All events, including sins, occur by Allah's will; otherwise, Allah would be weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every act, including your sins, occurs by Allah's will, does that make you feel less accountable or more dependent on His mercy? How does this truth change the way you approach repentance?
?
When you disobey Allah, do you truly believe that He could have prevented it but willed to let it happen for a wisdom? How does that affect your gratitude for His forgiveness?
?
The passage says that if something occurred against Allah's will, He would be weak. Do you live as though Allah is in full control, or do you sometimes act as if your choices escape His will? What would it look like to surrender that illusion today?

The King Analogy Refuting the Objection of Weakness
Ash'ari's analogy answering the objection of weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari uses the analogy of a king and a lame man to show that the occurrence of what one does not will does not necessarily indicate weakness.

Core Idea
True power is having one's will fulfilled, not preventing every unwanted event.

An objector raises a scenario: a king passes a lame man who insults him, but the king does not will the insult. Does this mean the king is weak? Ash'ari answers by turning the analogy around and then drawing the real lesson about divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy passage
Carefully read the story of the king and the lame blind man. Understand the objector's point: the king could not prevent the insult.
Notice the dialogue structure.
2
See the initial response
The author first agrees ('ajal') but then shifts the focus to what would show true power.
The agreement is a rhetorical device.
3
Understand the criterion
True power is that what one wills occurs and what one does not will does not occur. The king's will is not fully effective.
This is the key principle.
4
Apply to Allah
Allah's will is absolutely effective, so nothing can occur against His will. The analogy shows that the occurrence of unwanted events does not prove weakness in Allah.

Source Text

Objection to Divine Will as Requiring Helpers
Objection and partial response on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilī objection that God's will, if analogous to human will, implies weakness, and the beginning of Imam Ash'ari's rebuttal challenging that analogy.

Core Idea
God's will does not need helpers—unlike human will.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange from Imam Ash'ari's al-Luma'. An objector (likely Mu'tazilī) argues that if God's will operated like human will, it would require external support, implying imperfection. Ash'ari begins his response by questioning the basis of this analogy. The argument is incomplete, ending mid-sentence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a chapter title, an objection, and the start of a response.
The text ends abruptly—be aware of the incompleteness.
2
Identify the objection
The objector uses two analogies: a king and a crippled blind man, and a general statement about human will. Summarize the objection in your own words.
3
Identify the beginning of the response
Ash'ari responds by turning the objector's reasoning against him: if power depends on followers, then one who wills the opposite would be more powerful. He also challenges the premise that human power comes from helpers.
The response is incomplete; note where it breaks off.
4
Reflect on the incompleteness
Given the abrupt end, consider what Ash'ari might have concluded. How would he defend the doctrine that divine will does not entail weakness?
This lesson covers only the available text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائر المحدثات
قيل له أجل ولَوْ لم يكُنْ ما أراد الزمن من شتمه وكان ما أراده الملك من مدحه إياه كان ذلك أولى بزوال الضعف والوهن عنه
على أن الملك إذا لم يرد شتم الأعمى المقعد له فقد كره شتمه إياه وأبى ذلك وقد كان شتمه شاء ذلك الملك أو أباه وهذه صفة الضعف والوهن
وأيضًا فإن من إذا أراد منا أمرًا كان وإذا لم يرد كونه لم يكن أولى بصفة الاقتدار ممن يريد كون ما لا يكون وأن لا يكون ما يكون ورب العالمين لا يوصف إلا بالوصف الذي هو أولى بصفة الاقتدار
فإن قال قائل من إذا أراد [منا؟] أمرًا كان وإذا لم يرده لم يكن إنما يكون اقتداره بمن يتبعه ويعينه ويكون ضعفه لقلّة أنصاره وأتباعه ورب العالمين لا يتكثر بأحد
يقال لهم فما أنكرتم إن كان هذا على ما تدعون أن يكون من أراد من فعله كون ما لا يكون وأن لا يكون [ما يكون] فهو أولى بصفة الاقتدار ممن يريد كون ما يكون وألا يكون ما لا يكون [لأنه] إنما يصح وصفه بالاقتدار لأنه ممن يتكثر بفعله ويجب اقتداره بمن ينصره وضعفه بمن يقعد عنه
ويقال لهم لم زعمتم أن من أراد منا كون ما يكون إنما يصح وصفه بالاقتدار لأنه ممن يقوى بكثرة من يتبعه ويضعف بكثرة من يقعد عنه
فإن قالوا لأن هذا فيما بيننا هكذا قيل وكذلك إنما يدل الفعل الحكمي على أن من ظهر منه عالم قادر لأنه ممن يعلم بعلم ويقدر بقدرة لأنَّا كذلك وجدنا فيما بيننا من دلت الأفعال الحكمية على أنه عالم قادر
فما أنكرتم من أنه واجب على اعتلالكم أن لا تدل الأفعال
Chapter on speech concerning will and that it encompasses all originated things. It was said to him: Yes, and if what the ailing man intended of his insult did not occur, and what the king intended of his praise of him occurred, that would be more fitting for the removal of weakness and frailty from him. Moreover, when the king does not will the insult of the blind cripple for him, then he has disliked his insult of him and refused that, and [yet] his insult occurred whether that king willed or refused, and this is the attribute of weakness and frailty. And also, whoever among us when he wills a thing it is, and when he does not will it to be it is not, is more deserving of the attribute of power than one who wills that what is not should be and that what is should not be. And the Lord of the worlds is not described except by the attribute which is more deserving of the attribute of power. Then if a questioner says: When one among us wills a thing it is, and when he does not will it it is not, his power is only through those who follow and help him, and his weakness is due to the fewness of his helpers and followers, and the Lord of the worlds does not seek reinforcement through anyone. It is said to them: What prevents you, if this is as you claim, that one who from his act wills that what is not should be and that what is should not be is more deserving of the attribute of power than one who wills that what is should be and that what is not should not be, because he is only correctly described with power because he is one who seeks reinforcement through his act, his power must be through those who help him, and his weakness through those who fail him? And it is said to them: Why do you claim that one among us who wills that what is should be is only correctly described with power because he is one who becomes strong through the multitude of those who follow him and becomes weak through the multitude of those who fail him? Then if they say: Because this among us is so, it is said: Likewise, the wise act only indicates that one from whom it appears is knowing and powerful because he is one who knows through knowledge and is powerful through power, because thus we have found among us those whose wise acts indicate that he is knowing and powerful. So what prevents you from [the conclusion] that it is necessary according to your reasoning that the acts do not indicate
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is part of a dialectical argument about divine will (al-irāda) and power (al-iqtidār). It opens with a chapter title stating that God's will encompasses all originated things. The text then presents an objection (likely from a Mu'tazilī opponent) arguing that if God's will were similar to human will, it would entail weakness because human will only succeeds with helpers. The objection uses a comparison: a king who wills praise from a disabled person but does not will insult, yet the insult still occurs—this shows the king's weakness. The objector concludes that one whose will is always effective is more deserving of power than one whose will sometimes fails. The objector then asserts that human effective will depends on followers and helpers, so God, who does not need reinforcement, cannot be described with such will. The author's response (beginning on the next line) uses a counter-argument: If the opponent's reasoning is followed, then one whose will produces the opposite of what occurs would be more powerful. The author then challenges the opponent's premise that human power depends on numbers of followers. Finally, the author draws an analogy: just as wise acts in humans indicate knowledge and power from knowledge and power (not from helpers), so too should God's acts indicate His attributes. The argument is cut off mid-sentence, so the conclusion is missing.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الاقتدار
الضعف
الوهن
الملك
الأعمى المقعد
الفعل الحكمي
العالم القادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will is like human will and requires external support or helpers.
Correction: God's will is independent of any helper; His power is intrinsic, not dependent on followers.

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim about one among us whose will is effective?
A) Such a person is deserving of power without any helpers.
B) Such a person's power is only through those who follow and help him.
C) Such a person's will never fails.
D) Such a person is comparable to God in power.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilī objection that God's will implies weakness is rebutted by showing that human power's dependence on helpers does not apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that God's will 'needs' your obedience or worship to be fulfilled? How does this passage's insistence on God's self-sufficiency challenge that subtle assumption?
?
The objector saw weakness in God if His will were as ineffective as human will. Where in your life do you depend on others to accomplish your will? How does the lesson's logic about power and independence shift your trust from creation to the Creator?
?
The author argues that true power is shown by the ability to produce opposite outcomes. When a prayer of yours goes unanswered, do you feel God's power is limited? How can the logic of this passage reshape your trust in God's will, even when it contradicts your desires?

The Reductio on Divine Power
Ash'ari presents a reductio against Mu'tazila definition of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari uses reductio ad absurdum to challenge the Mu'tazila notion that divine power is analogous to human power depending on followers.

Core Idea
If power means willing what exists, then willing the opposite would be even greater power.

Ash'ari begins by attacking the Mu'tazila assumption that God's power is like human power, which requires followers for strength. He presents a reductio: if the criterion of power is bringing about what one wills, then someone who wills the opposite would be more powerful. This forces the Mu'tazila to reconsider their definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the rhetorical question structure.
The author uses a direct address 'It is said to them'.
2
Identify the reductio premise
Find the conditional statement 'if this is as you claim' and what follows. What assumption of the Mu'tazila is being challenged?
3
Understand the definition of power
The passage implies that the Mu'tazila define power as bringing about what one wills. How does the reductio expose a problem with that definition?
Consider who would be considered 'more powerful' under that definition.
4
Reflect on the implication about supporters
Notice the phrase about being strengthened by action and power depending on supporters. What does this reveal about the Mu'tazila's underlying assumption?
This sets up the next part of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال لهم : فما أنكرتُم إن كانَ هذا على ما تَدعونَ، أَنْ يكونَ مَنْ أرادَ مِنْ فِعله كونَ ما لا يكونُ، وأن لا يكون [ما يكون] فهو أولى بصفة الاقتدار مِمَّن يريدُ كونَ ما يكونُ وألا يكون ما لا يكونُ، [لأنه ] إنما يصح وصفه بالاقتدار ؛ لأنه ممن يتكثّرُ بفعله، ويجب اقتدارُه بِمَن ينصرُهُ، وضَعْفُهُ بِمنْ يَقْعُدُ عَنْهُ؟! ويقال لهم : لم زعمتم أن من أراد مِنا كونَ ما يكون إنما يصح وصفه بالاقتدارِ ؛ لأنّه ممنْ يقْوَى بِكثرة مَنْ يتبعُه، ويضعُفُ بكثرةٍ مَنْ يقْعُدُ عَنْهُ؟ فإنْ قالوا : لأنَّ هذا فيما بيننا هكذا، قيل : وكذلك إنما يدلُّ الفعلُ الحِكْميُّ على أنّ مَنْ ظَهرَ مِنْه عالِمٌ قادِرٌ ؛ لأنَّه ممَّنْ يَعلَمُ بعِلم ويَقْدِرُ بقُدرَةٍ ؛ لأنَّا كذلكَ وجَدْنا، فيما بيننا، مَنْ دلتِ الأفعال الحكمية على أنه عالِمٌ قادِرُ . فما أنكرتُمْ من أنه واجب على اعتلالكم أن لا تدل الأفعال الحكمية على أنَّ البارِيَ -تعالى- قادر عالمٌ .
It is said to them: 'What prevents you, if this is as you claim, that one who from his action wills the existence of what does not exist, and the non-existence of what exists, is more worthy of the attribute of power than one who wills the existence of what does exist and the non-existence of what does not exist? Because he is only correctly described as powerful because he is one who is strengthened by his action, and his power depends on those who support him, and his weakness by those who hold back from him?' And it is said to them: 'Why do you claim that one among us who wills the existence of what exists is only correctly described as powerful because he is strengthened by the multitude of his followers, and weakened by the multitude of those who hold back from him?' If they say: 'Because this is how it is among us,' it is said: 'Likewise, the wise act only indicates that the one from whom it appears is knowing and powerful because he is one who knows by knowledge and is powerful by power, because we have found it thus among us: that wise acts indicate that one is knowing and powerful. So what prevents you from it being necessary according to your reasoning that wise acts do not indicate that the Exalted Creator is powerful and knowing?'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's claim that God's power is analogous to human power, which depends on followers. The Ash'ari begins with a reductio ad absurdum: if power means willing what exists and not willing what does not exist (as humans do), then one who wills the opposite would be even more powerful. Then he questions their standard that human power depends on followers. They respond that this is how it is among humans. The Ash'ari then turns their own analogy against them: if human wise acts indicate knowledge and power because humans have knowledge and power, then by the same reasoning, God's wise acts should indicate His knowledge and power—but on their own reasoning, since God has no followers, His acts would not indicate power and knowledge, which is absurd. Thus their position is self-defeating.

Key Terms
الاقتدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power is similar to human power in that it depends on followers.
Correction: God's power is absolute and does not depend on any external support.

Checkpoint
What is the reductio premise that the Ash'ari challenges?
A) That God's power depends on angels.
B) That power means bringing about what one wills.
C) That power is measured by the number of followers.
D) That human power is limited.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila definition of power leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a deeply held desire of yours goes unfulfilled, do you find yourself questioning God's power? How does this passage's reductio challenge you to rethink what true power means beyond mere outcomes?
?
Have you ever assumed that God's power is most clearly seen when things go according to your plan? What would it take for you to trust that His power is equally present in what you consider 'failure'?
?
In what area of your life are you most tempted to measure God's power by human standards—by visible success or dominance? What concrete step can you take today to submit your definition of power to the one this passage points toward, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Turning the Analogy Against the Mu'tazila
Ash'ari turns the Mu'tazila's analogy against them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari turns the Mu'tazila's own analogy to show that their reasoning undermines their belief in God's power and knowledge.

Core Idea
If human acts indicate power because of followers, then God's acts would not indicate His power on that logic.

After the reductio, Ash'ari directly challenges the Mu'tazila's claim that power depends on followers. When they admit this is based on human experience, he turns the tables: if we use human experience to judge divine attributes, then since God has no followers, His actions would not indicate His power and knowledge—an unacceptable conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the direct question and response structure.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's response
Find how they justify the claim that power depends on followers. What reason do they give?
They appeal to human experience.
3
See how Ash'ari applies the same logic to God
Notice the parallel: if human wise acts indicate knowledge and power because of human experience, then the same should apply to God. But what is the problem?
God does not have followers like humans do.
4
Understand the self-defeating conclusion
The Ash'ari concludes that on their reasoning, wise acts would not indicate God's power and knowledge. Why is that problematic for the Mu'tazila?
They themselves affirm that God is powerful and knowing.

Why God Does Not Compel Faith
Ash'ari refutes Mu'tazila on divine will and compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school holds that God's will is for faith to be voluntary so that reward is meaningful, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite objection that God could compel belief.

Core Idea
God wills faith voluntarily to preserve human accountability and reward.

Imam Ash'ari opens a new chapter on divine will, asserting that God's will encompasses all created acts. He then addresses a Mu'tazilite objection: if God is all-powerful, why doesn't He compel belief? This would show no weakness. Ash'ari's response is subtle: God wills faith to be a voluntary act so that believers earn reward. Compulsion would nullify the very basis of reward. This passage clarifies that God's will is not constrained by inability but by a wise purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Ash'ari structures his argument: statement, objection, response.
2
Identify the components
Identify the initial statement about divine will, the Mu'tazilite objection, and the Ash'ari response. Understand the flow of the argument.
3
Understand the analogy used
The Mu'tazila use an analogy: human wise acts indicate a knowing/powerful agent. Reflect on how this is used to set up the objection about compulsion.
This analogy is a common kalam tool.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objection says compulsion is possible and would show no weakness. Ash'ari responds by explaining the purpose of will: voluntary faith for reward.
Key insight: God's will is purposeful, not arbitrary.
5
Grasp the theological implication
Consider why this matters for understanding divine justice and human accountability. The response preserves human free will in earning reward.
This connects to broader soteriology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلامِ في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ الحكمية على أنَّ البارِيَ -تعالى- قادر عالمٌ . وكلك يُعَارَضُونَ بأَنَّ الأفعال الحكمية تدل على أن من ظهرت منه عالِمٌ قادِر ؛ لأنه ممن له علم وقدرة، من أجل أن ذلك فيما بيننا كذلك. فإن قال قائل : ما أنكرتُم أن لا يلحق الباري الضعفُ والوَهنُ والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده ؛ لأنّه يقدر أنْ يُلجِيءَ عِبادَه إلى ما أرادَ كونَهُ منهم؟ قيل لهم : إن الباري -تعالى- إنما أراد كون الإيمان منهم - أصلك بأن يقع ذلك منهم طوعًا يستحقون عليه الثواب، وإذا أَلْجَأَهُم إليه لم يكونوا - عِنْدَكَ - طائعين، ولا للثواب مستحقين .
Chapter on speech about the will, and that it encompasses all created things that are wise acts, that the Exalted Creator is powerful and knowing. And all of them are refuted by [the statement] that wise acts indicate that the one from whom they appear is knowing and powerful, because he is one who has knowledge and power, because that is how it is among us. So if someone says: 'What prevents you from asserting that weakness, feebleness, and inability to achieve what He wills do not befall the Creator, because He is able to compel His servants to what He wills to come from them?' It is said to them: 'The Exalted Creator only willed the existence of faith from them according to your principle, that it occurs from them voluntarily so that they deserve reward for it, and if He compelled them to it, they would not be — in your view — obedient, nor deserving of reward.'
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on the divine will, asserting that it encompasses all created things and that God is powerful and knowing. It then presents a refutation: the Mu'tazila claim that wise acts indicate the agent is knowing and powerful because they have knowledge and power—based on human analogy. An objection is raised: if God is powerful, why could He not compel people to believe? That would show no weakness. The answer is that God wills faith to be voluntary so that humans deserve reward. Compulsion would nullify the voluntary nature and thus reward. This refutes the idea that God's inability to compel is a defect; rather, it is by His wise will that He leaves humans free to earn reward.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المُحْدَثَات
الطوع
الإلجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's inability to compel belief shows weakness or limitation in His power.
Correction: Ash'ari explains that God deliberately wills faith to be voluntary so that humans can earn reward; compulsion would negate the purpose of faith as a test.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari, why does God not compel people to have faith?
A) Because He is unable to compel them.
B) Because He wills it to be voluntary so they deserve reward.
C) Because compulsion would make them stronger in faith.
D) Because the Mu'tazila deny compulsion.

Key Takeaway
God wills human faith to be a voluntary act, not compelled, so that it becomes a basis for reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
In what areas of your life are you trying to compel others to believe or act as you want, ignoring that God Himself does not compel? What does this passage teach you about respecting others' free will?

God's Will and the Refutation of Compulsion
Ash'ari announces the scope of divine will and refutes an objection about God's power to compel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari argument distinguishes between God's power to compel and His wise choice not to, and how this is grounded in the purpose of faith and reward.

Core Idea
God's not compelling faith is not weakness but wisdom, preserving the voluntary reward of faith.

Imam al-Ash'ari anticipates a powerful objection: if God can compel belief, why does He not? Some might think this implies a limit on His power. In this passage, Ash'ari refutes that by clarifying the very purpose of faith—it must be voluntary to merit reward. Let's read the passage to see how he defends divine will and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage opens the chapter and then immediately presents an objection and response.
The passage is a complete unit: statement of topic, objection, and answer.
2
Identify the objection
Find the sentence that begins with 'فإن قال قائل'. This is the opponent's challenge. What exactly does he claim about God? That weakness, frailty, and failure could be attributed to God if He does not compel His servants to do what He wills.
The opponent uses God's ability to compel as a premise to argue that not compelling implies imperfection.
3
Analyze the answer
Read Ash'ari's response starting with 'قيل لهم'. He does not deny God's ability to compel. Instead, he points out that God willed faith to occur voluntarily (ṭawʿan) so that believers would deserve reward (thawāb). Compulsion would negate voluntariness and thus the basis for reward.
This shows that divine wisdom, not lack of power, explains why God does not force faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلامِ في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ الحكمية على أنَّ البارِيَ -تعالى- قادر عالمٌ . وكذلك يُعارَضون بأن الأفعال الحكمية تدل على أن من ظهرت منه عالم قادر؛ لأنه ممن له علم وقدرة، من أجل أن ذلك فيما بيننا كذلك. فإن قال قائل: ما أنكرتم أن لا يلحق الباري الضعف والوهن والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده؛ لأنه يقدر أن يُلجئ عباده إلى ما أراد كونه منهم؟ قيل لهم: إن الباري تعالى إنما أراد كون الإيمان منهم - أصلك بأن يقع ذلك منهم طوعاً يستحقون عليه الثواب، وإذا ألجأهم إليه لم يكونوا - عندك - طائعين، ولا للثواب مستحقين.
Chapter on speech about the will and that it encompasses all contingent things, according to the fact that the Creator - exalted - is powerful, knowing. And similarly they object by saying that the contingent actions indicate that the one from whom they appear is knowing, powerful; because he is one who possesses knowledge and power, because that is the case among us. So if someone says: What prevents you from admitting that weakness, frailty, and failure to achieve what He wills might befall the Creator, because He is able to compel His servants to do what He willed from them? It is said to them: The Creator - exalted - only willed the occurrence of faith from them - according to your principle - that it should occur from them voluntarily, deserving reward for it; and if He compelled them to it, they would not - according to you - be voluntary, nor deserving of reward.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on the divine attribute of will (al-irāda). The author first states the topic: that the will encompasses all created things, and that God is described as powerful and knowing. He then anticipates an objection from the Mu'tazila, who argue that contingent actions (al-afʿāl al-ḥukmiyya) indicate a doer who is knowing and powerful, based on analogy with human actions. The objector then poses a challenge: why do you deny that weakness (ḍaʿf), frailty (wahn), and failure (taqṣīr) could affect God if He does not attain what He wills? After all, He is able to compel (yuljiʾ) His servants to do what He wills from them. The author's answer is that God willed faith to occur voluntarily (ṭawʿan) so that they would deserve reward (thawāb). If He compelled them, they would not be voluntary agents and would not deserve reward. Thus, God's not compelling is not a sign of weakness, but rather a wise choice to preserve the meaningfulness of reward. The argument follows the structure of objection (ifāda) and answer (jawāb).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's failure to force everyone to believe means He is unable to do so, implying a limitation on His power.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that God has the power to compel (as the objector admits), but He chooses not to because faith must be voluntary for reward to be meaningful. Thus, not compelling is a matter of wisdom, not inability.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Ash'ari's response, why did God not compel people to believe?
A) Because He is not able to compel them.
B) Because He wanted faith to be voluntary so they could deserve reward.
C) Because He does not will faith from them.
D) Because compulsion would be an injustice.

Key Takeaway
God's will for faith to be voluntary is the reason He does not compel, not any lack of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If Allah had compelled you to believe from birth, would your faith be as valuable to you? Right now, are you taking responsibility for the voluntary nature of your faith, or are you relying on circumstances to keep you obedient?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. Are you making excuses that Allah willed it, rather than facing your own choice to disobey? The lesson says God willed faith voluntarily—how does this truth demand that you stop blaming your environment and start taking ownership of your repentance?

Weakness in God: A Parallel Argument
Ash'ari presents a parallel argument: as not willing implies weakness, so not having power over a willed outcome implies weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari uses a parallel argument to show that denying God's power to compel implies weakness in God.

Core Idea
Denying God's power to compel entails weakness just as not willing something does.

Imam al-Ash'ari continues his refutation by drawing a parallel. If the opponents claim that God cannot compel disbelief, then they must accept that God is weak in achieving His will. This lesson unpacks that logical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage is a single argument in two parts.
2
Identify the two sides of the parallel
The argument compares two cases: not willing something without power to compel, and willing something without power to realize it in the desired way. Find each case.
The first case is hypothetical about not willing; the second is about willing in a particular way.
3
Understand the logical necessity
Imam al-Ash'ari uses 'yajibu' (it is necessary) to show that both cases result in weakness. Ask yourself why this is a necessary consequence.
The necessity comes from the opponent's own premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكما يجب بكون ما لا يريده الضعف والوهن والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده، لو لم يوصف بالقدرة على أن يُلجئهم إلى ذلك، فكذلك يجب له الضعف والوهن والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده، إذا أراد كونه على وجه لا يوصف بالقدرة على وقوعه على ذلك الوجه. وأيضاً فإنه يلزم القدرية - إذا كان من قدر أن يؤمن قدر أن يكفر - أن لا يكون الباري تعالى موصوفاً بالقدرة على الأمر، الذي لو فعله لكانوا مؤمنين لا محالة؛ لأنهم يقدرون - عندهم - على أن يكفروا، عند نزول الآيات الملجئات إلى الإيمان، كما يقدرون أن يؤمنوا قبل ذلك، ومن قدر على الكفر عند نزول الآية لم يؤمن وقوعه منه.
For just as it is necessary that not willing something is weakness, frailty, and failure to achieve what He wills, if He were not described as having power to compel them to that, so likewise, weakness, frailty, and failure to achieve what He wills must befall Him, if He wills it to be in a way such that He is not described as having power over its occurrence in that way. And also, it necessarily follows for the Qadariyya - if one who is able to believe is able to disbelieve - that the Creator - exalted - is not described as having power over the matter which, if He did it, they would inevitably be believers; because they are able - according to them - to disbelieve at the time of the compelling signs to faith, just as they are able to believe before that, and one who is able to disbelieve at the time of the sign cannot be sure that it will not occur from him.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage continues the author's refutation by presenting a parallel argument and then targeting the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) position. The author first argues by analogy: just as it is necessary (yajibu) that if God does not will something, and He is not described as having the power to compel them to it, that would be considered weakness (ḍaʿf) and deficiency, similarly, if God wills something to occur in a particular way (e.g., voluntary faith) and He is not described as having power to bring it about in that way (i.e., without compulsion), then that also would entail weakness. Since the opponents deny that God could compel, they are forced to admit that God cannot achieve faith in a voluntary manner, which is absurd. Then the author makes a specific point against the Qadariyya (who affirm human free will and deny God's creation of acts). If a person who is able to believe is also able to disbelieve, then even when God sends compelling signs (āyāt muljiʾāt) that would normally force belief, the person could still choose to disbelieve according to their own ability. This means God cannot guarantee that they will believe, and thus God would not be described as having power over the matter of making them inevitably believers. This is a reductio ad absurdum of the Mu'tazila position, showing it undermines God's omnipotence. The argument is structured as a conditional proof and a refutation of the Qadariyya's claim about human power.

Key Terms
ضعف
وهن
تقصير
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'ari merely asserts that weakness follows without logical proof.
Correction: Ash'ari's argument is a logical parallel: if the opponent's position is true, then weakness necessarily follows from their own premises.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari's argument, what follows if God wills something but is not described as having power over its occurrence in that way?
A) It is weakness, frailty, and failure.
B) It is not possible for God.
C) It is just and wise.
D) It shows God's compassion.

Key Takeaway
Denying God's power over the manner of events leads to attributing weakness to Him, just as not willing something without power does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God has power over the exact manner of every event, including the way you respond to Him, why do you still hold back from total trust? What area of your life are you treating as outside His control? How will you surrender that area to Him today?
?
When you disobey God, do you ever comfort yourself with the thought that He could have compelled you to obey but didn't? Does this argument challenge you to see your sin as a deeper rebellion against His will and power? How will you respond to His patience without compulsion?

Refuting the Qadariyya on Power to Disbelieve
Ash'ari refutes the Qadariyya by showing their view leads to denying God's power to ensure faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qadariyya position on human power to disbelieve undermines divine omnipotence, according to Imam al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
If a person can always disbelieve, God cannot guarantee faith even with compelling signs.

Imam al-Ash'ari targets the Qadariyya view that humans have independent power to choose faith or disbelief. He shows that this leads to a contradiction: God would not be able to ensure belief even when sending clear signs. This lesson explores that logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the second part of the Arabic passage and its translation. This argument is a reductio against the Qadariyya.
The Qadariyya are the Mu'tazila who affirm human free will.
2
Understand the Qadariyya premise
The premise: 'one who is able to believe is able to disbelieve.' This is the Qadariyya claim about human power.
This premise is accepted for the sake of argument.
3
Follow the reductio
If a person can disbelieve at the time of compelling signs (āyāt muljiʾāt), then God cannot be sure that belief will occur. Therefore, God is not described as having power over making them inevitably believers.
The compelling signs are miracles that would force belief.
4
Conclude the implication
The conclusion is that the Qadariyya position implies a limit on God's power, which is absurd for those who affirm divine omnipotence.

The Universality of God's Will: Al-Ash'ari's Argument
Chapter heading with proof, objection, and rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's argument that if God does not will all actions, weakness would be attributed to Him, and why the objection that human and divine actions differ is invalid.

Core Idea
If God does not will an action, that implies weakness—so He must will all things.

This passage from al-Luma marks the beginning of Al-Ash'ari's discussion on the divine will. He argues that God's will must encompass all originated things, including human actions. To reject this would lead to an absurdity: attributing weakness to God. Al-Ash'ari then presents an objection from those who claim that human actions not willed by the agent are due to forgetfulness or weakness, which does not apply to God, and he refutes it.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter heading and initial argument
Start by reading the chapter heading and the first conditional argument. Notice how Al-Ash'ari sets up the logical structure.
The heading announces that the will encompasses all originated things.
2
Identify the objector's attempt to differentiate
The objector claims that when a human does not will his own action, it is due to forgetfulness or weakness, but this does not apply to God's actions. Identify this distinction.
3
Follow Al-Ash'ari's rebuttal
Al-Ash'ari responds by saying the situation is the same: if an agent knows the action and it is not willed, weakness follows regardless. Trace his reasoning.
The rebuttal is cut off, but the logic is clear.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this argument establishes that God must will all actions. How does rejecting the distinction preserve God's perfection?
The full answer likely completes the parity argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائر المُحْدَثَاتِ وأيضًا فلو كان يقعُ من الإنسان ما لا يريده الباري - سُبحانَه - ولا يلحقُ الباري بذلك وهن ولا ضعف ؛ لأنه قادر أن يُلجِنَّهُمْ إِلَيْهِ ، لجازَ أن يقعَ مِنَ الباري - سُبحانَه مِنْ أفعاله ما لا يريده، ولا يلحقه ضعف وتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده ؛ لأنه قادر على إيقاعه وتكوينه. فإن لم يجب هذا، ولزم بكون ما لا يريده من فعله الضعفُ والوهن ، لزم ذلك في فعلِ عباده. فإن قال قائل : ما أنكرتُم أن يكونَ كونُ ما لا يريدُهُ الإِنسانُ مِنْ فعلِهِ، يُوجِبُ وقوع ذلك عن سهو أو عن ضعفٍ وَوَهَنِ، وليسَ يلزم في كون ما لا يريدُه مِنْ فِعْلِ غيره، فوجب مثلُ ذلك في القديم أيضًا؟ قيل له : ليسَ الأمر كما ظننت ، بل القِصَّةُ فيما يكون من الإنسانِ ومن غيرِهِ واحدةٌ، وذلك أنَّ الإنسان إذا كان من فعله ما لا يريده، فإما أن يكون ذلك عن سهو، أو عن ضعف ووهن و تقصير عن بلوغ ما يُريدُه . وكذلك القولُ فيما يكونُ مِن غيرِهِ؛ لأنه إِنْ لم يكن ذلك عن سهو، فواجب أن يكون عن ضعف وتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده . وذلك أن العلة التي لها لزِمَ الإنسانَ - إذا كان عالما بما وقع منه ، وهو غيرُ
Chapter on speech about the will and that it encompasses all originated things. And also, if it were that what the human does that the Exalted Creator does not will occurs, and no weakness or feebleness attaches to the Creator because of that, because He is powerful to compel them to it, then it would be permissible that from the Exalted Creator—from His actions—what He does not will occurs, and no weakness or falling short of attaining what He wills attaches to Him, because He is powerful to cause it and bring it into existence. If this is not necessary, and weakness and feebleness necessarily follow from the occurrence of what He does not will from His own action, then the same necessarily follows in the action of His servants. If someone says: What prevents you from saying that the occurrence of what a human does not will from his own action necessitates its occurrence due to forgetfulness or due to weakness and feebleness, and it is not necessary in the occurrence of what he does not will from the action of another, so the like of that is necessary for the Eternal as well? It is said to him: The matter is not as you thought; rather, the story concerning what occurs from a human and from what occurs from another is one and the same. That is because the human, if there occurs from his action what he does not will, then either that is due to forgetfulness, or due to weakness and feebleness and falling short of attaining what he wills. And the same is the statement concerning what occurs from another; because if that is not due to forgetfulness, then it must be due to weakness and falling short of attaining what he wills. And that is because the cause due to which it was necessary for the human—if he is knowledgeable of what has occurred from him, and he is not...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and a proof followed by an objection and answer. The chapter is on the will (al-irāda) and its universality over all originated things. Al-Ash'ari begins with a conditional argument: if human actions that God does not will occur, and no weakness attaches to God because He can compel them, then by the same logic, if God Himself performs actions that He does not will, no weakness would attach because He is powerful to bring them about. The objector (Qadari) tries to distinguish: human actions not willed by the agent often occur due to forgetfulness or weakness, but when the action is from another (i.e., God), that is not the case—forgetfulness and weakness are impossible for God. Al-Ash'ari's answer is that the situation is the same: in both cases, if the agent knows the action and it is not willed, it must be due to weakness. For the human, if not forgetfulness, then weakness. So for God, if an action is not willed, it implies weakness. Therefore, to avoid attributing weakness to God, we must affirm that He wills all actions that occur.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
سهو (forgetfulness)
ضعف (weakness)
وهن (feebleness)
علة (cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God can allow actions He does not will without any implication of weakness, because He has the power to prevent them.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that if an action occurs without God's will, it implies either forgetfulness (impossible for God) or weakness, because the agent cannot achieve what He wills. Thus, to preserve divine perfection, God must will all actions that occur.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ash'ari's rebuttal, what is the cause that forces weakness if an action occurs without the agent's will?
A) The action occurs due to forgetfulness or weakness
B) The action is caused by another agent
C) The action is inherently evil
D) The action is a test from God

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's argument for the universality of God's will is based on rejecting any distinction that would let human actions escape God's will without implying divine weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every action you take is willed by God, how would that change the way you view your own mistakes and shortcomings? Are there areas in your life where you act as if your choices are outside God's will, and what would it mean to surrender those to Him starting today?
?
Al-Ash'ari argues that any knowledge of an action occurring without one's will implies either forgetfulness or weakness. When you do something you know is wrong and later regret it, do you ever reflect on the fact that if God had not willed it, it would not have happened? How does that realization compel you to turn back to Him in repentance and trust, rather than despair?
?
Consider a recent situation where you felt that your own will conflicted with what happened. Instead of seeing it as a failure of your plans, could you see it as a manifestation of God's will overriding yours? What would it look like to actively embrace that and say, 'I accept Your decree, for You are never weak'?

The Universality of Divine Will: The Initial Argument
Ashari states the topic and presents a reductio argument for universal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Ashari's foundational argument that God's will must encompass all created events because any unwilled event would imply divine weakness.

Core Idea
If God did not will something, He would be weak—so all events must be willed.

This passage opens the chapter on divine will. Imam Ashari immediately asserts that God's will covers all created things. He then presents a powerful reductio argument: if a human could do something God does not will, and God is not weakened, then the same would apply to God's own actions; but if an unwilled act implies weakness in an agent, then it would apply to humans as well. This sets the stage for a rigorous defense of universal divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical flow: claim → conditional → reductio.
2
Identify the initial claim and the conditional argument
The passage begins by stating that divine will encompasses all created things. Then it proposes a conditional: if an unwilled human act does not weaken God, then an unwilled divine act also does not weaken God. Look for key terms like 'لجاز' and 'فإن لم يجب هذا'.
The argument uses symmetry between human and divine actions.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
If one denies the conditional and insists that an unwilled act implies weakness, then the same would apply to human acts. Why? Because humans also act, and if their unwilled acts imply weakness, then the same logic forces universal divine will. This refutes the idea that some events are outside God's will.
This is a classic kalam argument: assuming the opposite leads to an absurdity.
4
Reflect on the implications for divine attributes
Ashari connects will (irada) to power (qudra): if an event occurs against God's will, it would indicate inability to prevent it—a form of weakness. Since Allah is perfect, all events must be willed. Consider why this matters for tawhid.
This also defends the attribute of will as eternal and all-encompassing.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ وأيضًا فلو كان يقعُ من الإنسان ما لا يريده الباري - سُبحانَه - ولا يلحقُ الباري بذلك وهن ولا ضعف ؛ لأنه قادر أن يُلجِنَّهُمْ إِلَيْهِ ، لجازَ أن يقعَ مِنَ الباري - سُبحانَه مِنْ أفعاله ما لا يريده، ولا يلحقه ضعف وتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده ؛ لأنه قادر على إيقاعه وتكوينه. فإن لم يجب هذا، ولزم بكون ما لا يريده من فعله الضعفُ والوهن ، لزم ذلك في فعلِ عباده. فإن قال قائل : ما أنكرتُم أن يكونَ كونُ ما لا يريدُهُ الإِنسانُ مِنْ فعلِهِ، يُوجِبُ وقوع ذلك عن سهو أو عن ضعفٍ وَوَهَنِ، وليسَ يلزم في كون ما لا يريدُه مِنْ فِعْلِ غيره، فوجب مثلُ ذلك في القديم أيضًا؟ قيل له : ليسَ الأمر كما ظننت ، بل القِصَّةُ فيما يكون من الإنسانِ ومن غيرِهِ واحدةٌ، وذلك أنَّ الإنسان إذا كان من فعله ما لا يريده، فإما أن يكون ذلك عن سهو، أو عن ضعف ووهن و تقصير عن بلوغ ما يُريدُه . وكذلك القولُ فيما يكونُ مِن غيرِهِ؛ لأنه إِنْ لم يكن ذلك عن سهو، فواجب أن يكون عن ضعف وتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده . وذلك أن العلة التي لها لزِمَ الإنسانَ - إذا كان عالما بما وقع منه ، وهو غيرُ مُريد له - الضعفُ والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده : أن مراده لم يقع ، وأنه لم يُرِدْهُ ؛ لأنه لو كان ما يريده لم يلحقه ضعف ولا وهَنْ ، فإذا لم يقع ، فإنّما لَحِقَهُ الوهنُ والتقصيرُ عن بلوغ ما يريدُهُ ؛ من أَجْلِ أنه وقع منه وهو عالم به غيرُ مُريدٍ لَه . فإذا كانت العلةُ ما ذكرنا ، وجَبَ أيضًا مثلُ ذلك فيما يقعُ من غيره وهو لا يريده ؛ لأنه إذا كانت العلة التي لها وجب أن يوصف الإنسان بضدّ العلم - في وقوع ما يقع منه - أنه وقع مِنهُ وهو لا يعلَمُه ، فكذلك قصة ما يقع من غيره وهو لا يعلمه؛ إذ كانت العلة في ذلك واحدة، وكذلك القولُ في الإرادة. وأيضًا فإنه : إذا كان من غيره ما لا يريده، فقد كَرِهَهُ، وإذا كره كَوْنَهُ فقَدْ أبَاهُ ، وهذا يُوجِبُ أن الشيء كان، شاء أم أبى، وهذه صفة الضَّعفِ والوهن.
Chapter on the discourse concerning the will and that it encompasses all created things. And also, if what occurs from a human is what the Creator, exalted is He, does not will, and the Creator is not thereby affected by weakness or feebleness—because He is able to compel them to it—then it would be permissible for what occurs from the Creator, exalted is He, of His acts to be what He does not will, and no weakness or failure to attain what He wills would affect Him, because He is able to bring it about and originate it. If this is not necessary, and by virtue of something He does not will being from His act, weakness and feebleness necessarily follow, then that would necessarily follow in the act of His servants. If someone says: 'What prevents you from holding that the occurrence of what a human does not will from his act entails that it occurs by forgetfulness or by weakness and feebleness, but that this does not necessarily follow in the occurrence of what He does not will from the act of another, so the like of that would be necessary regarding the Eternal as well?' It is said to him: 'The matter is not as you supposed. Rather, the case regarding what occurs from a human and from other than him is one and the same. For if something occurs from a human that he does not will, then it is either by forgetfulness or by weakness, feebleness, and failure to attain what he wills. And similarly the statement regarding what occurs from other than him; because if it is not by forgetfulness, then it must be by weakness and failure to attain what he wills. That is because the cause for which the human—if he is knowledgeable of what occurred from him and he does not will it—is necessarily characterized by weakness and failure to attain what he wills is that his intended object did not occur and that he did not will it; for if what he willed had occurred, no weakness or feebleness would have affected him. So when it did not occur, he is affected by feebleness and failure to attain what he wills, due to the fact that it occurred from him while he knows it and does not will it. So if the cause is as we have mentioned, then the like of that is also necessary regarding what occurs from another and he does not will it; because if the cause for which it is necessary to describe the human by the opposite of knowledge—in the occurrence of what occurs from him—is that it occurred from him while he does not know it, then similarly is the case of what occurs from another while he does not know it, since the cause in that is one. And likewise the statement concerning the will. And also, if what he does not will occurs from another, then he has disliked it, and if he dislikes its occurrence, he has rejected it, and this entails that the thing existed, whether he wills or rejects, and this is a description of weakness and feebleness.'
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the scope of divine will over all created events. Imam Ashari begins by stating the topic: the will (al-irada) encompasses all created things (al-muhdathat). He then presents a conditional argument: if it were possible for a human to do something that God does not will, and yet God suffers no weakness—because He can compel them—then it would also be possible for God to do something He does not will from His own acts, without weakness. But if one insists that an act contrary to will necessarily implies weakness in the agent, then the same would apply to God's servants (i.e., humans). This is a reductio ad absurdum aiming to show that all events must be willed by God.

An objector then raises a distinction: when a human does something he does not will, it is due to forgetfulness or weakness; but when another agent (God) does something He does not will, the same may not follow. Ashari replies that the situation is identical. He analyzes the cause: if a knowledgeable agent fails to bring about what he wills, weakness is implied. Conversely, if something occurs from him that he knows and does not will, that also indicates weakness because his will was not fulfilled. This cause is general: it applies whether the agent is human or divine. Therefore, for God, if something happened that He did not will, it would imply weakness, which is impossible. Hence, all events must be subject to His will.

He strengthens the argument by noting that disliking (karaha) an event is a form of rejection (aba'), and if something occurs despite that, it shows powerlessness—again impossible for God. The passage concludes that the description of weakness and feebleness would apply to anyone who fails to prevent an unwilled occurrence. Thus, God's will is universal.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المُحْدَثَات
ضعف
وهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that God can allow some events to occur without willing them, as long as He is able to prevent them.
Correction: Ashari argues that if an unwilled event occurs despite God's ability to prevent it, it still implies a failure to actualize His will, which is a form of weakness incompatible with divine perfection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the occurrence of an unwilled event imply about the agent?
A) It implies the agent is all-powerful.
B) It implies weakness or failure to achieve what the agent wills.
C) It implies the agent is indifferent.
D) It implies the agent is unaware.

Key Takeaway
All events must be willed by God because an unwilled event would imply a deficiency in divine power or will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something undesirable happens to you, do you genuinely accept that it was willed by God, or do you internally resist, thinking it should not have happened? How does that resistance reveal a hidden belief about the limits of God's will?
?
The argument shows that any event outside God's will would imply weakness in Him. Examine your own life: do you act as if some events are outside His control? What would change in your reactions if you fully internalised that everything is willed by Him?
?
If you truly believed that even your own mistakes are willed by God, how would your sense of guilt and your reliance on His mercy be transformed? Are you willing to submit to that belief today?

Defending Universality: Objection and Response
Ashari refutes an objection by identifying the universal cause of weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ashari refutes the objection that unwilled human acts result from forgetfulness or weakness, but the same does not apply to God.

Core Idea
The same cause—failure to achieve one's will—implies weakness in any agent, divine or human.

After setting out his initial argument, Imam Ashari anticipates an objection: perhaps when humans do something they do not will, it is due to forgetfulness or weakness, but God is not subject to such limitations. In this passage, Ashari dismantles the distinction by analyzing the underlying cause. He shows that if a knowledgeable agent fails to fulfill his will, that is weakness—regardless of whether the agent is human or divine. Thus, the objection collapses, and the universality of divine will stands.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector argues that unwilled human acts are due to forgetfulness or weakness, but this does not apply to God because the unwilled act is from another (i.e., from humans). Find the exact wording: 'ما أنكرتُم أن يكونَ كونُ ما لا يريدُهُ الإِنسانُ مِنْ فعلِهِ، يُوجِبُ وقوع ذلك عن سهو أو عن ضعفٍ وَوَهَنِ، وليسَ يلزم في كون ما لا يريدُه مِنْ فِعْلِ غيره'.
The objector tries to differentiate between acts of the self and acts of others.
2
Identify Ashari's initial reply
Ashari flatly rejects the distinction: 'ليس الأمر كما ظننت بل القصة فيما يكون من الإنسان ومن غيره واحدة'. He asserts that the case is identical. Then he explains why: if an unwilled act occurs from oneself, it's either by forgetfulness or weakness; if from another, the same cause (weakness) applies if not forgetfulness.
Notice the use of 'فإما أن يكون' for enumeration of possibilities.
3
Analyze the cause (illa)
Ashari identifies the underlying cause: when a knowledgeable agent does not will something that occurs, it is because his intended object did not occur. This indicates he could not achieve his will—which is weakness. This cause is universal: it applies whether the agent is human or divine. Trace the reasoning: 'العلة التي لها لزم الإنسان...'
The cause is not forgetfulness or the source of the act, but the mismatch between will and occurrence.
4
Extend the argument to God
If the cause is universal, then the same judgment applies to God: if something occurs that He does not will, He would be weak. Since that is impossible, all events must be willed by Him. The passage also adds that if God dislikes (karaha) something, it is a form of rejection (aba'), and if it still occurs, that is a sign of powerlessness.
This reinforces the earlier conclusion that God's will is all-encompassing.

Commentary

He then presents a conditional argument: if it were possible for a human to do something that God does not will, and yet God suffers no weakness—because He can compel them—then it would also be possible for God to do something He does not will from His own acts, without weakness. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That human unwilled acts are due to forgetfulness, so the same logic does not apply to God.
Correction: Ashari shows that even if forgetfulness is excluded, the underlying cause—failure to achieve one's will—applies universally, so God cannot have unwilled events.

Checkpoint
In Ashari's response, what is the ultimate cause (illa) that necessitates weakness when an unwilled event occurs?
A) The agent is forgetful.
B) The agent's intended object did not occur.
C) The act is from another person.
D) The agent is not all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
Any unwilled event in a knowledgeable agent implies weakness because his will is not fulfilled; thus, God must will all events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something happens in your life that you strongly dislike, do you ever feel that Allah must not have willed it? Based on this lesson, what does that feeling imply about your view of Allah's power, and how can you train your heart to accept that His will is always effective?
?
If Allah's will is truly all-encompassing, then every hardship, loss, or trial you face is willed by Him. Are you willing to stop seeing any event as a 'mistake' or something that slipped past Allah? What would it cost you to hold that belief in your most painful moments?
?
Consider a time you felt weak because you could not achieve a goal despite trying. Now apply the source's logic: could it be that your weakness was precisely because Allah did not will it for you? How does recognizing that your will is subject to His change how you view your own efforts and failures?

Divine Attributes

Weakness Necessitated by Occurrence of What is Not Willed
Ash'ari argues that whatever occurs without one's will implies weakness, and answers the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari argues that anything occurring contrary to one's will implies weakness, and how he defends divine omnipotence through the answer to the objection.

Core Idea
If something occurs against one's will, that person is weak — and God cannot be weak.

Al-Ash'ari presents a clear analogy: just as ignorance is shown by events occurring without knowledge, weakness is shown by events occurring without will. He then faces an objection that perhaps God merely hates sin without being weak. His answer is decisive: if an act occurs despite opposition, the one opposed is shown to be weak. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the repetition of key terms like إرادة and كراهة.
2
Identify the core analogy
Ash'ari compares will to knowledge. Just as an event occurring without knowledge proves ignorance, an event occurring without will proves weakness. Find this analogy in the text.
The phrase 'كذلك القول في الإرادة' marks the final step of the analogy.
3
Examine the objection and answer
The opponent says God may hate an act without being weak. Ash'ari replies that hatred implies opposition, and if the act occurs despite opposition, it shows weakness. Understand this refutation.
The objection starts with 'فإن قالوا' and the answer with 'قيل لهم'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مريد له - الضعف والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده: أن مراده لم يقع، وأنه لم يرده؛ لأنه لو كان ما يريده لم يلحقه ضعف ولا وهن، فإذا لم يقع، فإنما لحقه الوهن والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده؛ من أجله أنه وقع منه وهو عالم به غير مريد له. فإذا كانت العلة ما ذكرنا، وجب أيضًا مثل ذلك فيما يقع من غيره وهو لا يريده؛ لأنه إذا كانت العلة التي لها وجب أن يوصف الإنسان بضد العلم - في وقوع ما يقع منه - أنه وقع منه وهو لا يعلمه، فكذلك قصة ما يقع من غيره وهو لا يعلمه؛ إذ كانت العلة في ذلك واحدة، وكذلك القول في الإرادة. وأيضًا فإنه: إذا كان من غيره ما لا يريده، فقد كرهه، وإذا كره كونه فقد أباه، وهذا يوجب أن الشيء كان شاء أم أبى، وهذه صفة الضعف والوهن. فإن قالوا: ما أنكرتم من أن الذي يجب في كون ما لا يريده الباري -تعالى- من عباده، أن يكون كارها لذلك فقط؛ ولا يجب في ذلك ضعف ولا وهن؟ قيل لهم: بل وقوع ذلك منهم، وهو كاره له، يوجب الضعف والوهن لا محالة؛ لأنه إذا كان ما كره كونه كان ما أبى كونه، وإذا كان ما أبى كونه، فقد كان الشيء شاءه أم أباه! وهذا يوجب أن الشيء كان شاء الله -تعالى- ذلك أم أباه، وهذه صفة الضعيف.
One who wills something - weakness and incapacity to attain what he wills: that his intention did not occur, and that he did not will it; because if what he willed had occurred, no weakness or feebleness would have befallen him. When it did not occur, feebleness and incapacity to attain what he wills befell him only because it occurred from him while he was knowing of it but not willing it. So if the cause is what we mentioned, the like of that is also necessary regarding what occurs from another and he does not will it; because if the cause on account of which a human is described as contrary to knowledge - in the occurrence of what occurs from him - is that it occurred from him while he does not know it, then likewise is the case of what occurs from another and he does not know it, since the cause in that is one. And likewise is the statement concerning will. And also: if there is from another what he does not will, then he has hated it, and if he hates its existence then he has opposed it. This necessitates that the thing occurred, whether he wills or refuses, and this is the attribute of weakness and feebleness. Then if they say: what do you deny that what is necessary regarding the occurrence of what the Exalted God does not will from His servants is that He hates that only, and no weakness or feebleness is necessary in that? It is said to them: rather, the occurrence of that from them while He hates it necessitates weakness and feebleness unavoidably; because if what He hates to exist exists, then what He opposes exists. And if what He opposes exists, then the thing occurred whether He wills it or opposes it! This necessitates that the thing occurred whether God - Exalted - wills that or opposes it, and this is the attribute of the weak.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument followed by an objection and answer. The author, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, first establishes a principle: if a person intends something but it does not occur, and he is aware of it but does not will it, this indicates weakness and incapacity. He then extends this by analogy to will: just as ignorance is shown when something occurs without knowledge, likewise feebleness is shown when something occurs without will. He adds that if something occurs that one does not will, one hates and opposes it, yet it still occurs; this implies the thing occurs despite opposition, which is a sign of weakness. The opponent objects: perhaps the only necessary implication is that God hates the act, not that He is weak. The author answers: hatred and opposition are the same; if the act occurs despite opposition, it shows the one opposing it is weak, because his opposition was not effective. The argument concludes that if something occurs even though God opposes it, that is a trait of the weak, which cannot be attributed to God. The author thus defends God's omnipotence by arguing that everything that occurs does so by God's will; if something occurred against His will, that would imply weakness, which is impossible.

Key Terms
إرادة
كراهة
ضعف
وهن
مريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for God to hate an act that occurs without implying weakness.
Correction: Ash'ari argues that if the act occurs despite God's hatred, it implies weakness because the hatred constitutes opposition, and the occurrence shows opposition is ineffective.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari, what does it imply if something occurs from someone while he is unwilling and aware of it?
A) It implies the person has perfect power.
B) It implies the person is weak and incapable.
C) It implies the person is unaware.
D) It implies the person is indifferent.

Key Takeaway
God's will encompasses all events; any occurrence against His will would imply weakness, which is impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage proves that if anything occurred against God's will, He would be weak. Do you truly live as if nothing happens outside His will, or do you secretly believe some events are beyond His control? What would change in your heart if you fully internalized this truth?
?
When you experience something painful or unjust, your soul may protest, 'This should not have happened.' This lesson says that such a thought implicitly accuses God of weakness. Can you repent from that hidden accusation and instead say, 'This happened by Your will, and Your will is perfect'? How would that reshape your patience?
?
You know that every sin you commit occurs by God's will (as this passage argues). Yet you are still responsible. Does this make you feel trapped or free? How does the truth that God wills even your failures drive you to depend on His mercy rather than your own strength?
