Two Mu'tazilite Views on God's Will and Their Implications
Ash'ari identifies two Mu'tazilite positions on God's will and their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazilite positions on God's will regarding human actions and the problematic implication of equating will with command.

Core Idea
Equating God's will with His command leads to attributing hatred to Him for uncommanded actions.

Ash'ari presents two Mu'tazilite views on God's will in human actions. One view identifies God's will with His command; the other distinguishes them. He then shows that the first view leads to an unacceptable consequence: God would hate actions like those of children and the insane, which He does not command. This argument is used to refute the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice that Ash'ari directly introduces two groups within the Mu'tazila.
The passage is concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the two views
Find the two positions: one says God's will is command; the other says it is different from command. Note the Arabic terms: 'الأمر بها' and 'خلف غير الأمر بها'.
'خلف' here means 'different from'.
3
Analyze the implication
Ash'ari points out that if will equals command, then for actions God does not command (e.g., children's actions), He has no will. But absence of will necessitates hatred (كراهة), which is inappropriate for God. Ask yourself why this is a problem for the Mu'tazilite view.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا فإن المعتزلة رجلان أحدهما يقول: إن إرادة الله تعالى - في أفعال عباده الأمر بها. والآخر يقول: إرادته في أفعال عباده خلف غير الأمر بها. فمن ذهب إلى أنها الأمر، لزمه - إذا لم يكن الباري آمرًا بأفعال الأطفال والمجانين - أن يكون كارها لها؛ إذ كان يجب بنفي الإرادة لأفعال العباد الكراهة لها، والله -تعالى- لا يكره.
And also, the Mu'tazila are two men: one of them says that God's will - Exalted - regarding the actions of His servants is command to them. And the other says that His will regarding the actions of His servants is different from (khalaf), not command to them. So whoever holds that it is command, it follows for him - if the Creator is not commanding the actions of children and the insane - that He hates them; since the negation of will for the actions of servants necessitates hatred for them, and God - Exalted - does not hate.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a brief classification of the Mu'tazilite positions on God's will concerning human actions. The author identifies two main views: (1) Some Mu'tazila equate God's will with His command; that is, what God wills for His servants is what He commands them. (2) Others say God's will is different from (خلف) His command; it is something else. The author then points out an implication of the first view: if God's will is identical to command, then for actions He does not command—such as those of children and the insane—He would have no will (since He does not command them). The negation of will necessitates hatred, so God would hate those actions. But the author suggests that this leads to an undesirable consequence, as God does not hate (in the sense of weak opposition). The argument is that this view leads to attributing hatred to God for actions that occur, which would imply weakness as argued earlier.

Key Terms
إرادة
أمر
خلف
كراهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God does not will an action, He must hate it.
Correction: Ash'ari uses this as a consequence of the first Mu'tazilite view to show its absurdity, but it does not mean that God necessarily hates uncommanded actions in reality—the point is that the equation of will and command forces this unacceptable conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is the first Mu'tazilite view on God's will mentioned by Ash'ari?
A) God's will is identical to His command.
B) God's will is different from His command.
C) God's will is both command and prohibition.
D) God's will is irrelevant to human actions.

Key Takeaway
The first Mu'tazilite view equating God's will with His command leads to the problematic conclusion that God would hate actions He does not command, which is contrary to His perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions that are not explicitly commanded by God—like unconscious habits or unintentional mistakes—do you ever assume God hates them? This lesson shows that equating will with command leads to that false conclusion. How does separating God's will from His command change how you view those actions and your relationship with Him? Will you trust that His will for you is broader than just what He commands?
?
This passage argues that God does not hate the actions of children and the insane, even though He does not command them. Think of a time when you saw something happen that seemed contrary to God's commands—perhaps suffering or sin. Did you struggle with the idea that God allows it? How does the Ash'ari position that God's will is not limited to His commands help you accept that He has a purpose in all that He wills, even when it is not commanded? Can you truly submit to that?
?
If you had to choose between the two Mu'tazilite views, which one would you lean towards before reading this? The logical consequence of the first view is that God would hate uncommanded actions. Are you willing to hold a view about God that entails something unworthy of Him? This lesson challenges you to ensure your understanding of God's attributes is logically consistent and rooted in revelation. How will you guard your heart from adopting flawed theological notions?

The Implication of Weakness in God's Will
Al-Ash'ari presents an argument that God cannot hate an event that occurs without implying weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Ash'ari argues that if God hates an action yet it occurs, it would imply weakness in God.

Core Idea
A cause that fails to prevent its contrary is a weak cause.

Al-Ash'ari begins his refutation by exposing a logical flaw in the Mu'tazilite position. If God hates a human action, yet it still takes place, then God's will is not effective—this is a sign of weakness. This lesson explores that initial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the logical conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
The argument is compact; pay attention to the chain of implications.
2
Identify the key implication
Al-Ash'ari argues that if God hates an event and it still occurs, then the event happens regardless of His will. Why does this imply weakness?
Think about what it means for a being to be truly powerful.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that such a situation would be 'the attribute of the weak.' Ask yourself if this applies to God.
This argument sets the stage for refuting specific views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ
قيل لهم: بل وقوع ذلك منهم، وهو كاره له، يُوجِبُ الضعف والوهن لا محالة؛ لأنه إذا كان ما كَرِهَ كونَهُ كان ما أبَى كونه، وإذا كان ما أبَى كونه، فقد كان الشيء شاءه أم أباه! وهذا يوجب أن الشيء كان شاء الله -تعالى- ذلك أم أباه، وهذه صفة الضعيف.

وأيضًا فإن المعتزلة رجلان: أحدهما يقول: إن إرادة الله تعالى في أفعال عباده الأمر بها. والآخر يقول: إرادته في أفعال عباده خلف غير الأمر بها.

فمن ذهب إلى أنها الأمر، لزمه - إذا لم يكن الباري آمرًا بأفعال الأطفال والمجانين - أن يكون كارها لها؛ إذ كان يجب بنفي الإرادة لأفعال العباد الكراهة لها، والله -تعالى- لا يكره إلا معصية كما لا ينهى إلا عن معصية، وإذا لم يكن هذا عندهم هكذا أبطل ما قالوه.

وأيضًا فإذا كان يلزم فيما جاز الأمر به إذا لم يأمر به أن يكون له كارها، لزم من كان في عصر الرسول -صلى الله عليه وسلم- وجاز عنده أن يترك النهي عن المباح الذي ليس بطاعة، أن يكون إذا لم يرد من الله -تعالى- الأمر به فقد كرهه، وهذا يوجب أن كل مباح معصية.

ومن ذهب إلى القول الثاني - وهو قول الجبائي -: إن إرادة الله -تعالى- لأفعال عباده هي غير الأمر بها، يقال له: إذا كان يجب بنفي الإرادة لأفعال عباده الكراهة، فحدثنا: هل أراد الله كون الأفعال التي ليست بمعاصي ولا طاعات؟
Chapter on speech concerning the will and that it encompasses all originated things.

It was said to them: Rather, the occurrence of that from them, while He hates it, necessarily entails weakness and feebleness; because if what He hates to be happens, then what He refuses to be happens, and if what He refuses to be happens, then the thing occurs whether He wills it or abhors it! And this necessitates that the thing occurs whether God - exalted - wills that or abhors it, and this is the attribute of the weak.

Also, the Mu'tazila are two men: one of them says that God's will - exalted - in the acts of His servants is the command to them. The other says that His will in the acts of His servants is something else (khulf) other than the command to them.

So whoever holds that it is the command, it is necessary for him - if the Creator is not commanding the acts of children and the insane - that He hates them, since it follows from the negation of will for the acts of servants the hatred of them, and God - exalted - does not hate except disobedience just as He does not forbid except disobedience, and if this is not so according to them, then what they said is invalidated.

Also, if it is necessary that in what is permissible to command, if He does not command it, that He hates it, then it is necessary for one who was in the era of the Messenger - peace be upon him - and it was permissible in his view to abandon forbidding what is permissible that is not obedience, that if God - exalted - did not intend the command to it, then He hated it, and this necessitates that every permissible thing is disobedience.

And whoever holds the second view - which is the view of al-Jubba'i - that God's will - exalted - for the acts of His servants is other than the command to them, it is said to him: If it is necessary that with the negation of will for the acts of servants there is hatred, then tell us: Did God will the occurrence of acts that are neither disobediences nor acts of obedience?
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Mu'tazilite doctrines regarding divine will (iraada) and its relation to human actions. The passage begins with a chapter heading stating that the will encompasses all originated things (muhdathaat).

The author first addresses an argument: if it is claimed that God hates an action yet it occurs, that would imply weakness. The author argues that if what God hates occurs, then what He refuses also occurs, meaning the action happens regardless of His will or aversion—this is a sign of weakness, not strength.

Then the author identifies two positions among the Mu'tazila: (1) God's will concerning human acts is identical to His command (amr), and (2) His will is something other than command (khulf), as held by al-Jubba'i.

Refuting the first view: if will equals command, then since God does not command the acts of children and the insane (who are not morally accountable), He must hate those acts. But God only hates sin (ma'siya), and these acts are not sinful. Moreover, God only forbids sin. If the Mu'tazila do not accept this consequence, their position collapses.

Next, the author extends the argument: if God does not command a permissible (mubaah) act (which is not obedience), it would follow that He hates it, making all permissible acts sins—an absurd conclusion.

Finally, the author turns to the second view (al-Jubba'i). The question is posed: if negation of will necessitates hatred, then does God will the occurrence of acts that are neither sins nor acts of obedience (i.e., permissible acts)? The passage ends with this open question, leaving the reader to consider the implications. The argument implies that al-Jubba'i's view also leads to difficulty, as the answer must be consistent with the principle that negation of will implies hatred.

Key Terms
إرادة
كراهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can hate an action without it affecting His power.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that if God hates an action and it still occurs, then His will is ineffective, implying weakness.

Checkpoint
What does the occurrence of an action despite God's hatred imply according to Al-Ash'ari?
A) God's mercy
B) Weakness
C) God's command
D) Free will

Key Takeaway
If an event occurs despite God's hatred, then God's will is not sovereign, implying weakness—an impossibility for the Almighty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin against God, do you truly believe that He hates that sin, yet it still happens? What does this lesson force you to admit about your own view of God's sovereignty? Will you now humble yourself and acknowledge that every action occurs only by His permission, turning to Him in repentance?
?
This lesson argues that if God's will were overridden, He would be weak. Do you live as if your own desires are stronger than God's decree? In what specific habit or attitude do you need to submit to His absolute will today?
?
Consider the weight of this truth: nothing occurs against God's will. How does that change your perspective on the trials you face? Will you now accept them as from His wise will, or will you continue to resent what He has decreed?

Refuting the Identification of Will with Command
Al-Ash'ari refutes the first Mu'tazilite view that divine will is identical to command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why identifying God's will with His command leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
If God's will is merely His command, then acts not commanded become hated—making permissible acts sins.

The second part of Al-Ash'ari's refutation targets the Mu'tazilite view that God's will for human acts is nothing other than His command. This lesson unpacks the logical consequences for children, the insane, and permissible acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two positions
The passage begins by stating two Mu'tazilite views. Identify them before focusing on the first.
The first view is that will equals command.
2
Follow the argument against the first view
Al-Ash'ari says: if will equals command, then God must hate acts He does not command. Since He does not command acts of children and the insane, He would hate them. But God only hates sin, so these acts become sins—an absurdity.
Notice the use of 'if... then...' logic.
3
Understand the extension to permissible acts
The argument is extended: if God does not command a permissible act, He would hate it, making that permissible act a sin. This is clearly false.
This shows the view leads to a contradiction.

?

?

Refuting the View that Will is Other than Command
Al-Ash'ari questions al-Jubba'i's view by applying the same principle of hatred.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed to al-Jubba'i's view that God's will is distinct from His command.

Core Idea
If will is not command, then the same principle—negation of will implies hatred—raises a difficult question about permissible acts.

The last part of the passage engages the second Mu'tazilite position, associated with al-Jubba'i. Al-Ash'ari poses a critical question that exposes an inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the second view
Al-Ash'ari states the view: God's will for human acts is something other than command.
This is the view of al-Jubba'i.
2
Note the principle applied
The same principle from earlier is used: negation of will implies hatred.
This principle is assumed by both sides.
3
Understand the dilemma
Al-Ash'ari asks: Does God will acts that are neither sins nor obedience (i.e., permissible acts)? If yes, then the principle fails (since these acts are not hated). If no, then God hates them, making them sins. Either way, the view is inconsistent.
Think about how this question traps the opponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ
قيل لهم: بل وقوع ذلك منهم، وهو كاره له، يُوجِبُ الضعف والوهن لا محالة؛ لأنه إذا كان ما كَرِهَ كونَهُ كان ما أبَى كونه، وإذا كان ما أبَى كونه، فقد كان الشيء شاءه أم أباه! وهذا يوجب أن الشيء كان شاء الله -تعالى- ذلك أم أباه، وهذه صفة الضعيف.

وأيضًا فإن المعتزلة رجلان: أحدهما يقول: إن إرادة الله تعالى في أفعال عباده الأمر بها. والآخر يقول: إرادته في أفعال عباده خلف غير الأمر بها.

فمن ذهب إلى أنها الأمر، لزمه - إذا لم يكن الباري آمرًا بأفعال الأطفال والمجانين - أن يكون كارها لها؛ إذ كان يجب بنفي الإرادة لأفعال العباد الكراهة لها، والله -تعالى- لا يكره إلا معصية كما لا ينهى إلا عن معصية، وإذا لم يكن هذا عندهم هكذا أبطل ما قالوه.

وأيضًا فإذا كان يلزم فيما جاز الأمر به إذا لم يأمر به أن يكون له كارها، لزم من كان في عصر الرسول -صلى الله عليه وسلم- وجاز عنده أن يترك النهي عن المباح الذي ليس بطاعة، أن يكون إذا لم يرد من الله -تعالى- الأمر به فقد كرهه، وهذا يوجب أن كل مباح معصية.

ومن ذهب إلى القول الثاني - وهو قول الجبائي -: إن إرادة الله -تعالى- لأفعال عباده هي غير الأمر بها، يقال له: إذا كان يجب بنفي الإرادة لأفعال عباده الكراهة، فحدثنا: هل أراد الله كون الأفعال التي ليست بمعاصي ولا طاعات؟
Chapter on speech concerning the will and that it encompasses all originated things.

It was said to them: Rather, the occurrence of that from them, while He hates it, necessarily entails weakness and feebleness; because if what He hates to be happens, then what He refuses to be happens, and if what He refuses to be happens, then the thing occurs whether He wills it or abhors it! And this necessitates that the thing occurs whether God - exalted - wills that or abhors it, and this is the attribute of the weak.

Also, the Mu'tazila are two men: one of them says that God's will - exalted - in the acts of His servants is the command to them. The other says that His will in the acts of His servants is something else (khulf) other than the command to them.

So whoever holds that it is the command, it is necessary for him - if the Creator is not commanding the acts of children and the insane - that He hates them, since it follows from the negation of will for the acts of servants the hatred of them, and God - exalted - does not hate except disobedience just as He does not forbid except disobedience, and if this is not so according to them, then what they said is invalidated.

Also, if it is necessary that in what is permissible to command, if He does not command it, that He hates it, then it is necessary for one who was in the era of the Messenger - peace be upon him - and it was permissible in his view to abandon forbidding what is permissible that is not obedience, that if God - exalted - did not intend the command to it, then He hated it, and this necessitates that every permissible thing is disobedience.

And whoever holds the second view - which is the view of al-Jubba'i - that God's will - exalted - for the acts of His servants is other than the command to them, it is said to him: If it is necessary that with the negation of will for the acts of servants there is hatred, then tell us: Did God will the occurrence of acts that are neither disobediences nor acts of obedience?
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Mu'tazilite doctrines regarding divine will (iraada) and its relation to human actions. The passage begins with a chapter heading stating that the will encompasses all originated things (muhdathaat).

The author first addresses an argument: if it is claimed that God hates an action yet it occurs, that would imply weakness. The author argues that if what God hates occurs, then what He refuses also occurs, meaning the action happens regardless of His will or aversion—this is a sign of weakness, not strength.

Then the author identifies two positions among the Mu'tazila: (1) God's will concerning human acts is identical to His command (amr), and (2) His will is something other than command (khulf), as held by al-Jubba'i.

Refuting the first view: if will equals command, then since God does not command the acts of children and the insane (who are not morally accountable), He must hate those acts. But God only hates sin (ma'siya), and these acts are not sinful. Moreover, God only forbids sin. If the Mu'tazila do not accept this consequence, their position collapses.

Next, the author extends the argument: if God does not command a permissible (mubaah) act (which is not obedience), it would follow that He hates it, making all permissible acts sins—an absurd conclusion.

Finally, the author turns to the second view (al-Jubba'i). The question is posed: if negation of will necessitates hatred, then does God will the occurrence of acts that are neither sins nor acts of obedience (i.e., permissible acts)? The passage ends with this open question, leaving the reader to consider the implications. The argument implies that al-Jubba'i's view also leads to difficulty, as the answer must be consistent with the principle that negation of will implies hatred.

Key Terms
إرادة
خلف
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Jubba'i's view avoids the problems of the first view.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that his view also leads to a dilemma about permissible acts.

Checkpoint
What question does al-Ash'ari ask the proponent of the second view?
A) Does God will acts that are neither sins nor obedience?
B) Does God hate permissible acts?
C) Is will equal to command?
D) Are children's acts forbidden?

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's view that will is other than command still faces the problem of explaining God's attitude toward acts that are neither obedience nor disobedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform actions that seem neutral—neither clearly obedient nor sinful—do you consider whether God wills them or not? How does this lesson challenge you to view even your 'neutral' moments as falling under divine will?
?
Are there areas of your life where you assume God's command and will are the same, and others where you separate them? What would it mean for your accountability if they were distinct?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly subtle theological distinction leads to logical inconsistency. Where in your own faith do you hold inconsistent positions that you have not examined? Will you ask God to show them to you today?

The Absurdity of Equating Non-Command with Aversion
Al-Ash'ari's reductio against the Mu'tazilite view on non-command and aversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's reductio ad absurdum argument against the Mu'tazilite claim that God's not commanding something implies His aversion to it.

Core Idea
If not commanding meant aversion, then every permissible act would be a sin.

Al-Ash'ari here deploys a powerful logical argument to expose the absurd consequences of the Mu'tazilite principle linking non-command to divine aversion. By applying their own premise to a concrete example from the time of the Prophet, he shows that it would force the conclusion that all permissible acts are sins. This passage demonstrates the use of reductio ad absurdum in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
2
Identify the premise
The Mu'tazilite premise: if God does not command something, He is averse to it. Locate this in the text.
The phrase 'إذا لم يأمر به أن يكون له كارها' expresses this.
3
See the absurd conclusion
Applying the premise to permissible acts leads to the conclusion that every permissible act is a sin. Ask yourself why this is absurd.
This is the core of the reductio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا فإذا كان يلزم فيما جاز الأمر به إذا لم يأمر به أن يكون له كارها، لزم من كان في عصر الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وجاز عنده أن يترك النهي عن المباح الذي ليس بطاعة، أن يكون إذا لم يرد من الله تعالى الأمر به فقد كرهه، وهذا يوجب أن كل مباح معصية.
And also, if it is necessary that concerning what it is permissible to command, if He does not command it, that He is averse to it, then it is necessary for one who was in the era of the Messenger, peace be upon him, and it was permissible in his view to refrain from forbidding the permissible that is not an act of obedience, that if God Almighty does not will the command of it, He is indeed averse to it, and this necessitates that every permissible thing is a sin.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against the Mu'tazilite position that God's not commanding something implies His aversion to it. Al-Ash'ari first states the general principle: if it is necessary that when something is permissible to command but God does not command it, He is averse to it, then apply this to the time of the Prophet. At that time, it was permissible for the Prophet to refrain from forbidding permissible acts that are not acts of obedience. According to the Mu'tazilite principle, if God does not command such permissible acts (i.e., does not will the command of them), then He is averse to them. But aversion to something, in their view, makes it a sin. Therefore, every permissible act would be a sin, which is absurd. Thus, the Mu'tazilite principle is false.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
كراهة (aversion)
معصية (sin/disobedience)
مباح (permissible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Ash'ari is claiming that God actually has aversion to permissible acts.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari is not asserting that God is averse to permissible acts; he is showing that if the Mu'tazilite principle were true, then every permissible act would be a sin, which is absurd. So the principle must be false.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari's argument, what would follow from the Mu'tazilite view that not commanding implies aversion?
A) That permissible acts become obligatory.
B) That all permissible acts become sins.
C) That God never commands anything.
D) That the Prophet forbade all permissible acts.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazilite view by showing it leads to the absurd conclusion that all permissible acts are sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because God has not explicitly commanded something in the Quran or Sunnah, He must dislike it or it is sinful? How does al-Ash'ari's argument expose the danger of that assumption for your own life? Commit to not labeling permissible actions as sinful without clear evidence.
?
If you find yourself judging others' permissible actions as wrong because you think God 'would not want' them, what does this lesson reveal about the nature of such judgments? How will you change your attitude towards permissible differences among Muslims starting today?
?
The Mu'tazilite view logically leads to absurdity. But in your own heart, do you sometimes feel that God's silence on an issue means He is displeased? How can you train yourself to distinguish between your feelings and God's actual commands, especially when it comes to matters of personal preference?

The Dilemma of Neutral Actions
Al-Ash'ari questions al-Jubba'i about God's will for neutral actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses a dilemma to expose an inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite view of divine will and neutral actions.

Core Idea
Neutral actions cannot stay neutral under the Mu'tazilite view of God's will.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari engages the leading Mu'tazilite theologian al-Jubba'i in a crisp logical exchange. He poses a simple question about actions that are neither sins nor acts of obedience. Depending on the answer, the action must become either obedience or sin, revealing a flaw in the Mu'tazilite framework.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
Al-Ash'ari first states al-Jubba'i's view: God's will for human actions is distinct from the actions themselves. This means God does not directly will every human act.
This is the background needed for the dilemma.
2
Read the dilemma question
Al-Ash'ari asks: Did God will the occurrence of actions that are neither sins nor obedience? This is the hinge of the argument.
3
Follow the two horns
Trace both possible answers: "Yes" makes it obedience, "No" makes it sin. Neither leaves the action neutral.
Notice how al-Ash'ari uses al-Jubba'i's own definitions to force the contradiction.
4
Grasp the force of the argument
The dilemma shows that the Mu'tazilite framework cannot accommodate neutral actions, undermining their theology.
This is a classic example of dialectical reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذهب إلى القول الثاني - وهو قول الجبائي - : إن إرادة الله تعالى لأفعال عباده هي غير، يقال له: إذا كان يجب بنفي الإرادة لأفعال عباده الكراهة، فحدثنا: هل أراد الله كون الأفعال التي ليست بمعاصي ولا طاعات؟

بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ

فإن قال: نعم.
قيل له: فلزمك أن يكون طاعة؛ لأن الطاعة عندك إنما كانت طاعة للمطاع لأنه أرادها.
فإن قال: لم يردها.
قيل له: فيلزمك أنه كاره لكونها، وهذا يوجب أن تكون معصية؛ لأن ما كرهه الله سبحانه فهو معصية، كما أن ما نهى عنه فهو معصية عندكم.
And whoever holds the second opinion—which is the opinion of al-Jubba'i—that God Almighty's will for the actions of His servants is something else, it is said to him: If aversion is necessitated by negation of will for the actions of His servants, then tell us: did God will the occurrence of actions that are not sins nor acts of obedience?

Chapter on the discussion of will and that it encompasses all originated things.

If he says: Yes.
It is said to him: Then it follows for you that it is an act of obedience; because the act of obedience, in your view, is only an act of obedience to the obeyed because He willed it.
If he says: He did not will it.
It is said to him: Then it follows for you that He is averse to its occurrence, and this necessitates that it be a sin; because what God—exalted is He—is averse to is a sin, just as what He forbids is a sin according to you.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a dialogue with the Mu'tazilite theologian al-Jubba'i. Al-Ash'ari first states al-Jubba'i's position: God's will for human actions is something other than the actions themselves (i.e., God does not directly will all human actions). Then he poses a dilemma: Consider actions that are neither sins nor acts of obedience (i.e., neutral actions). Did God will their occurrence or not? If al-Jubba'i says yes, then according to his own definition, an act of obedience is only such because God willed it; so this neutral action would become an act of obedience, which is contradictory since it is neutral. If he says no, then God is averse to its occurrence, and aversion in their view makes it a sin, so it becomes a sin. Either way, the neutral action cannot remain neutral; thus the Mu'tazilite position fails. The chapter heading introduces the discussion on will and its encompassing all originated things, which is the broader context.

Key Terms
إرادة
كراهة
طاعة
معصية
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Ash'ari is claiming that neutral actions actually are obedience or sin.
Correction: He is not asserting that; he is showing that the Mu'tazilite view forces them into one category, which is problematic.

Checkpoint
What are the two possible answers al-Jubba'i could give to the question about God's will for neutral actions, and what are the consequences?
A) Yes: it becomes forbidden; No: it becomes recommended.
B) Yes: it becomes obedience; No: it becomes sin.
C) Yes: it becomes sin; No: it becomes obedience.
D) Yes: it remains neutral; No: it becomes disliked.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's dilemma shows that the Mu'tazilite definition of will leaves no room for neutral actions; they must be either obedience or sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about your daily actions that you consider 'neutral' (neither good nor bad). How does this dilemma challenge your assumption that some actions are morally insignificant before God? Are you willing to see every action as falling under His will, either as obedience or sin?
?
In your life, do you sometimes try to keep actions 'neutral' to avoid responsibility? How does al-Ash'ari's argument expose the impossibility of such a stance? What would it mean for you to consciously will every action as obedience?
?
If you truly believed that every action that is not willed by God becomes an object of His aversion, how would that change the way you approach even the smallest choices today? Can you identify one 'neutral' habit you need to transform into an act of worship?

Eternal Aversion from Negation of Will
Al-Ash'ari argues that if negation of will implies aversion, then God was eternally averse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses a logical reductio to refute the Mu'tazilite principle that negation of will implies aversion, showing it leads to an absurd eternal attribute.

Core Idea
If negation of will means aversion, then God was eternally averse before creation.

Al-Ash'ari delivers a final blow to the Mu'tazilite claim by forcing them to consider the logical consequences of their own rule applied to God's eternal state. Before any objects of will existed, God was not willing anything; if that implies aversion, then God must have been eternally averse—an absurd and theologically unacceptable conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
The phrase 'بَنَّةٌ' means 'at all'.
2
Identify the premises
The argument uses two premises: (1) Negation of will implies aversion. (2) Before creation, God was eternally not willing anything. Locate these in the text.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
What conclusion follows from the premises? Why is it problematic for the Mu'tazilites? Consider that they might not accept an eternal attribute of aversion.
Al-Ash'ari is not asserting that God has aversion; he is showing the absurdity of their principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: إذا كان نفي الإرادة يوجب إثبات كراهة، فيلزمكم - إذا كان الله تعالى لم يزل غير مريد لشيء بَنَّةٌ - أن يكون لم يزل كارها؛ إذ كان نفي الإرادة يوجب إثبات كراهة.
And it is said to them: If negation of will necessitates affirmation of aversion, then it follows for you—if God Almighty was ever not willing anything at all—that He was ever averse; since negation of will necessitates affirmation of aversion.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents a final argument against the Mu'tazilite principle that negation of will implies aversion. Al-Ash'ari points out that before creation, God was eternally not willing anything (since there were no objects of will). If the Mu'tazilite principle holds, then God must have been eternally averse to everything. But this is problematic because it implies an eternal attribute of aversion, which they might not accept, or it contradicts the fact that God only becomes willing at the time of creation. The argument forces them to abandon the principle.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
كراهة (aversion)
أزل (eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari believes God has eternal aversion.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari is using a logical reductio ad absurdum to refute the Mu'tazilite premise; he does not affirm aversion for God.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ash'ari conclude from the Mu'tazilite principle that negation of will implies aversion, when applied to God's eternal state?
A) God was eternally willing.
B) God was eternally averse.
C) God had no attributes.
D) The principle is only valid for temporal things.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazilite principle by showing it forces an eternal attribute of aversion on God, which is theologically unacceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's eternal will and the fact that He was not willing anything before creation, do you sometimes fall into the trap of imagining God as passive or indifferent? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
The Mu'tazilites tried to rationalize divine attributes, but ended up with an absurd consequence. Have you ever found yourself over-rationalizing matters of faith to the point of losing sight of God's transcendence? What would it take for you to submit to divine mysteries instead?
?
Al-Ash'ari's argument exposes the incoherence of denying God's will for fear of anthropomorphism. Are there any aspects of God's attributes that you are uncomfortable affirming because they seem 'too human'? How can you trust that God's attributes are perfect and unique, even when they share names with human traits?

Denying God's Will Implies Hatred and Disobedience
Ash'ari argues that denying God's will for acts implies hatred and disobedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how denying God's will for any act leads to the absurd conclusion that God hates that act, and why this is a key argument in Ash'ari's theology.

Core Idea
Denying God's will for an act forces you to say God hates it.

This passage presents a classic Ash'ari argument against the Mu'tazila concerning the scope of God's will. The discussion uses a dialectical format where the opponent is forced into a contradiction: if God did not will an act, then He must dislike it, and anything God dislikes is disobedience. This argument aims to prove that God's will encompasses all created acts, even those we consider evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the dialectical exchange.
Notice the question-and-answer format.
2
Understand the dialectical structure
The argument presents two possible responses from the opponent—'Yes' or 'No'—and draws consequences for each.
3
Identify the key moves
The first move: if God wills, the act is obedience. The second: if God does not will, He dislikes, so the act is disobedience.
The author uses the opponent's own definition of obedience and disobedience.
4
Grasp the final implication
The argument extends to show that if God never willed anything, He would be eternally disliking—an absurd consequence.
This forces the opponent to affirm that God's will encompasses all acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ
فإن قال : نعم .
قيل له : فلزِمَك أن يكونَ طاعة؛ لأن الطاعة -عندك - إنما كانت طاعةٌ للمُطاعِ لأنَّه أرادَهَا .
فإن قال : لم يردها .
قيل له : فيلزمك أنه كاره لكونها، وهذا يُوجِبُ أن تكون معصية ؛
لأن ما كَرِهَهُ اللَّهُ -سبحانه - فهو معصية، كما أن ما نهى عنه فهو معصية عندكم.
ويقال لهم : إذا كان نفي الإرادة يوجب إثبات كراهة، فيلزمكم - إذا كان الله -تعالى- لم يزل غير مريد لشيءٍ بتةً، أن يكونَ لمْ يزل كارِها ؛ إذ كانَ نفي الإرادة يُوجِبُ إثباتَ كراهة.
Chapter on the discussion of will and that it encompasses all created things.
If he says: Yes.
It is said to him: You must then consider it obedience; because obedience, according to you, is only obedience to the one obeyed because He willed it.
If he says: He did not will it.
It is said to him: Then it necessarily follows that He dislikes its existence, and this obligates that it be disobedience; because what Allah, glorified be He, dislikes is disobedience, just as what He forbids is disobedience according to you.
And it is said to them: Since the negation of will obligates the affirmation of dislike, it follows necessarily for you—if Allah, exalted, never ceased to be non-willing of anything absolutely—that He never ceased to be disliking; since the negation of will obligates the affirmation of dislike.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the Mu'tazila regarding God's will (al-irada). The author begins by stating the chapter topic: that God's will encompasses all created things. He then presents a dialectical exchange. The opponent (Mu'tazili) is asked: if you say that God willed an act, then that act must be obedience, because obedience is defined as that which the obeyed wills. But if the opponent says God did not will it, then it follows that God dislikes (kariha) its existence, and whatever God dislikes is disobedience (ma'siya). The argument uses the Mu'tazili's own principle that what God dislikes is disobedience. Then the author extends the argument: if negation of will implies affirmation of dislike, then if God was eternally non-willing of anything, He would be eternally disliking—which is absurd. Thus, the Mu'tazili position leads to contradiction.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الكراهة
الطاعة
المعصية
نفي الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will only applies to good and obedient acts, not to sinful acts.
Correction: The passage argues that if God did not will a sinful act, then He must dislike it, making it disobedience. This implies that God's will encompasses all acts, even sinful ones, because denying will leads to hatred.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the Mu'tazili claim about God's will for an act lead to if they say God did not will it?
A) The act is obedience because it was created.
B) The act becomes disobedience because God dislikes it.
C) The act is neither obedience nor disobedience.
D) The act is permissible because God did not intend it.

Key Takeaway
Denying God's will for any act logically entails that God dislikes it, which contradicts the nature of God and makes the act disobedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed a good outcome in your life solely to your own effort, forgetting that God's will encompasses it? According to this passage, if you deny God's will for that success, you are implying God dislikes it. How does that challenge your gratitude? Will you now take a moment to sincerely acknowledge His will behind every blessing?
?
When you face a trial or sin, do you ever think 'God did not will this for me'? This passage shows that saying 'God did not will it' means 'God dislikes it.' Does your complaint about the trial actually accuse God of disliking you? How can you reconcile your difficulty with the belief that God's will covers all things, including tests that purify you?
?
The argument reveals that the Mu'tazila definition of obedience as 'what God wills' forces a contradiction. Look at your own understanding: do you sometimes define obedience as merely following rules, without acknowledging that God's will is behind your ability to obey? Will you now align your theology to affirm that every act of obedience and even every act of disobedience falls under God's will, and then humble yourself before His absolute sovereignty?

Refuting the Mu'tazili on Foolishness and Divine Will
Ash'ari refutes Mu'tazili claim by analogies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari uses analogy to refute the Mu'tazili claim that God would be foolish if He willed what does not occur.

Core Idea
God's will for what does not happen is like a wish, not foolishness.

The Mu'tazila argued that only a fool wills foolishness, and since God wills things that never happen, He would be a fool. Ash'ari counters with two analogies from human experience: wishing for the impossible and allowing evil while hating it. Both show that the Mu'tazili reasoning leads to absurdity, not a defect in God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazili claim
The Mu'tazila assert that only a fool wills foolishness (al-safah). Read the opening lines to see how Ash'ari presents their argument.
Foolishness here means irrational or morally blameworthy willing.
2
First analogy: wishing
Ash'ari compares willing the impossible to wishing. If a person wills what he knows cannot happen, we call him a wisher (mutamannin), not a fool. Apply this to God: He wills some things that never happen—so by Mu'tazili logic, God would be a 'wisher,' not a fool.
3
Second analogy: the negligent master
Ash'ari then imagines a master who sees his slaves fornicating, could stop them, dislikes the act, yet does nothing. That master would be called a fool. The Mu'tazila deny that God wills evil, so if they apply this analogy consistently, they would have to call God a fool—which they reject, showing their contradiction.
4
Conclusion: reductio ad absurdum
The Mu'tazila cannot consistently apply their own criterion to God without absurdity. Thus, their original claim about foolishness is false when applied to the divine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللمع في الرد على أَهلِ الزَّيْغِ والبدع
١١- مسألة من مَسائل الإرادة
هل مُريد السَّفَهِ؟ ويُقالُ للمعتزلة : لِمَ زعمتمْ أنَّه لا يُريدُ السَّفَهَ إِلَّا سَفِيهُ؟
فإن قالُوا : لأَنَّ مُريدَ السفهِ منَّا سفِيهٌ.
يقال لهم : فكَذَلِكَ مَنْ أرادَ مِنَّا ما يَعْلَمُ أنَّهُ لا يكونُ، أَوْ يَغْلِبُ عِنْدَهُ أَنَّهُ لَا يكونُ، فهو مُتَمَنٍ، فاقْضُوا بذلِكَ علَى اللَّهِ -تعالى-؛ إِذْ زَعَمْتُم أنَّه أَرادَ أنْ يكونَ ما عَلِمَ أَنَّه لا يكونُ.
ويُقالُ لهُمْ : وكذَلِك أيضًا : مَنْ خَلَّى بَينَ عَبِيدِهِ وَإِمَائِهِ يَزْنِي بَعضُهُم بِبَعْضٍ، وهُوَ يَرَاهُم، وهو لَا يُعْجِزُ عَنِ التَّفريقِ بينَهُم، مَعَ كَرَاهِتِهِ الزِّنا - على أصولكم - وقَدْ نَهاهُم قبلَ ذلِكَ عن الزِّنَا، فهو سَفِيه. فاقْضُوا بذَلِك علَى اللَّهِ -تعالى- وإِلَّا كُنتُم مُنَاقِضِين.
Al-Luma' in refutation of the people of deviation and innovation.
Issue 11 from the issues of will.
Is the one who wills foolishness? And it is said to the Mu'tazila: Why do you claim that none wills foolishness except a fool?
If they say: Because the one who wills foolishness among us is a fool.
It is said to them: Likewise, whoever among us wills what he knows will not be, or he is predominantly certain that it will not be, is a wisher (mutamannin). So apply that to Allah, exalted, since you claim that He willed that what He knew would not be come to be.
And it is said to them: And also, similarly, whoever leaves his male and female slaves to commit fornication with each other, while he sees them, and he is not powerless to separate them, despite his dislike of fornication—according to your principles—and he had forbidden them before that from fornication, he is a fool. So apply that to Allah, exalted; otherwise you are contradictory.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazili argument that only a fool wills foolishness. The author (Ash'ari) presents the Mu'tazili claim: they assert that willing foolishness (al-safah) is a characteristic of a fool. The author then draws an analogy: among humans, one who wills something he knows will not happen is called a wisher (mutamannin), not a fool. Since the Mu'tazila claim that God willed things that He knew would not happen (e.g., the faith of a disbeliever that He knew would not believe), then by their own analogy, God would be a wisher—but that is not blameworthy like foolishness. Then Ash'ari gives a second analogy: a master who leaves his slaves to fornicate while he can prevent them, and dislikes it, is considered a fool. Since the Mu'tazila deny that God wills evil, they would have to apply this foolishness to God if they are consistent. But if they refuse, they are contradictory. The argument uses reductio ad absurdum.

الكراهة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God willing something that never happens makes Him foolish.
Correction: God's will for what does not occur is analogous to a wish (tamanni), not foolishness, and is not blameworthy.

Checkpoint

A) A foolish action.
B) A wish (tamanni).
C) A command.
D) A dislike.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari uses analogy to show that God's will for what does not occur is not foolishness but akin to wishing, and the Mu'tazili argument collapses into contradiction.

This lesson teaches that God's will is above our categories of foolishness. What belief in your heart needs to change to trust God's will even when it differs from what you wanted?
?
Consider the analogy of the master who sees his slaves fornicating and does not intervene. How does this challenge your understanding of God's justice? Are you willing to accept that God's ways are beyond human judgment, even when they seem contradictory to your sense of right and wrong? What would it cost you to surrender that judgment?
?
This passage refutes the Mu'tazili by showing that applying human standards to God leads to contradiction. In your own life, where are you applying your human reasoning to God and demanding that He act according to your logic? What would it mean for you to stop and admit that His wisdom is infinitely greater?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Willing Foolishness
Imam Ashari refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God cannot will foolishness by two analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God cannot will foolishness by using analogies from human experience to expose their inconsistency.

Core Idea
God's will includes all things, even evil, without any imperfection.

The Mu'tazila argued that willing foolishness is a sign of foolishness, so God cannot will it. Imam al-Ash'ari counters this by pointing out that human analogies are inconsistent when applied to God. In this passage, he presents two analogies to expose the contradiction in the Mu'tazila position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to understand the argument.
Note the direct address 'يقال للمعتزلة' indicating a refutation style.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Find the initial claim: 'لا يريد السفه إلا سفيه' (none wills foolishness except a fool). Notice how Ash'ari quotes it.
The Mu'tazila base this on human experience.
3
Examine the first analogy
Ash'ari draws a parallel: a person who wills what he knows will not happen is called a wishful thinker (mutamannin). Since the Mu'tazila say God willed what would not happen, they should call God a wishful thinker too—which they reject.
This shows the Mu'tazila apply a label inconsistently.
4
Examine the second analogy
Ash'ari presents a master who sees his slaves fornicating, can prevent it but does not, and hates fornication—human judgment calls him foolish. The Mu'tazila's own principles (God permits evil) would force them to call God foolish if consistent.
The conclusion: otherwise they are contradicting themselves (مناقضين).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال للمعتزلة: لم زعمتم أنه لا يريد السفه إلا سفيه؟ فإن قالوا: لأن مريد السفه منا سفيه. يقال لهم: فكذلك من أراد منا ما يعلم أنه لا يكون، أو يغلب عنده أنه لا يكون، فهو متمن، فاقضوا بذلك على الله تعالى؛ إذ زعمتم أنه أراد أن يكون ما علم أنه لا يكون. ويقال لهم: وكذلك أيضًا: من خلى بين عبيده وإمائه يزني بعضهم ببعض، وهو يراهم، وهو لا يعجز عن التفريق بينهم، مع كراهته الزنا - على أصولكم - وقد نهاهم قبل ذلك عن الزنا، فهو سفيه. فاقضوا بذلك على الله تعالى وإلا كنتم مناقضين.
It is said to the Mu'tazila: Why do you claim that none wills foolishness except a foolish person? If they say: Because the one who wills foolishness among us is foolish. It is said to them: Likewise, whoever among us wills what he knows will not happen, or it is predominant in his mind that it will not happen, is a wishful thinker. So apply that to God Almighty, since you claimed that He willed what He knew would not happen. And it is said to them: Also similarly: Whoever leaves his male and female slaves such that some of them fornicate with others, while he sees them and is not incapable of separating them, despite his hatred of fornication—according to your principles—and he had forbidden them before that, is foolish. So apply that to God Almighty, otherwise you would be contradicting yourselves.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that God cannot will foolishness. The author first quotes the Mu'tazila argument: they claim that willing foolishness is a sign of foolishness, based on human experience. In response, the author makes two analogies. The first analogy: among humans, someone who wills what he knows will not happen is called a wishful thinker (mutamannin), not necessarily foolish. Since the Mu'tazila claim that God willed what He knew would not happen (i.e., events that do not occur), they should apply the same label of 'wishful thinker' to God, which they would not accept. The second analogy: a master who leaves his slaves to fornicate while he watches and is able to prevent them, despite hating fornication, is considered foolish in human judgment. Since the Mu'tazila claim God permits evil but does not will it, they should apply this judgment to God as well. The author warns that failing to apply these analogies consistently makes the Mu'tazila contradictory (munāqiḍīn).

Key Terms
مريد السفه
سفيه
متمن
مناقضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that willing foolishness necessarily makes one foolish, so God cannot will it.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that human standards are not universally applicable; the same human actions that are foolish in one context (e.g., wishful thinking, neglecting prevention) are not foolish when attributed to God because His wisdom and will are absolute.

Checkpoint
In the second analogy, what condition makes the master 'foolish' according to the Mu'tazila's own principles?
A) He forbids fornication but does not punish it.
B) He has the ability to prevent fornication but does not, despite hating it.
C) He lets his slaves marry each other without permission.
D) He is unaware of the fornication occurring.

Key Takeaway
God's will encompasses all things, including evil, without any imperfection, as human analogies fail to capture divine reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You often judge people's actions by what you consider 'reasonable' or 'good.' Are you willing to admit that God's will is not measured by your standards? What would it mean for your daily trust in Him to truly believe that He is never foolish—even when His commands or decrees seem difficult?
?
The Mu'tazila wanted to protect God's perfection by limiting His will. Have you, in your own heart, tried to 'protect' God by mentally rejecting some of His decrees? Will you now repent from that subtle arrogance and submit to the full reality of His will, whether you call it 'good' or not?

Answering the Objection That God Would Then Be Liar
Ashari responds to the objection that if God wills evil He could lie, showing inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ashari refutes the Mu'tazila objection about God lying by using a counter-analogy and the shahid-ghaib principle.

Core Idea
Counter-analogy and the shahid-ghaib principle defeat the Mu'tazila objection.

In this passage, Imam Ashari addresses a clever objection from the Mu'tazila. They argue that if God can will foolishness without being foolish, then He could also lie without being a liar. Ashari responds by turning their own reasoning against them and then invoking the consistent application of observed categories to the unseen. This refutation is a classic example of kalam dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Read the opening sentence of the passage to grasp the objection: 'If it were permissible for one who is not a fool to will foolishness, then it would be permissible for one who is not a liar to speak lies.'
2
Examine Ashari's counter-analogy
Ashari presents a parallel case: what about someone who wills what he knows will not happen (a wishful thinker) and permits fornication despite hating it? The Mu'tazila would have to accept that such a person is not a fool, yet their own logic would then allow a non-liar to lie.
This shows the inconsistency in the Mu'tazila's own position.
3
Apply the shahid-ghaib principle
Ashari concludes by arguing from the witnessed (human) realm to the unseen (God): just as among humans, willing foolishness makes one foolish and willing obedience makes one obedient, so the same should apply to God. This refutes the Mu'tazila's attempt to deny that God's willing entails attributes.
The principle of shahid-ghaib is a foundational method in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لو جاز أن يريد السفه من ليس بسفيه لجاز أن يقول الكذب من ليس بكاذب. يقال لهم: ما الفرق بينكم وبين من قال: ولو جاز أن يريد ما علم أنه لا يكون من ليس بمتمن، ويخلّي بين عبيده وإمائه، يزني بعضهم ببعض، مع كراهته الزنا - عندكم - وقدرته على المنع والحيلولة، من ليس بسفيه، لجاز أن يقول الكذب من ليس بكاذب، وهذا ما لا يجدون فيه فرقًا. ويقال لهم: كما أن مريد السفه منا سفيه، فكذلك مريد الطاعة منا مطيع، فاقضوا بذلك على الغائب.
If they say: If it were permissible for one who is not a fool to will foolishness, then it would be permissible for one who is not a liar to speak lies. It is said to them: What is the difference between you and one who says: If it were permissible for one who is not a wishful thinker to will what he knows will not happen, and to leave between his male and female slaves, with some of them fornicating with others, despite his hatred of fornication—according to you—and his ability to prevent and hinder, while he is not a fool, then it would be permissible for one who is not a liar to speak lies? And this is something they find no difference in. And it is said to them: Just as the one who wills foolishness among us is foolish, likewise the one who wills obedience among us is obedient, so apply that to the Unseen.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific objection from the Mu'tazila. They argue that if it were possible for a non-fool to will foolishness, then by analogy it would be possible for a non-liar to tell lies—an absurd consequence they think refutes the Ashari position. The author responds with two points. First, he offers a counter-analogy: the Mu'tazila themselves hold that God wills what He knows will not happen (according to their denial of His will over evil acts). If they follow their own reasoning, they would have to accept that God could also be a wishful thinker, and that a master who permits fornication without being a fool would also be a liar. The Mu'tazila cannot find a relevant difference (farq) between these cases. Second, the author uses the principle of shahid (the witnessed) to ghā'ib (the Unseen): just as among humans, willing foolishness makes one foolish, and willing obedience makes one obedient, so the same should be applied to God. That is, if God wills obedience, He is obedient (in a sense appropriate to Him), thus the analogy is consistent. This refutes the Mu'tazila attempt to impose human categories on God in a way that undermines His transcendence.

Key Terms
كاذب
متمن
غائب
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ashari is applying human moral categories to God directly, implying that God can be called 'foolish' or 'obedient' in the same sense as humans.
Correction: Ashari is using the Mu'tazila's own method of analogy (shahid-ghaib) to show that their objection is inconsistent; he is not claiming God has human attributes but that the same reasoning must apply universally.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila objection that Ashari refutes in this passage?
A) If God wills evil, then He must be evil.
B) If God wills foolishness, then He could also lie without being a liar.
C) If God is all-powerful, then He cannot will evil.
D) If God wills everything, then human freedom is negated.

Key Takeaway
Imam Ashari refutes the Mu'tazila objection by showing that their own analogy leads to absurdities if applied consistently, and that the principle of shahid-ghaib demands applying human categories to God only analogically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself questioning Allah's decree because it seems unjust by your own standards, are you applying analogies from human justice that you would never apply consistently to your own shortcomings? How will you train yourself to submit to His wisdom even when your logic protests?
?
The Mu'tazila were so confident in their reasoning that they accused Allah of being a liar. Have you ever been so intellectually proud that you assumed your understanding of good and evil was absolute, forgetting that Allah's knowledge surpasses yours? What step will you take today to humble your intellect before His revelation?
?
Ashari shows that consistency matters: if you judge Allah by one standard, you must apply the same standard to all. In your daily life, do you hold yourself to the same moral standards you expect from Allah? For example, do you demand forgiveness from Him while refusing to forgive others? What will you change today to be consistent?

Quranic Proof for the Universality of Divine Will
Imam Ashari cites Quranic verses to prove God's will encompasses all possible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how five Quranic verses demonstrate that God's will encompasses all possible human actions, including belief and disbelief.

Core Idea
God's will is universal; no human action occurs without His willing it.

Imam Ashari provides scriptural evidence for the doctrine that God's will covers everything possible. He cites five verses from the Quran that condition human actions upon God's prior will. Together they show that all things, including faith and disbelief, happen by God's decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its literal translation. Let the verses speak for themselves.
Notice the pattern: each verse uses conditional language with 'law' (if).
2
Identify each verse's implication
For each of the five verses, ask: what human action does it condition on God's will? Write it down.
Verses cover willing, believing, guidance, doing (disbelief), and fighting.
3
Synthesize the overall proof
Combine the implications: why do these verses together prove that God wills everything possible?
The author's conclusion is cut off, but the logic is clear: if God did not will something, it would not happen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يبين أن الله تعالى مريد لكل شيء يجوز أن يراد: قول الله تعالى: {وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله} فأخبر أنا لا نشاء إلا ما شاء أن نشاءه. وقال تعالى: {ولو شاء ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلهم جميعا}، وقال تعالى: {ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها}، وقال: {ولو شاء ربك ما فعلوه}، وقال: {ولو شاء الله ما اقتتلوا ولكن الله يفعل ما يريد} فأخبر أنه لو لم يرد.
And among what makes clear that God Almighty is willer of everything that it is possible to will is the saying of God Almighty: {And you do not will except that God wills} (76:30). So He informed that we do not will except what He wills for us to will. And He said Almighty: {And if your Lord had willed, all who are on the earth would have believed, all of them together} (10:99). And He said Almighty: {And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance} (32:13). And He said: {And if your Lord had willed, they would not have done it} (6:112). And He said: {And if God had willed, they would not have fought each other, but God does what He wills} (2:253). So He informed that if He did not will...
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage provides transmitted (naqli) evidence for the generality of God's will. The author cites five Quranic verses, each of which conditions human actions upon God's prior will. The first verse (76:30) explicitly states that human willing is subordinate to God's will: 'You do not will except that God wills.' The second (10:99) states that if God had willed universal faith, everyone would have believed. The third (32:13) is similar regarding guidance. The fourth (6:112) indicates that if God had willed, disbelievers would not have done what they do. The fifth (2:253) says that if God had willed, people would not have fought, but God does what He wills. The author then starts to draw the conclusion, but the text cuts off. The clear implication is that all human actions, including belief and disbelief, obedience and sin, occur only by God's will. The verses collectively demonstrate that God's will encompasses all possible things (kull shay' yajuz an yurād). This is a foundational proof in Sunni kalam for the doctrine of divine predestination (qadr).

Key Terms
مريد
مشيئة
هدى
نشاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These verses support fatalism by implying humans have no choice or responsibility.
Correction: The verses affirm that God's will is all-encompassing, but they do not negate human responsibility. In Sunni theology, God's will and human free will coexist without contradiction.

Checkpoint
Which verse does Ashari use to show that human will depends on God's will?
A) {ولو شاء ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلهم جميعا} (10:99)
B) {وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله} (76:30)
C) {ولو شاء الله ما اقتتلوا} (2:253)
D) {ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها} (32:13)

Key Takeaway
These five Quranic verses collectively prove that God's will encompasses all possible things, including all human actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every action you have ever done—every sin, every good deed—occurred only because God willed it to happen. Does that truth humble you or unsettle you? How will you respond to Him knowing that your very ability to repent depends on His will?
?
This lesson shows that even your desire to obey or disobey is subordinate to God's will. The next time you feel a temptation, will you remember that you cannot act without His permission? Will you turn to Him in that moment, asking Him to will good for you?
?
God could have willed universal faith, yet He did not. You are among those who believe—not by your own power, but by His will. How does this awareness change your gratitude for your faith? Will you thank Him specifically today for willing you to believe?

The Objection Against Divine Will and Its Refutation
Al-Ash'ari presents an objection and replies with a tu quoque argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite objection that God cannot will evil without being foolish, and al-Ash'ari's tu quoque response.

Core Idea
The objection against God's will fails because it can be mirrored against the objectors.

The opponents argued that if a wise being could will folly, then it would be permissible for a non-liar to lie. Al-Ash'ari counters by pointing out that their own position on God's knowledge and power leads to a similar inconsistency. This lesson presents the objection and al-Ash'ari's initial refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opponent's statement carefully. They are drawing a parallel between willing folly and speaking falsely.
2
Understand the tu quoque
Notice how al-Ash'ari turns the same logic against them by citing their views on God's foreknowledge and inaction.
This is a standard dialectical move in kalam.
3
Notice the conclusion
See that al-Ash'ari concludes they cannot find a difference between the two cases, thus the objection is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثاتِ

فإن قالُوا : لَوْ جازَ أنْ يريدَ السَّفَهَ مَن ليسَ بسَفيه لجاز أن يقول الكذِبَ مَنْ ليسَ بكاذِب.
يُقالُ لهُم : ما الفرقُ بينَكُم وبينَ مَنْ قالَ : ولَوْ جَازَ أنْ يُريدُ ما علِمَ أنَّه لا يَكُونُ مَنْ ليسَ بِمُتَمَنِّ ، ويُخلّي بين عَبِيدِه وإِمائِهِ ، يَزْنِي بعضُهم بِبَعْضٍ، مع كراهتِهِ الزِّنَا - عندكم - وقدرتِهِ عَلَى المَنْعِ والحَيْلولةِ، مَنْ ليسَ بسَفيه، لجازَ أن يقول الكذبَ مَنْ ليسَ بِكاذب، وهذا ما لا يَجِدُونَ فيه فَرْقًا .
ويقالُ لهمْ : كَما أَنَّ مُريدَ السَّفْهِ مِنَّا سَفيه، فكذلكَ مُرِيدُ الطاعةِ مِنَّا مُطِيع، فاقضُوا بذلِكَ علَى الغائِبِ . وممَّا يُبيِّنُ أنَّ الله - تعالى - مُريدٌ لِكُلِّ شيءٍ يَجوزُ أَنْ يُرَادَ : قولُ الله -تعالى- : ﴿وَمَا نَشَاءُونَ إِلَّا أَن يَشَاءَ اللهُ ﴾ فأخبر أَنَّا لا نَشاءُ إِلَّا مَا شَاءَ أَنْ نَشَاءَهُ. وقال - تعالى - : ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جَمِيعًا ﴾ ، وقال - تعالى -: ﴿وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَا نَيْنَا كُلَّ نَفْسٍ هُدَنهَا ، وقال : وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ مَا فَعَلُوهُ ﴾، وقال: ﴿وَلَوْ شَاءَ اللهُ : مَا أقْتَتَلُوا وَلَكِنَّ اللَّهَ يَفْعَلُ مَا يُرِيدُ﴾ فَأَخْبَرَ أَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يُرِدِ القتال لم يكُنْ، وأيضًا أنَّ ما أرادَ مِنْ ذلك فَقَدْ فَعَلَهُ .
Chapter on speech concerning the will (al-irada) and that it encompasses all originated things (al-muhdathat). For if they say: If it were permissible for one who is not foolish to will foolishness, it would be permissible for one who is not a liar to speak a lie. It is said to them: What is the difference between you and one who says: If it were permissible for one who is not a wishful thinker to will what he knows will not happen, and he leaves his male and female slaves to fornicate with one another, while he hates fornication—according to you—and has the power to prevent and hinder, then that one is not foolish, and it would be permissible for one who is not a liar to speak a lie? And this is something in which they find no difference. And it is said to them: Just as the one among us who wills foolishness is foolish, so too the one among us who wills obedience is obedient. So judge by that concerning the unseen (al-gha'ib). And among what clarifies that God—exalted—is the willer of everything that is permissible to be willed is the saying of God—exalted: {And you do not will unless God wills} [76:30], so He informed that we do not will except what He wills that we will. And He—exalted—said: {And if your Lord had willed, all who are on the earth would have believed entirely} [10:99]; and He—exalted—said: {And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance}; and He said: {And if your Lord had willed, they would not have done it} [6:112]; and He said: {And if God had willed, they would not have fought, but God does what He wills} [2:253], so He informed that if He had not willed the fighting, it would not have been, and also that what He willed of that, He indeed did it.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the doctrine that God's will (al-irada) encompasses all originated things (al-muhdathat). It begins by presenting an objection from opponents: if it were permissible for a non-foolish being to will folly, then it would be permissible for a non-liar to lie. Al-Ash'ari counters with a tu quoque argument: he asks what distinguishes this case from the opponents' own doctrine that God dislikes evil but does not prevent it, while still being omniscient and omnipotent. The opponents cannot find a valid distinction. He then invokes the principle of analogy from the witnessed (al-shahid) to the unseen (al-gha'ib): just as in human experience, willing folly makes one foolish, and willing obedience makes one obedient, so apply the same rule to God. Finally, he provides scriptural evidence: several Quranic verses (76:30, 10:99, 6:112, 2:253) are cited to show that human will is subordinate to God's will, and that if God did not will something, it would not occur. The conclusion is that God's will is universal—everything that happens does so because He wills it, and if He had not willed it, it would not have been. The passage thus refutes the Mu'tazilite notion that God only wills good and not evil.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المُحْدَثات
السَّفَه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills evil, He must be evil or foolish.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the Mu'tazilite objection leads to an absurdity when applied consistently, and that willing something does not entail the attribute of that thing in God's case.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the result of comparing the two cases?
A) The opponents find a clear difference.
B) The opponents find no difference.
C) The opponents win the argument.
D) Al-Ash'ari concedes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari refutes the objection against God's will by showing it can be mirrored against the objectors without distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you ever question why God does not prevent it? How does this lesson challenge your assumption that God's will must align with your understanding of wisdom?
?
The opponents in this lesson argued that a non-foolish being cannot will foolishness. Have you ever limited God's will by your own standards of what is reasonable? What would it mean for you to surrender that judgment tonight?
?
Al-Ash'ari exposes the inconsistency of the objectors. Are there areas in your life where you hold God to a standard you do not apply to yourself? Will you repent from that double standard before you sleep?

The Analogy from the Witnessed and Scriptural Proof for Universal Divine Will
Al-Ash'ari employs shahid-ghaib analogy and Quranic proofs for universal divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses the shahid-ghaib analogy and Quranic verses to prove that God's will encompasses all things.

Core Idea
Our experience of human will teaches that a willer shares the quality of what is willed, and scripture confirms God's will is universal.

Having refuted the objection, al-Ash'ari now provides positive evidence for the doctrine. He invokes the principle of analogy from the witnessed to the unseen, and then cites multiple Quranic verses that directly state that human will is subordinate to God's will. This lesson examines these proofs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the shahid-ghaib analogy
Notice how al-Ash'ari uses human examples: a person who wills foolishness becomes foolish, and one who wills obedience becomes obedient. He then says to judge the unseen (God) by this same rule.
This analogy is a foundational method in Sunni kalam.
2
Examine the scriptural proofs
Read each Quranic verse cited. They all show that human will is dependent on God's will, and that if God had not willed something, it would not occur.
Verses include 76:30, 10:99, 6:112, 2:253.
3
Draw the conclusion
Al-Ash'ari concludes that God wills everything that can be willed, and that whatever He wills, He does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something difficult happens in your life, do you truly believe that it occurred only because God willed it? Or do you secretly think it was a mistake or unfair? How would your response change if you fully internalized that God wills everything?
?
If you are striving to obey God but struggle, do you recognize that your very desire to obey is only possible because God willed that for you? How does that humble you right now?
?
Consider an area where you feel entitled—like health or wealth. Do you realize that if God had not willed it, you would have none of it? Write down one thing you take for granted and thank God for willing it for you, then commit to living with that gratitude today.
