Refuting Interpretations of Divine Will Verses
Al-Ash'ari refutes interpretations limiting God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's refutation of interpretations that limit God's will to specific modalities, and why he insists on the apparent meaning of Quranic verses about divine will.

Core Idea
God's will is not restricted by human compulsion or capacity.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari encounters two objections that attempt to restrict the meaning of divine will verses. He responds by insisting on the literal reading (zahir) and then challenges the assumption that compulsion would override human ability. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the two objections raised and Al-Ash'ari's responses.
Notice the structure: objection, then response, then another objection, then response.
2
Analyze the first objection and response
The opponents claim that 'law shaa' means God willed to prevent fighting. Ask yourself: Why does Al-Ash'ari insist on the apparent meaning?
He argues that the verse should be taken as it is, without restricting it to a specific modality.
3
Examine the second objection about compulsion
The opponents argue that if God had willed to compel faith, people would be believers. What assumption are they making?
They assume compulsion would necessarily produce faith.
4
Understand Al-Ash'ari's counterargument
Al-Ash'ari points out that even under compulsion, people remain capable of disbelief. So compulsion cannot necessitate faith. How does this refute the objection?
This preserves both God's absolute will and human accountability.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why insisting on the apparent meaning and rejecting compulsion is important for the Ash'ari doctrine of divine will and human action.
This lesson shows the balance between divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالُوا : معنى هَذا : ولَوْ شَاءَ اللهُ مَا أَقْتَتَلُوا أَيْ: لَوْ شَاءَ أَنْ يَمْنَعَهُمْ مِنَ القِتالِ لَمْ يَكُنْ .
يُقالُ لَهُم : وَلم لَا حَمَلْتُم الآيةَ على ظاهِرِها وقلتم : على أي وجه شاءَ أن لا يكونَ القتالُ لَمْ يَكُنْ؟ وكذلك المطالبة عليهِمْ في قولِ اللَّهِ - تعالى -: ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جَمِيعًا ﴾ .
فإِنْ قالُوا : لَوْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ أنْ يُلْجِتَهُم إلى الإيمانِ لكانُوا مُؤْمِنِينَ .
قيلَ لهُم : أَوَلَيْسُوا مَعَ الإِلْجاءِ قادرينَ على أَنْ يَكْفُرُوا ، كَمَا قَادِرُونَ علَى أن يؤمنوا، فكيف يجب بالإلجاء كونُ الإيمانِ منهم، وهم قادِرونَ أَلَّا يكونَ مِنهُمْ إِيمَانُ مَعَ الإلجاءِ، كَما هُم قادرون على الإيمانِ مَعَ عَدَمِ الإِلْجاءِ؟
For if they say: The meaning of this is: 'And if God had willed, they would not have fought,' meaning: if He had willed to prevent them from fighting, it would not have happened. It is said to them: Why do you not take the verse according to its apparent meaning (zahiriha) and say: in whatever manner He willed that the fighting not be, it would not have happened? Likewise, the same demand is made upon them regarding the saying of God—exalted: {And if your Lord had willed, all who are on the earth would have believed entirely} [10:99]. Then if they say: If God had willed to compel them to faith, they would have been believers. It is said to them: Are they not, along with compulsion (ilja'), capable of disbelieving, just as they are capable of believing? So how is it necessary by compulsion that faith occur from them, while they are capable of there not being faith from them along with compulsion, just as they are capable of faith without compulsion?
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents two objections from opponents concerning the interpretation of the Quranic verses on divine will, along with al-Ash'ari's responses. The first objection: opponents claim that the verse 'And if God had willed, they would not have fought' (2:253) means only that if God had willed to prevent them from fighting, it would not have happened—limiting the will to a specific modality. Al-Ash'ari responds by asking why they do not take the verse according to its apparent meaning (zahir), which states simply that if God willed the non-occurrence of fighting, it would not occur, regardless of the mode. The same applies to the verse about universal belief (10:99). The second objection: opponents argue that if God had willed to compel people to faith, then they would indeed be believers—implying that God's will is only effective when it involves compulsion. Al-Ash'ari refutes this by noting that even under compulsion, people remain capable (qadirun) of both faith and disbelief. Compulsion does not remove their ability, so it cannot necessitate faith. If they are capable of disbelief despite compulsion, then faith cannot be forced. Thus, the opponents' interpretation fails because it assumes compulsion destroys choice, while al-Ash'ari maintains that human capacity remains intact even under God's overarching will.

Key Terms
الآية
ظاهر
الإلجاء
الإيمان
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'law shaa' implies hypothetical compulsion and that God's will is only effective when it compels humans.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that 'law shaa' should be taken according to its apparent meaning (zahir) without restricting it to a specific modality, and that compulsion (ilja') does not remove human capacity, so it cannot necessitate faith.

Checkpoint
What is al-Ash'ari's main objection to the opponents' interpretation of 'law shaa'?
A) They should take the verse metaphorically
B) They should take the verse according to its apparent meaning
C) They should ignore the verse
D) They should reinterpret the verse to mean compulsion

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari insists on the apparent meaning of divine will verses and argues that compulsion does not eliminate human capacity, thus preserving both God's absolute will and human accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that nothing happens except by God's will, do you ever use that as an excuse to avoid taking responsibility for your own actions? How can you live this truth without becoming passive?
?
When you struggle with obedience, do you secretly wish God would just 'compel' you to do right? What does al-Ash'ari's argument about capacity say about that desire?
?
Are there areas of your life where you interpret God's will as a 'prevention' rather than a sovereign decree? How does taking the verse as zahir challenge you to submit without over-explaining?

Refuting Literal and Compulsion Interpretations of Divine Will
Al-Ash'ari refutes the interpretation of 'if God willed' as mere prevention and the idea that compulsion necessitates belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's refutation of the interpretation that divine will in certain Quranic verses means only prevention, and why he insists on taking the verses literally while also rejecting the notion that compulsion necessitates belief.

Core Idea
Compulsion does not guarantee belief; humans retain ability to disbelieve.

Al-Ash'ari engages with an objection about Quranic verses that say 'if God willed, they would not have fought'. Some argued that this means only prevention. He challenges them to take the verse literally. Then he addresses a second argument about compulsion and belief, showing that even under compulsion, humans are still able to disbelieve, so compulsion does not necessitate belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialectical passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the back-and-forth structure: the opponents present an interpretation, then al-Ash'ari responds.
The passage contains two objections from opponents and two rebuttals.
2
Identify the first objection
The opponents claim that 'if God willed' in the fighting verse means only that God willed to prevent them. Find this claim in the text.
They restrict the meaning of will to prevention.
3
Understand the first rebuttal
Al-Ash'ari asks why they do not take the verse literally: whatever way God willed the fighting not to occur, it would not occur. He insists on the apparent meaning without restriction.
This establishes that divine will is not limited to prevention.
4
Understand the second rebuttal
The opponents then argue that if God compelled belief, all would believe. Al-Ash'ari points out that even under compulsion, humans remain able to disbelieve, so compulsion does not guarantee belief.
This refutes the idea that compulsion removes human ability.

القتال لم يكُنْ، وأيضًا أنَّ ما أرادَ مِنْ ذلك فَقَدْ فَعَلَهُ . فإن قالُوا : معنى هَذا : ولَوْ شَاءَ اللهُ مَا أَقْتَتَلُوا أَيْ: لَوْ شَاءَ أَنْ يَمْنَعَهُمْ مِنَ القِتالِ لَمْ يَكُنْ . يُقالُ لَهُم : وَلم لَا حَمَلْتُم الآيةَ على ظاهِرِها وقلتم : على أي وجه شاءَ أن لا يكونَ القتالُ لَمْ يَكُنْ؟ وكذلك المطالبة عليهِمْ في قولِ اللَّهِ - تعالى -: ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جَمِيعًا [يونس : ٩٩] . فإِنْ قالُوا : لَوْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ أنْ يُلْجِتَهُم إلى الإيمانِ لكانُوا مُؤْمِنِينَ . قيلَ لهُم : أَوَلَيْسُوا مَعَ الإِلْجاءِ قادرينَ على أَنْ يَكْفُرُوا ، كَمَا قَادِرُونَ علَى أن يؤمنوا، فكيف يجب بالإلجاء كونُ الإيمانِ منهم، وهم قادِرونَ أَلَّا يكونَ مِنهُمْ إِيمَانُ مَعَ الإلجاءِ، كَما هُم قادرون على الإيمانِ مَعَ عَدَمِ الإِلْجاءِ؟
The fighting did not occur, and also that whatever He willed from that He has done. If they say: The meaning of this is: 'And if God had willed, they would not have fought' meaning: if He had willed to prevent them from fighting, it would not have been. It is said to them: Why do you not take the verse upon its apparent meaning and say: In whatever way He willed that the fighting not be, it would not have been? And likewise the demand upon them regarding the saying of God Most High: 'And if your Lord had willed, everyone on earth would have believed, all of them' [Yunus:99]. For if they say: If God had willed to compel them to faith, they would have been believers. It is said to them: Are they not, together with compulsion, able to disbelieve, just as they are able to believe? So how does faith from them become obligatory through compulsion, while they are able that faith from them not occur with compulsion, just as they are able to believe without compulsion?
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This is a dialectical passage in which al-Ash'ari rebuts an objection concerning the interpretation of verses about divine will. The opponents had argued that the phrase 'if God willed, they would not have fought' means merely that if God willed to prevent them, it would not happen—i.e., a restriction of will to prevention. Al-Ash'ari first points out that they should take the verse literally: whatever way He willed that fighting not occur, it would not occur, without qualification. He then presents a second objection from the verse about universal belief by compulsion. The opponents claim that if God compelled belief, all would believe. Al-Ash'ari's rebuttal is subtle: even under compulsion, humans retain the ability to disbelieve, so compulsion does not guarantee belief; if it did, it would contradict their ability. Thus, the literal interpretation of will without restriction is necessary.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الإلجاء
ظاهر الآية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'if God willed' in the Quran only means prevention, and compulsion eliminates human choice.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that the verses should be taken literally, and even under compulsion, humans remain able to disbelieve, so compulsion does not necessitate belief.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, why does compulsion not necessitate belief?
A) Because humans have no free will
B) Because humans remain able to disbelieve even under compulsion
C) Because God does not use compulsion
D) Because belief requires free choice

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari insists on the literal meaning of divine will verses and argues that compulsion does not guarantee belief because humans retain the ability to disbelieve.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ash'ari argues that even if God compelled you, you could still disbelieve. Think of a time you felt pressured to do good, yet you chose otherwise. Did you ever blame God for your failure? How does this passage challenge that excuse?
?
When you read verses like 'If your Lord had willed, all would believe', do you sometimes feel fatalistic, thinking your efforts don't matter? Al-Ash'ari insists on literal meaning. Does your belief in God's will affect your daily choices? Are you using divine will as an escape from responsibility?
?
The opponents thought that 'if God willed' only meant prevention. Are there any verses you habitually reinterpret to avoid their confronting truth? What would change if you took them literally?

Distinguishing God's Command from His Will
Al-Ash'ari refutes the claim that what God does not will implies no weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari insists that what God does not will implies weakness, while what He does not command does not.

Core Idea
Not commanding is not weakness, but not willing is.

Al-Ash'ari's opponents argue that if God does not command something, it happens without weakness—so why should not willing be different? In this passage, al-Ash'ari responds by distinguishing two categories: God's own actions and actions of others. He shows that the key factor is will, not command.

Guided Path
1
Set up the objection
Read the first sentence where the opponents draw an analogy between not commanding and not willing. Ask yourself what they are trying to prove.
2
Examine al-Ash'ari's initial response
He first points to God's own actions: they are not commanded but happen because He wills them. Notice the conditional: if they occurred without His will, that would be weakness.
3
Apply the distinction to others' actions
Read the part about 'others' (ghayrihi). Al-Ash'ari says not commanding others' actions does not entail weakness, but not willing them does. Why?
Because not willing means something escapes God's control.
4
Consider the exception
Al-Ash'ari adds that what God forbids (and does not command) He still wills its occurrence—so no weakness. See how this strengthens his argument.
5
Understand the chapter heading
The passage ends with a heading about will encompassing all originated things. Reflect on how this argument leads to that doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإِنْ قالُوا : ليسَ في كون ما لا يريده إيجابُ ضعف، كما ليس في كون ما لم يأمر به إيجابُ ضَعْفِ. قيلَ لهم : قد كانتْ أفعاله -عندكم- ولَمْ يأمر بها، ولا يلحقه ضعفٌ، ولو كانَتْ وهوَ لا يُريدُها لَحِقَهُ الضعفُ . فكذلك كونُ ما لم يأمر به مِن غيرِهِ لا يوجب له ضعفًا، وفي كون ما لم يُرِده من غيره [ما] يَدُلُّ على الضعف . وأيضًا فإن ما لم يأمر به و[ل] نهى عنه، أراد وقوعه، فلذلك لم يلحقه الضعف . بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعمُّ سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ
If they say: There is no implication of weakness in the existence of what He does not will, just as there is no implication of weakness in the existence of what He did not command. It is said to them: His actions—according to you—occurred and He did not command them, and no weakness befalls Him, but if they occurred while He did not will them, weakness would befall Him. Likewise, the existence of what He does not command from others does not entail weakness for Him, but in the existence of what He does not will from others, there is that which indicates weakness. Also, what He did not command but forbade, He willed its occurrence, and therefore weakness does not befall Him. Chapter on speech about will and that it encompasses all originated things.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer in which the opponents compare not willing to not commanding. They argue that if something happens which God did not command, that does not imply weakness; similarly, if something happens which God did not will, that should also not imply weakness. Al-Ash'ari responds by distinguishing the two cases. He points out that God's own actions (like creating the world) are not commanded by anyone, yet they occur without weakness because God wills them. But if something occurred without His will, that would imply weakness because it would mean something happens against His will. As for actions of others, not commanding them does not imply weakness, but not willing them does imply weakness, because it shows the creature is acting outside God's control. Al-Ash'ari adds that what God forbids but does not command, He still wills its occurrence (as in the case of evil), so no weakness arises. The passage ends with the chapter heading on will, indicating a new section.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الأمر
الضعف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that if God does not command something, it means He does not will it either, so both should have the same implication regarding weakness.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that command and will are distinct: God may not command something (e.g., evil) but still will its occurrence, and that does not imply weakness. Only what He does not will would imply weakness.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what is the difference between what God does not command and what He does not will?
A) No difference
B) Not commanding implies weakness, not willing does not
C) Not willing from others implies weakness, but not commanding does not
D) Both imply weakness

Key Takeaway
God's will covers all things; what He does not will cannot occur without implying weakness, whereas what He does not command can occur if He wills it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that everything that happens—including your own sins and hardships—occurs by God's will? Examine a recent difficulty: did you at any point assume it was outside His will, and how did that thought reduce your reliance on Him?
?
Al-Ash'ari teaches that God wills even what He forbids. When you encounter evil in the world, do you hold a hidden grievance against God, thinking He should not have willed it? How does this lesson call you to submit to His wisdom?
?
You often command yourself to do good, yet your actions sometimes follow your own will instead. How does the distinction between command and will in this passage expose the gap between what you intend and what you actually do, and what does that reveal about your need for Allah's help?

The Distinction Between Will and Command
Ash'ari refutes the claim that not willing does not imply weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari argues that the absence of divine will indicates weakness, while the absence of divine command does not.

Core Idea
Not willing shows weakness; not commanding does not.

In this short objection-and-answer, Ash'ari clarifies a subtle point about God's will and command. The objector claims that not willing something is like not commanding it—no weakness. But Ash'ari dismantles the analogy by showing that will is directly tied to the actual occurrence of actions, while command is not. Let's see how he does it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objector's claim
Read the first line of the Arabic where the objector says there is no weakness in not willing, just as there is no weakness in not commanding.
Notice the analogy being drawn between will and command.
2
Find Ash'ari's first response
Ash'ari points to God's own actions: they occur without God commanding them (since He doesn't command Himself), yet no weakness. But if they occurred without His will, weakness would follow. This breaks the analogy.
Focus on the distinction: command is about obligation, will is about actual occurrence.
3
Understand the second point
Ash'ari adds that even prohibited actions occur because God wills them (for a wise purpose), so no weakness arises from not commanding.
Here will encompasses both commanded and prohibited actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعمُّ سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ
فإِنْ قالُوا : ليسَ في كون ما لا يريده إيجابُ ضعف، كما ليس في كون ما لم يأمر به إيجابُ ضَعْفِ.
قيلَ لهم : قد كانتْ أفعاله -عندكم- ولَمْ يأمر بها، ولا يلحقه ضعفٌ، ولو كانت وهوَ لا يُريدُها لَحِقَهُ الضعفُ . فكذلك كونُ ما لم يأمر به مِن غيرِهِ لا يوجب له ضعفًا، وفي كون ما لم يُرِده من غيره [ما] يَدُلُّ على الضعف
وأيضًا فإن ما لم يأمر به و[ل] نهى عنه، أراد وقوعه، فلذلك لم يلحقه الضعف
Chapter on speech concerning the will and that it encompasses all created things. If they say: There is no implication of weakness in the fact that something He does not will [occurs], just as there is no implication of weakness in the fact that something He did not command [occurs]. It is said to them: His actions—according to you—have occurred and He did not command them, and no weakness attaches to Him. But if they occurred and He did not will them, weakness would attach to Him. Similarly, the fact that something He did not command occurs from someone other than Him does not necessitate weakness for Him, but the fact that something He did not will occurs from someone other than Him indicates weakness. Moreover, what He did not command and prohibited, He willed its occurrence, and therefore no weakness attaches to Him.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit within a larger discussion on the divine will. The objector argues that not willing something does not imply weakness, analogizing it to not commanding something. Ash'ari responds by first distinguishing between command and will: God's actions occur without His command (since He does not command Himself), yet no weakness attaches. But if those actions occurred without His will, weakness would follow. So the analogy is invalid. Then he adds a second point: even in the case of what He prohibits, He wills its occurrence (as a test or for other wisdom), so weakness is avoided. The key theological move is to show that will (irāda) is more essential to power than command (amr), and thus the absence of will would indicate incapacity.

Key Terms
إرادة
ضعف
أمر
نهى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that not commanding and not willing are equally harmless for God.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that not willing indicates weakness because will is essential for the actual occurrence of actions, whereas command is not.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari, why does not willing something indicate weakness while not commanding does not?
A) Because command is unrelated to power.
B) Because will is directly linked to the actual occurrence of actions, whereas command pertains to obligations.
C) Because God never fails to command what He wills.
D) Because weakness only appears when there is a lack of command.

Key Takeaway
Will is essential to power; command is not, so lacking will implies weakness, but lacking command does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God's will encompasses everything, including your sins. Do you truly accept that every sin you commit was willed by God? How does that change your view of His mercy and your own responsibility?
?
If God does not command something but wills it (like your mistakes), how does that affect your repentance? Do you repent out of fear of punishment or out of love for the One who willed it all?
?
The passage distinguishes will from command. In your life, do you confuse what God wills with what He commands? Are there things you assume God wants because they happen, but maybe He only permits? Examine your heart: where have you mistaken divine permission for divine approval?

The Seven Consequences of Impossible Attributes
Al-Ash'ari lists the seven consequences that render an attribute impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven specific consequences that, according to al-Ash'ari, make an attribute impossible for God.

Core Idea
An attribute is impossible for God only if it leads to temporality, likeness, or injustice.

Al-Ash'ari begins his argument for the possibility of seeing God by laying down a foundational principle: anything that is impossible to attribute to God is so because it would entail one of seven harmful consequences. This principle guides rational theological inquiry by setting clear boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the beginning of the answer
Read the passage from the start through the list of consequences. Notice how al-Ash'ari frames the discussion as a response to a hypothetical objector.
The objector questions the method of using analogy (qiyas).
2
Identify the seven consequences
Find the seven items in the list. Each one represents a distinct reason why an attribute might be impossible for God. Write them down.
They include temporality (hadath), a temporal meaning (hadath ma'na), likening (tashbih), similarity in genus (tajnis), alteration (qalb), injustice (tajwir/tazlim), and lying (takdhib).
3
Understand why each consequence is prohibited
For each consequence, think about why it would be problematic to attribute to God. For example, temporality would mean God is created; likening would make Him like creation.
4
Connect to the broader method
Reflect on how this principle shows that kalam uses rational deduction: impossibility is not arbitrary but follows from clear logical entailments.
This sets the stage for the application to vision in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الرؤية. إن قال قائل : لِمَ قلتُم : إِنَّ رؤيةَ اللَّهِ -تعالى- بِالْأَبصارِ جائِزَةٌ مِنْ إثباتها باب القياس؟ قيلَ لَهُ : قُلْنَا ذلكَ ؛ لأَنّ ما لا يجوزُ أنْ يُوصفَ بِهِ الباري -تعالى- ويَسْتحِيلُ عليْهِ : فإنَّما لا يجوزُ ؛ لأنَّ في تجويزِهِ إثباتَ حَدَثِهِ ، أو إثبات حدث معنّى فيه، أو تشبيهه، أو تجنيسه، أو قلبَهُ عَنْ حقيقتِهِ ، أَوْ تَجْوِيرَهُ وتظليمه، أو تكذيبه . وليسَ في جواز الرؤيةِ إثباتُ حدَثِهِ ؛ لأنَّ المرئي لم يكُن مرئيا لأَنَّه مُحدَث، ولو كانَ مرئيا لذلك لَلَزِمَهُمْ أَن يُرَى كلُّ محدَثٍ، وذلك باطل عندهم بالدليل العَقْلِيّ
Chapter on speech concerning vision. If a questioner says: Why did you say that seeing God, exalted, with the eyes is permissible from establishing it [by] the chapter of analogy? It is said to him: We said that because whatever it is not permissible to describe the Creator, exalted, with, and is impossible for Him – it is only not permissible because permitting it would prove His temporality, or prove a temporal meaning in Him, or likening Him, or making Him similar in genus, or altering Him from His true nature, or making Him unjust and wronging Him, or lying about Him. And there is not in the permissibility of vision proof of His temporality, because the seen is not seen because it is temporal. And if it were seen for that reason, it would be necessary for them that every temporal thing be seen, and that is false by rational proof.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure. The objector asks why al-Ash'ari and his school claim that God can be seen with the eyes, and he seems to challenge the method (possibly accusing them of using analogy). The answer explains a general principle: anything that is impossible to attribute to God is impossible only because it would lead to one of seven harmful consequences: (1) proving that He is temporally originated (hadath), (2) proving a temporal attribute in Him, (3) likening Him to creation (tashbih), (4) making Him similar in genus (tajnis) which implies composition, (5) altering His true nature, (6) attributing injustice or wronging (tajwir wa tazlim), or (7) lying about Him (takdhib). Then al-Ash'ari argues that permitting vision does not entail any of these, because a thing is seen not due to being temporal. If being temporal were the cause of visibility, then every temporal object – such as smells, sounds, etc. – would be visible, which is false. Therefore, the rational proof shows that vision of God is possible, since none of the exclusionary factors apply.

Key Terms
الحدث
التشبيه
التجنيس
قلب الحقيقة
التجوير
التظليم
التكذيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think impossibility of an attribute is determined solely by scripture or cultural norms, not rational analysis.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that rational impossibility must be grounded in specific logical entailments that conflict with God's nature.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, which of the following is NOT one of the consequences that would make an attribute impossible for God?
A) Proving His temporality
B) Likening Him to creation
C) Making Him visible to the eyes
D) Lying about Him

Key Takeaway
An attribute is rationally impossible for God only if it entails one of seven specific negative consequences that contradict His nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about God in your prayers or conversations, do you ever catch yourself describing Him in a way that could imply temporality or likeness to creation? Which of these seven traps do you need to be most vigilant against, and what concrete step will you take this week to purify your speech?
?
The last consequence is 'lying about God' (takdhib). How often do you state things about God without verifying them from revelation? Think of one belief you hold about God that you cannot directly attribute to the Quran or authentic Sunnah. Are you willing to abandon it if it leads to any of these consequences?
?
Al-Ash'ari presents this list as a rational safeguard for God's transcendence. Does your heart feel the weight of attributing injustice to God? Examine your own understanding of divine justice: have you ever secretly thought God was unfair in your life? Repent from that thought and affirm His perfect justice now.

Rational Proof for Seeing God
Al-Ash'ari argues that vision of God does not imply temporality and thus is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's rational argument that seeing God does not entail any of the seven prohibited consequences, thus it is permissible.

Core Idea
Seeing God is possible because it does not prove His temporality, and if it did, all temporal things would be visible—which is false.

In this lesson, we examine al-Ash'ari's application of the general principle to the specific case of vision. He argues that permitting vision does not lead to any of the seven consequences, especially temporality. His proof uses a reductio ad absurdum to show that the cause of visibility is not temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the application passage
Read the final part of the passage, starting from 'وليس في جواز الرؤية' to the end. Focus on the logical structure.
2
Identify the main claim
Identify the key claim: that vision does not prove God's temporality. Notice the reason given: the seen is not seen because it is temporal.
3
Examine the reductio argument
Al-Ash'ari then argues by contradiction: if being temporal were the cause of visibility, then every temporal thing would have to be visible. But we know this is false (e.g., sounds, smells are temporal but not visible). Therefore, temporality is not the cause.
This is a classic logical move: negating the antecedent by denying the consequent.
4
Conclude the rational proof
Since vision does not entail temporality or any of the other consequences, it is permissible for God. This is a rational proof (dalil 'aqli).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الرؤية. إن قال قائل : لِمَ قلتُم : إِنَّ رؤيةَ اللَّهِ -تعالى- بِالْأَبصارِ جائِزَةٌ مِنْ إثباتها باب القياس؟ قيلَ لَهُ : قُلْنَا ذلكَ ؛ لأَنّ ما لا يجوزُ أنْ يُوصفَ بِهِ الباري -تعالى- ويَسْتحِيلُ عليْهِ : فإنَّما لا يجوزُ ؛ لأنَّ في تجويزِهِ إثباتَ حَدَثِهِ ، أو إثبات حدث معنّى فيه، أو تشبيهه، أو تجنيسه، أو قلبَهُ عَنْ حقيقتِهِ ، أَوْ تَجْوِيرَهُ وتظليمه، أو تكذيبه . وليسَ في جواز الرؤيةِ إثباتُ حدَثِهِ ؛ لأنَّ المرئي لم يكُن مرئيا لأَنَّه مُحدَث، ولو كانَ مرئيا لذلك لَلَزِمَهُمْ أَن يُرَى كلُّ محدَثٍ، وذلك باطل عندهم بالدليل العَقْلِيّ

The objector asks why al-Ash'ari and his school claim that God can be seen with the eyes, and he seems to challenge the method (possibly accusing them of using analogy). The answer explains a general principle: anything that is impossible to attribute to God is impossible only because it would lead to one of seven harmful consequences: (1) proving that He is temporally originated (hadath), (2) proving a temporal attribute in Him, (3) likening Him to creation (tashbih), (4) making Him similar in genus (tajnis) which implies composition, (5) altering His true nature, (6) attributing injustice or wronging (tajwir wa tazlim), or (7) lying about Him (takdhib). Then al-Ash'ari argues that permitting vision does not entail any of these, because a thing is seen not due to being temporal. If being temporal were the cause of visibility, then every temporal object – such as smells, sounds, etc. – would be visible, which is false. Therefore, the rational proof shows that vision of God is possible, since none of the exclusionary factors apply.

Key Terms

الحدث

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that since God is not a body, He cannot be seen.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that visibility is not conditioned on being a body; rather, it depends on the absence of the seven consequences. Since vision does not entail any of them, it is possible for God.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason al-Ash'ari gives that vision of God does not prove His temporality?
A) God is eternal and therefore cannot be seen.
B) The seen is not seen because it is temporal.
C) All temporal things are visible, which is absurd.
D) Vision requires direction, which God lacks.

Key Takeaway
Vision of God is rationally permissible because it does not entail any of the seven prohibited consequences, especially not temporality.

?

Are you willing to submit the assumptions you make about God to both reason and revelation, even when they challenge what seems obvious from creation? Where in your life have you imposed limitations on God that He did not place on Himself? Repent and ask for guidance.

The Rational Proof for the Permissibility of Seeing God
Ash'ari defends permissibility of seeing God by refuting six objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ash'ari's rational argument that seeing God is permissible by demonstrating that it does not entail any of the six logical impossibilities.

Core Idea
Seeing God is permissible because it avoids all six consequences that would make something impossible for Him.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari presents a robust rational defense of the Sunni belief in the beatific vision. A questioner challenges the proof by analogy, and Ash'ari responds by first stating a general principle: anything impossible for God is only so because it would entail one of six unacceptable consequences. He then systematically shows that the permissibility of vision involves none of these, thus establishing the rational basis for seeing God.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical structure: the questioner's challenge, the principle, and the refutations.
2
Understand the six consequences
Identify the six consequences that would make something impossible for God: proving His having a beginning, occurrence of a meaning in Him, likening, making Him a genus, turning Him from His reality, or declaring Him unjust/liar.
These are numbered in the commentary.
3
Follow the refutations
See how Ash'ari refutes each consequence for vision: (1) being seen is not due to origination, (2) vision does not cause a meaning in the seen, (3) vision does not create likeness, (4) vision does not entail injustice.
The argument uses everyday examples like seeing colors, dead bodies, and blind eyes.
4
Grasp the overall analogy
Understand that the proof is a disjunctive syllogism: if vision were impossible, it would be for one of six reasons; but none apply, so it is permissible.
This is a foundational kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الرؤية إن قال قائل : لِمَ قلتُم : إِنَّ رؤيةَ اللَّهِ -تعالى- بِالْأَبصارِ جائِزَةٌ من باب القياس؟ قيلَ لَهُ : قُلْنَا ذلكَ ؛ لأَنّ ما لا يجوزُ أنْ يُوصفَ بِهِ الباري -تعالى- ويَسْتحِيلُ عليْهِ : فإنَّما لا يجوزُ ؛ لأنَّ في تجويزِهِ إثباتَ حَدَثِهِ ، أو إثبات حدث فيه، أو تشبيهه، أو تجنيسه، أو قلبَهُ عَنْ حقيقتِهِ ، أَوْ تَجْوِيرَهُ وتظليمه، أو تكذيبه . وليسَ في جواز الرؤيةِ إثباتُ حَدَثِهِ ؛ لأنَّ المرئي لم يكُن مرئيا لأَنَّه مُحدَث، ولو كانَ مرئيا لذلك لَلَزِمَهُمْ أَن يُرَى كلُّ محدَثٍ، وذلك باطل عندهم. على أن المرئي لو كان مرئيا لحُدُوثِه لكان الرائي مُحدِثًا للمَرْئي ؛ إذ كانَ مرئيا لحُدوثِهِ. وليس في الرُّؤْيَةِ إثباتُ حُدوثِ معنى في المرئي ؛ لأنَّ الألوان مرئيات، ولا يجوزُ حُدوثُ معنى فيها، وكانَ ذلك المعنى هو الرؤية ، وهذا يُوجِبُ أنَّا إذا رأينا المَيْتَ فقد حَدثَتْ فيهِ الرؤيةُ ، وجامعتِ الرؤية الموْتَ . وإذا رأينا عينَ الأَعْمَى حدثَتْ في عَيْنِهِ رؤيةٌ ، فكانَتِ الرُّؤية مجامعةٌ للعَمَى، فلمَّا لم يَجُز ذلكَ بطَلَ ما قالُوهُ . وليس في إثباتِ الرؤيةِ للَّهِ - تعالى - تشبيه الباري -تعالى-، ولا تجنيسه ، ولا قَلْبُهُ عن حقيقتِهِ؛ لأنَّا نرى السَّوادَ والبياض، فلا يتجانسانِ ولا يشتبهان بوقوع الرؤية عليهما ، ولا ينْقَلِبُ السواد عن حقيقته إلى البياض بوقوع الرؤيةِ عَلَيْهِما، ولا البياضُ إلى السَّوادِ. وليس في الرؤيةِ تجويره ولا تظليمُهُ ولا تكذيبُهُ ؛ لأنَّا نَرَى الجائر والظالم والكاذب ، ونرَى مَنْ ليس بجائرٍ ولا ظالم ولا كاذب.
Chapter on vision. If a questioner says: Why do you say that seeing Allah Almighty with the eyes is permissible by way of analogy? It is said to him: We said that because what is not permissible to be described of Allah Almighty and is impossible for Him is only not permissible because in deeming it permissible there is proving His having a beginning, or proving the occurrence of a meaning in Him, or likening Him, or making Him belong to a genus, or turning Him from His reality, or declaring Him unjust or oppressive, or declaring Him a liar. And in deeming vision permissible there is no proving of His having a beginning because the seen is not seen because it is originated; if it were seen for that reason it would be necessary for them that every originated thing be seen, and that is false according to them. Moreover, if the seen were seen for its origination, then the seer would be making the seen originated, since it was seen for its origination. And in vision there is no proving of the occurrence of a meaning in the seen because colors are seen, and it is not permissible for a meaning to occur in them, and that meaning would be vision. This necessitates that if we see a dead person, vision occurs in him, and vision coexists with death. And if we see the eye of a blind person, vision occurs in his eye, and vision coexists with blindness. When that is not permissible, what they said is invalid. And in proving vision for Allah Almighty there is no likening of Allah Almighty, nor making Him belong to a genus, nor turning Him from His reality, because we see black and white, but they do not become of the same genus nor do they become alike by the occurrence of vision upon them, nor does black turn from its reality into white by the occurrence of vision upon them, nor white into black. And in vision there is no declaring Him unjust nor oppressive nor a liar, because we see the unjust, the oppressive, and the liar, and we see someone who is not unjust nor oppressive nor a liar.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument from the chapter on the vision of God. The questioner asks for the rational proof that it is permissible to see God with the eyes. The answer begins by stating a general principle: anything that cannot be attributed to God is only impossible because it would entail one of six unacceptable consequences: proving that God had a beginning (ḥadath), proving the occurrence of a temporal meaning in Him, likening Him to created things (tashbīh), making Him belong to a genus (tajnīs), turning Him from His reality, or attributing injustice or falsehood to Him. The author then argues that deeming vision permissible does not entail proving a beginning for God, because the seen is not seen because it is originated; if it were, every originated thing would have to be seen, which is false. Moreover, if being seen were due to origination, the seer would be causing the seen to be originated, which is absurd. Next, vision does not prove the occurrence of a temporal meaning in the seen, because colors are seen and no meaning (like vision itself) occurs in them; if it did, then seeing a dead person would cause vision to occur in the dead person, coexisting with death, or seeing a blind person's eye would cause vision to occur in that eye, coexisting with blindness—both impossible. Then, vision does not entail likening God to creation, because seeing black and white does not make them alike; each retains its own reality. Finally, vision does not entail declaring God unjust, oppressive, or a liar, because we can see both unjust and just people without the act of seeing attributing their qualities to God. Thus, since none of the prohibited consequences follow, the permissibility of seeing God is rationally established by analogy.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that vision requires a physical organ or implies change in the seen.
Correction: The text shows that vision does not entail any change in the seen, as demonstrated by seeing colors without altering them, and it does not require God to have a body or eye.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the permissibility of seeing God not a form of likening Him to creation?

B) Because God is not like any creature.
C) Because vision is not a physical process.
D) Because the questioner accepted the premise.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of seeing God is rationally proven because it falls under none of the six categories that would make something impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage argues that seeing God entails none of the impossible consequences. Do you live as if you truly expect to one day see Him? What in your daily life reflects that hope or its absence?
?
If the possibility of seeing God does not change His essence, how might that transform how you seek Him? Are you content with knowing about Him, or do you yearn for the vision itself?
?

Vision Does Not Imply a Temporal Accident in the Seen
Al-Ash'ari refutes the argument that vision implies a temporal accident in the seen object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari argues that the act of seeing does not cause a temporal accident in the object seen.

Core Idea
Vision does not make the seen object temporal; absurd consequences prove it.

Al-Ash'ari here tackles a specific objection: if Allah sees, does that mean the seen object becomes temporal? He refutes the idea by showing that vision does not generate any temporal attribute in the seen. He uses concrete examples to demonstrate the logical impossibility of the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Original Arabic
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the logical flow from claim to refutation.
2
Identify the Opponent's Claim
The opponent claims that the seen object is seen because of its temporality, implying the seer causes temporality in the seen. Find this in the first sentence.
3
Understand the First Refutation
Al-Ash'ari denies that vision establishes any temporal meaning in the seen, using colors as an example. Note that colors are seen but no temporal meaning like vision occurs in them.
4
Follow the Reductio ad Absurdum
If vision did cause a temporal meaning, absurdities follow: vision would occur in a dead body or a blind eye. See how al-Ash'ari shows these are impossible.
This logical consequence invalidates the original claim.
5
Conclude the Refutation
The conclusion is that the opponent's claim is false. Understand how the examples support the denial of any temporal meaning from vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن المرئي لو كان مرئيا لحُدوثِه لكان الرائي مُحدِثًا للمَرْني؛ إذ كانَ مرئيا الحُدوثِهِ. وليس في الرُّؤْيَةِ إثباتُ حدوثِ معنى في المرئي؛ لأنَّ الألوان مرئيات، ولا يجوزُ حُدوثُ معنى فيها. وكانَ ذلك المعنى هو الرؤية، وهذا يُوجِبُ أنَّا إذا رأينا المَيْتَ فقد حَدثَتْ فيهِ الرؤيةُ، وجامعتِ الرؤية الموْتَ. وإذا رأينا عينَ الأَعْمَى حدثَتْ في عَيْنِهِ رؤيةٌ، فكانَتِ الرُّؤية مجامعةٌ للعَمَى، فلمَّا لم يَجُز ذلكَ بطَلَ ما قالُوهُ.
That if the seen object were seen because of its temporality, then the seer would be an originator of the seen, since it was seen because of its temporality. And there is not in vision the establishment of a temporal meaning in the seen; because colors are seen things, and it is not permissible for a temporal meaning to occur in them. And that meaning would be vision, and this necessitates that if we see a dead [person], then vision has occurred in him, and vision coincides with death. And if we see the eye of a blind [person], a vision occurs in his eye, so vision coincides with blindness. So when that is not permissible, what they said is invalidated.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by al-Ash'ari of an argument that vision of an object would entail a temporal accident (huduth) in that object. The opponent claims that if something is seen because it is temporal, then the seer would cause temporality in the seen. Al-Ash'ari first denies that vision establishes any temporal meaning in the seen object. He gives examples: colors are seen, yet no temporal meaning (like vision itself) occurs in them. If vision did cause a temporal meaning, then seeing a dead person would make vision occur in the dead body, and seeing a blind eye would make vision occur in that eye—both absurd because death and blindness are incompatible with vision. Since such consequences are impossible, the original claim that vision implies temporality in the seen is false.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming vision for Allah implies that Allah becomes the locus of temporal events or that the seen object changes.
Correction: This passage shows that vision does not necessitate any change or temporal accident in the seen object. It is a pure relation.

Checkpoint

D) Because vision only occurs in living beings

Key Takeaway
Vision does not introduce any temporal accident in the object seen, as shown by the absurd consequences of that view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at a dead person or a blind eye, do you truly believe that your act of seeing changes nothing in them? If so, how does that reshape your understanding of Allah's seeing of you—does His seeing change you or merely witness you? Will you live today with the conviction that you are fully seen without altering His essence?
?
Al-Ash'ari argues that colors are seen without any temporal accident occurring in them. Consider your own sins: if Allah sees them, does that seeing create any change in Him? If not, then what is the nature of His displeasure—does it stem from His eternal will, not from a reaction to your actions? How does this affect your repentance?
?
The absurdity of vision coinciding with death or blindness invalidates the claim that vision implies temporality in the seen. Now reflect: If your vision of a dead relative does not make them alive or dead again, what does that say about the power of your perception versus the reality of things? Are you living as though your perception of 'truth' is what makes it true, or do you submit to a truth that exists independent of you?

Vision of Allah Does Not Imply Anthropomorphism
Al-Ash'ari proves that affirming vision for Allah does not entail anthropomorphism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming the vision of Allah does not entail anthropomorphism, based on al-Ash'ari's rational argument.

Core Idea
Vision does not change the nature of the seen; seeing Allah does not alter His transcendence.

Al-Ash'ari anticipates a common objection: if we say Allah can be seen, does that make Him like physical objects? He refutes this by showing that vision itself does not impose similarity or change on what is seen. This passage is a classic example of using rational analogy to defend divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
Notice that al-Ash'ari uses real-world examples to argue a theological point.
2
Identify the examples
Find the two sets of examples: black/white and just/unjust. Understand how each example shows that vision does not change the nature of the seen object.
3
Trace the conclusion
Notice how al-Ash'ari concludes that since vision does not introduce any impermissible attribute, it is permissible for Allah. Understand the logical structure.
The conclusion is that vision is ja'iz (permissible) for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس في إثباتِ الرؤيةِ للَّهِ - تعالى - تشبيه الباري -تعالى-، ولا تجنيسه، ولا قَلْبُهُ عن حقيقتِهِ؛ لأنَّا نرى السَّوادَ والبياض، فلا يتجانسانِ ولا يشتبهان بوقوع الرؤية عليهما، ولا ينْقَلِبُ السواد عن حقيقته إلى البياض بوقوعِ الرؤيةِ عَلَيْهِما، ولا البياضُ إلى السَّوادِ. وليس في الرؤيةِ تجويره ولا تظليمُهُ ولا تكذيبُهُ؛ لأنَّا نَرَى الجائر والظالم والكاذب، ونرَى مَنْ ليس بجائرٍ ولا ظالم ولا كاذب. فلما لم يكن في إثباتِ الرؤيةِ شيءٌ مما لا يجوز على الباري لم تكن الرؤية مستحيلة، وإذا لم تكن مستحيلة كانت جائزةً علَى اللَّهِ.
And there is not in affirming vision for Allah - exalted - any likening of the Creator - exalted - nor making Him of a genus, nor turning Him from His reality; because we see black and white, and they do not become of one genus nor do they resemble each other by vision occurring upon them, nor does black turn from its reality to white by the occurrence of vision upon them, nor white to black. And there is not in vision making Him unjust, nor making Him darkness, nor making Him a liar; because we see the unjust, the oppressor, and the liar, and we see one who is not unjust, nor oppressor, nor liar. So when there is not in affirming vision anything that is impermissible for the Eternal, vision is not impossible, and if it is not impossible, it is permissible upon Allah.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the possibility of seeing Allah without anthropomorphism (tashbih). Al-Ash'ari argues that affirming vision (ru'ya) for Allah does not entail likening Him to created beings, assigning Him a genus (tajnis), or changing His essence. He gives examples: we see black and white, yet vision does not make them similar or change their natures. Similarly, we see both just and unjust people, and vision does not transfer their attributes to the seer. Therefore, seeing Allah does not imply that Allah has any impermissible attribute. Since nothing in the concept of vision contradicts the divine nature, vision is not impossible for Allah; rather, it is permissible (ja'iz). This establishes the Ash'ari position that Allah can be seen in the afterlife without implying corporeality or change.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming vision of Allah necessarily implies that Allah is a body, has location, or changes.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that vision does not require similarity between seer and seen, nor does it change the nature of the seen. Thus vision of Allah is compatible with His transcendence.

Checkpoint

B) By noting that seeing black and white does not make them alike or change them
C) By quoting Quranic verses
D) By arguing that vision is an accident cannot inhere in God

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari establishes that affirming vision of Allah is permissible because vision does not entail anthropomorphism or change in the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to long for the vision of Allah in Jannah, but deep down, do you fear that seeing Him might diminish His majesty in your heart? How does al-Ash'ari's argument challenge that fear, and what must you do today to replace that fear with confident hope?

When you imagine seeing Allah, do you unconsciously picture a form or location? This lesson demands that you strip away every physical image. Will you do the hard work of purifying your expectation, or will you remain comfortable with a flawed imagination?
?
If vision does not transfer attributes or change the seen, then your own sinfulness does not taint your perception of Allah. How does this truth compel you to seek His vision with urgency, knowing that He remains absolutely transcendent even as He is seen?

Response to Objection: Touch, Taste, Smell vs. Vision
Al-Ash'ari answers the objection that touch, taste, and smell would also be permissible if vision is.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why early Ash'ari theologians argued that touch, taste, and smell are not attributable to Allah, while vision is permissible, based on the nature of sensory perception.

Core Idea
Vision requires no contact; other senses do — that is why they differ.

Al-Ash'ari addresses a common objection: if vision is permissible for Allah, why not taste, touch, or smell? The opponents argue that these senses also do not imply temporality. However, Al-Ash'ari presents two responses from his school, showing that touch, taste, and smell inherently involve physical contact, which would imply change in Allah, whereas vision does not.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by two distinct answers.
The passage uses 'قيل لهم' to introduce the response.
2
Understand the two responses
Identify the two separate responses given by Ash'ari scholars. The first argues that touch, taste, and smell involve physical contact, implying temporality. The second suggests that the objector might mean Allah creates perception directly.
The first response is based on the nature of each sense; the second is a reinterpretation of the objector's intent.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why vision is unique among the senses in the Ash'ari framework. Vision does not require contact (mumassa), while the other senses do. This distinction preserves Allah's transcendence.
This connects to the broader Ash'ari defense of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن عارَضُونا : بأن اللمس والذوق والشم ليس فيها إثباتُ الحَدثِ ولا حُدوثُ معنى في البارِي -تعالى-. قيلَ لَهُم : قد قال بعضُ أصحابنا : إنَّ اللَّمسَ ضربٌ مِن [المانعون] ضُروبِ المماسات، وكذلك الذوقُ : وهو اتصالُ اللّسانِ والشَّهَوَاتِ بالجسمِ الذي لهُ الطَّعْمُ، وإن الشَّمَّ : هو اتصال الخيشوم بالمشْمُومِ، الذي يكونُ عنده الإدراك له، وأنَّ المُتَمَاسَّيْنِ إِنما يَتَمَاسَّانِ بِحُدُوثِ فيهما، وإن في إثباتِ ذلك إثبات حدوث معنى في الباري. ومن أصحابنا مَن يقولُ : لا يخلو القائل أن يكون : أراد بذكره اللمس والذوق والشم، أن يُحدثَ اللَّهُ -تعالى- له إدراكًا في هذه.
For if they object to us by saying: touch, taste, and smell do not involve establishing temporality nor the occurrence of a meaning in the Creator - exalted. It is said to them: Some of our companions have said: Indeed touch is a type of the types of contacts, and likewise taste: it is the connection of the tongue and the taste buds with the body that has flavor, and indeed smell: it is the connection of the nose with the smelled object, at which the perception of it occurs, and that the two things in contact only touch each other by the occurrence of [contact] in them, and that in affirming that there is an affirmation of the occurrence of a meaning in the Creator. And among our companions is one who says: The speaker cannot be other than intending by his mention of touch, taste, and smell that Allah - exalted - should create for him a perception in these.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response. The objectors argue that if vision is permissible for Allah, then other senses like touch, taste, and smell should also be permissible, but these also do not imply temporality in God. Al-Ash'ari reports two answers from his fellow theologians. The first answer: touch, taste, and smell inherently involve physical contact (mumassa) between two bodies, which would entail temporality (huduth) in both, making them unsuitable for Allah. The second answer: the objector's mention of these senses actually intends that Allah creates a perception directly, not through contact; thus it does not prove the objection. The passage shows the Ash'ari distinction between vision (which does not require contact) and the other senses (which do), thereby defending the uniqueness of vision as a possible attribute for Allah without anthropomorphism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sensory perceptions are the same; if vision is permissible for Allah, then so are touch, taste, and smell.
Correction: Vision does not require physical contact, whereas touch, taste, and smell inherently involve contact between bodies, implying temporality. Therefore, they are not attributable to Allah.

Checkpoint
Why do some Ash'ari theologians reject applying touch, taste, and smell to Allah?
A) Because they involve physical contact between bodies
B) Because they are less noble than vision
C) Because the Quran only mentions vision
D) Because they are not mentioned in the hadith

Key Takeaway
Vision is uniquely attributable to Allah among the senses because it does not require physical contact, unlike touch, taste, and smell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a theological truth because it seemed analogous to something you already understood? How can you guard against this rational trap when studying Allah's attributes?
?
The second response shows that the objector may not even intend what you assume. When someone challenges a creedal point, do you instantly defend, or do you first seek to understand their actual meaning? Commit to asking clarifying questions before responding this week.
?
If you truly believe that vision of Allah is possible without contact, does that belief translate into longing for His sight? What one practical change can you make today to increase your desire for that ultimate vision?
